

2013 WL 5872142 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANDREI JOSE PICADO
File: AXXX XX0 226 - Orlando, FL
September 25, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eva Kozlowska, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent was ordered removed in absentia by an Immigration Judge on May 26, 2011. His first motion to reopen that order was denied by an Immigration Judge on October 13, 2011, and on January 26, 2012, the Board found that the appeal of that decision was untimely filed. The respondent then filed a second motion to reopen which was also denied by an Immigration Judge, and the Board dismissed the appeal of that decision on March 29, 2013. On June 25, 2013, the respondent submitted the instant motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. The record will be remanded.
 
An alien who seeks to reopen removal proceedings following the entry of an in absentia removal order must demonstrate that the alien’s failure to appear was due to “exceptional circumstances.” Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i). Ineffective assistance of counsel may be an exceptional circumstance only if the alien shows prejudice. Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir.2005). A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel may amount to exceptional circumstances “when an applicant’s failure to appear is due to his attorney’s errant instruction.” Montano Cisneros v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 514 F.3d 1224, 1226 (11th Cir. 2008) (concluding the respondent showed prejudice as his attorney told him he did not need to appear at hearing and that venue had been changed); see also Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472, 473-74 (BIA 1996) (a respondent showed prejudice where he was incorrectly informed by his attorney’s staff that there had been a continuance and he did not need to appear at the hearing).
 
The respondent contends that former counsel, Ms. Goldenberg, provided ineffective assistance by not telling him that his motion to change venue was denied or that he had to appear at his scheduled hearing, and as counsel did not appear at the hearing herself, or make alternative arrangements for his representation. The respondent further notes that counsel filed a facially defective motion to change venue, an untimely appeal before the Board, and an untimely motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge. While the record reflects that the noted submissions were filed pro se, Ms. Goldenberg has not responded to the allegations that she, in fact, was responsible for filing them.
 
The respondent has submitted evidence reflecting compliance with the procedural requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (Motion, Tabs C, I, J). Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269 (11th Cir. 2005). Moreover, as counsel’s errors prevented him from presenting his case, she caused him prejudicial harm. In light of this record, we find it appropriate to grant the respondent’s motion to reopen, vacate the prior decisions, and remand the record for further proceedings. Accordingly, the following orders shall be issued.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s decisions dated January 26, 2012, and March 29, 2013, and the Immigration Judge’s decisions dated May 26, 2011, November 14, 2011, and March 29, 2012, are hereby vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion, and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

2013 WL 5872092 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ADAN PRIETO-CORTEZ
File: AXXX XX4 565 - Los Angeles, CA
September 23, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joseph J. Huprich, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s March 15, 2012, decision denying his untimely motion to reopen based on his claim of alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent does not dispute that his motion to reopen was untimely filed (I.J. at 2). Rather, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in finding he did not meet his burden in establishing he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel when his former counsel conceded removability as charged, did not file a motion to withdraw the pleadings, and did not file an application for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a). In this regard, the Immigration Judge concluded the respondent did not establish the doctrine of equitable tolling applied in his case because he did not provide any information as to when he became aware of the alleged deficient representation to show due diligence in filing the motion and did not comply at all with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), to establish ineffective assistance of counsel (I.J. at 2-4). See Singh v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 1090 (9th Cir. 2007) (equitable tolling requirements); Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002) (requirements to succeed on ineffective assistance of counsel claim).
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge issued her decision denying the motion “prematurely” because current counsel had not obtained the transcript of the proceedings despite his request to the Immigration Court and that prior counsel’s ineffective assistance should have been evident to the Immigration Judge just from a review of the record. Notwithstanding the respondent’s contentions on appeal, we find no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen for the reasons stated in his decision (I.J. at 2-4). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii) (2013) (de novo review).
 
In support of his contention that the Immigration Judge acted prematurely in rendering her decision on the motion, the respondent relies on Dent v. Holder, 627 F.3d 365 (9th Cir. 2010), and argues that the Immigration Judge should have waited until after counsel listened to the tapes of the prior proceedings before making his decision. However, we point out the decision in Dent dealt with the statutory mandate that an alien in removal proceedings be given access to his entry documents and any other records and documents, not considered by the Attorney General to be confidential, pertaining to his admission or presence in the United States. See id. at 374. It does not pertain to counsel’s ability to listen to tapes prior to filing a motion to reopen. We are unpersuaded by the respondent’s reliance on Dent in contending that the Immigration Judge acted prematurely in rendering her decision because counsel had not listened to the tapes of the prior proceedings.
 
*2 In addition, while the respondent has on appeal submitted an affidavit in an attempt to show he has substantially complied with Lozada, he has not complied with any of the other Lozada requirements and, notably, has not presented evidence showing that he is not removable as charged and/or that he is prima facie eligible for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act, as alleged (I.J. at 3-4).1 In this regard we note that the respondent, through prior counsel, conceded his removability as charged based on an illegal entry in December 2005 (I.J. at 2). See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986). The respondent claims on appeal that he was lawfully admitted to the United States with a visitor’s visa, a copy of which he provided on appeal, with an expiration date in 2011 and, as a result, former counsel should not have conceded removability and should have filed a motion to withdraw the pleadings.
 
However, the respondent has not provided evidence showing that he was lawfully admitted and states in his affidavit he told prior counsel he was lawfully admitted, was advised by prior counsel to obtain the necessary evidence, and he never did so. Thus, it appears prior counsel had a sufficient factual basis upon which to make the concessions to the facts and charges in the Notice to Appear and a sufficient basis to believe the respondent is ineligible for adjustment of status due to his illegal entry. Cf. Santiago-Rodriguez v. Holder, 657 F.3d 820 (9th Cir. 2011) (finding counsel provided ineffective assistance to alien by admitting to factual allegation without any factual basis for doing so). Under these circumstances, we find no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen.
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent has not submitted an application for adjustment of status nor has he provided evidence of his alleged marriage to a United States citizen.

2013 WL 5872070 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUIS VIVAR
File: AXXX XX7 402 - New York, NY
September 17, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Perham Makabi, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent filed an untimely motion to reopen on July 19, 2013, after we issued a final removal order on June 9, 2011. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The motion to reopen, to which the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”), has not responded, will be denied.1
 
The motion contends that the respondent received ineffective assistance of counsel, in that counsel did not inform him that he might seek voluntary departure (Respondent’s Mot. at 5; section 240B(b) of the Act). However, there is no statement or affidavit from the respondent concerning the claims in the motion. Statements of counsel are not evidence. See, e.g., INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980).
 
As no affidavit from the respondent is presented, the motion does not show that he acted with diligence in filing the motion two years after our final removal order. See Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 131 (2d Cir. 2008) (“no matter how egregiously ineffective counsel’s assistance may have been, an alien will not be entitled to equitable tolling unless he can affirmatively demonstrate that he exercised reasonable due diligence in pursuing his claim”) (citation omitted); Wang v. Board of Immigration Appeals, 508 F.3d 710 (2d Cir. 2007)(diligence must be shown by respondent wishing to pursue an untimely motion). Moreover, the respondent does not comply with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Debeatham v. Holder, 602 F.3d 481, 484 (2d Cir. 2010). Most critically, the motion does not present “. . . an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement with former counsel concerning what action would be taken and what counsel did or did not represent in this regard.” Id. (citations omitted). The motion is, therefore, denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent was removed to Ecuador on July 19, 2013. The United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit held in Luna v. Holder, 637 F.3d 85 (2d Cir. 2011), that we must consider a statutory motion to reopen, even if the alien is no longer in the United States. We deny the respondent’s motion on the merits, and therefore do not reach the applicability of Luna to this case.

IN RE: SANG RAE KO
File: AXXX XX0 957 - Baltimore, MD
September 13, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Laurence F. Johnson, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Amy S. Paulick
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(B)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(B)(i)(I)] - Nonimmigrant - no valid passport
 
Lodged: Sec. 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)] - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
 
 
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status
 
The respondent timely appeals from the Immigration Judge’s January 26, 2012, decision denying the respondent’s application for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“the Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i). The respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in denying her application. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) asserts that the Immigration Judge’s decision is correct and should be affirmed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent is a native and citizen from South Korea who entered the United States on August 9, 2005. The respondent was placed in removal proceedings because DHS alleged that she was not in possession of a valid immigrant visa at the time she filed her adjustment of status application (I.J. at 2). The primary issue on appeal is summarized in the Form I-485 denial letter (DHS Group Exh. 1A) and overseas investigation (DHS Group Exh. 1B and 1C), which outline the discrepancies and concerns regarding the letter of employment the respondent submitted to support her application for adjustment of status (I.J. at 6-10).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that she would have been able to explain the discrepancies and errors if she had not received ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent, however, did not comply with the procedural requirements for filing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as enunciated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Barry v. Gonzales, 445 F.3d 741 (4th Cir. 2006) (finding no compliance with Lozada), cert. denied, 549 U.S. 1182 (2007). Accordingly, that issue is not properly before us.
 
The respondent additionally offers new evidence to explain or clarify issues highlighted in the overseas investigation report and relied upon by the Immigration Judge in reaching the adverse credibility finding. We have not considered the evidence the respondent submitted to the Board. The Board is an appellate body whose function is to review, not create, a record. See Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984). To the extent this evidence is new, the respondent has not filed a motion to remand, and we cannot consider new evidence presented for the first time on appeal. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv). Moreover, the respondent has not shown how this new evidence was not previously available and could not have been presented at her hearing before the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992). As previously discussed, the respondent’s ineffective assistance claim does not warrant a remand for further discussion of this evidence because she did not comply with the procedural requirements outlined in Matter of Lozada, supra. Consequently, we adjudicated the appeal based upon the evidence contained in the record when it was before the Immigration Judge.
 
*2 Based on the foregoing, we conclude that the Immigration Judge’s adverse credibility finding is not clearly erroneous (I.J. at 9-10). The Immigration Judge’s findings in that regard are supported by DHS’s overseas investigation, which was not rebutted by the respondent (DHS Group Exh. 1A-C). Moreover, the respondent has not presented any persuasive arguments on appeal which warrant reversing the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s adjustment of status and 212(i) waiver applications. Thus, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2013 WL 5295821 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LENNOX S REECE
File: AXXX XX7 338 - Miami, FL
September 11, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Samson Koyonda, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 17, 2012, denying his motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent removed in absentia for his failure to appear at the hearing on May 19, 2011. We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii). The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
In his motion to reopen, the respondent asserts that he failed to appear at his removal hearing due to exceptional circumstances. Although the respondent’s motion is untimely, he contends that the filing deadline should be equitably tolled because he was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of his former counsel. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion on the basis that equitable tolling is not permitted pursuant to controlling precedent of the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit (I.J. at 2). However, subsequent to the Immigration Judge’s decision, the Eleventh Circuit held that the 90-day filing deadline for a motion to reopen is subject to equitable tolling. Avila-Santoya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 188 F.3d 1273 (11th Cir. 2013) (abrogating Anin v. Reno, 188 F.3d 1273 (11th Cir. 2009)). In light of this intervening precedent, we find it appropriate to remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further consideration of the respondent’s claims regarding exceptional circumstances and ineffective assistance of counsel, and the entry of a new decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv) (limiting the Board’s fact-finding ability on appeal).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further consideration of the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
Elise Manuel
FOR THE BOARD
2013 WL 5295821 (BIA)
End of Document

2013 WL 5295839 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARCHIBALD CLAUDIUS RICHARD HALL
File: AXXX XX2 153 - New York, NY
September 9, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nicholas J. Mundy, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Marguerite C. Mills
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This respondent moves the Board to reconsider its June 28, 2013, decision denying his motion to reopen and rescind an order of removal entered in absentia on March 16, 2006. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); section 240(c)(6) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g). The motion will be denied.
 
In its prior order, the Board found that equitable tolling of the filing deadline imposed on motions to reopen and rescind was not warranted in this case because the respondent did not comply with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim before the Board, and he did not establish that he acted with due diligence in bringing his ineffective assistance of counsel claim before the Board.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel may qualify as an exceptional circumstance and serve to equitably toll the 180-day limitation period for filing a motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia order of removal. See Aris v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 595, 599 (2d Cir. 2008); Iavorski v. I.N.S., 232 F.3d 124, 134-35 (2d Cir. 2000). To prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, however, the alien must substantially comply with the procedures set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Yi Long Yang v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d 133, 142-43 (2d Cir. 2007); Twum v. I.N.S., 411 F.3d 54, 59 (2d Cir. 2005). Moreover, an alien seeking equitable tolling based on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim must show that he exercised due diligence during the entire period he seeks to toll. See Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 132 (2d Cir.2008); Jian Hua Wang v. B.I.A., 508 F.3d 710, 715 (2d Cir. 2007).
 
In the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind, he did not offer evidence that his former attorney had been informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. Further, the motion did not reflect whether a complaint had been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and it did not include an explanation for why a complaint had not been filed. Id. See also Garcia-Martinez v. Department of Homeland Security, 448 F.3d 511 (2d Cir. 2006). The respondent therefore did not substantially comply with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. Failure to comply with the Lozada requirements constitutes a forfeiture of the ineffective assistance claim. See Jian Yun Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 46 (2d Cir. 2005).
 
*2 Further, the respondent did not show that he exercised due diligence during the entire period he seeks to toll. The respondent stated that he never heard from his former attorney after he met with the attorney a few days after the March 16, 2006, court date. See Motion to Reopen and Rescind at 8. The respondent, however, did not explain what efforts he made to contact his former attorney or to ascertain the status of his case in the 7 years that elapsed between their last meeting and the filing of the motion to reopen and rescind on April 16, 2013. In sum, the Board finds no error of fact or law in our prior order to warrant reconsideration. See Matte of O-S-G-, supra.
 
With the instant motion, the respondent has submitted a supplement to his motion to reopen and rescind that is dated June 21, 2013. See Motion Exhibit E. In that document, the respondent emphasizes that his former attorney rendered ineffective assistance of counsel when he also failed to appear at the March 16, 2006, hearing. The respondent asserts that although he previously submitted the supplement to the Board, the Board did not consider it in its June 28, 2013, decision. See Motion to Reconsider at 4.
 
In rendering its prior order, the Board considered a supplement to the motion that was filed on May 17, 2013. The Board has no record of receiving the supplemental document, dated June 21, 2013, prior to the filing of the instant motion, and the respondent has offered no evidence of its mailing to or receipt by the Board prior to its June 28, 2013, order. Even if the Board had received the document, the argument therein would not have altered its decision because the respondent neither set forth an ineffective assistance of counsel complaint in substantial compliance with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, nor demonstrated that he acted with due diligence in bringing his ineffective assistance of counsel claim before the Board.
 
For the foregoing reasons, the respondent’s motion to reconsider will be denied. The respondent’s request for a stay of removal is also denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

2013 WL 4925052 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALFRED AJANGA AHUTA
File: AXXX XX9 217 - Haskell, TX
September 5, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Paul Hunker
Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On July 5, 2013, the respondent filed a motion to reopen proceedings in which the Board dismissed his appeal on January 23, 2013. The Board granted his associated request for a stay of removal on July 16, 2013. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely as it was filed more than 90 days after the Board’s decision. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). However, he contends that equitable tolling is appropriate as former counsel provided him with ineffective assistance. Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 496 (5th Cir. 2000); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). With his motion he has included an affidavit, and evidence that he informed former counsel of the allegations against him and filed a state bar complaint in this regard (Motion, Tabs J, K).
 
To demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel in an immigration proceeding, the respondent must demonstrate substantial prejudice resulting from counsel’s performance. Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383, 385 n. 2 (5th Cir. 2001). Proving prejudice requires that the respondent make a prima facie showing that, absent counsel’s deficient performance, he would have been entitled to the relief he sought. Miranda-Lores v. INS, 17 F.3d 84, 85 (5th Cir. 1994).
 
The respondent raises several allegations of error by former counsel, including: asserting that he misrepresented to the respondent the events at his initial hearing; whether he presented an unpublished Board decision to the Immigration Judge; and counsel’s actions with respect to challenging the moral turpitude charge and the Immigration Judge’s evaluation of the same. We initially note that an Immigration Judge and this Board are not bound by either unpublished Board decisions, or by the out-of-circuit cases cited in the respondent’s motion (Motion at 11, 28-29). Matter of Anselmo, 20 I&N Dec. 25, 31 (BIA 1989) (explaining that the Board historically follows a court’s precedent in cases arising in that circuit). Therefore, counsel’s alleged failure to ensure that an unpublished decision was presented before the Immigration Judge is not, without more, prejudicial. We also note that the decision cited by the respondent does not stand for the proposition that the respondent’s conviction is not a crime involving moral turpitude, but rather remands for the type of analysis conducted by the Board in in this case in evaluating the respondent’s appeal.
 
*2 In addition, the Board has already addressed in detail whether the Immigration Judge properly found the respondent removable as charged. In doing so, the Board acknowledged the manner in which the Immigration Judge conducted his analysis, and in turn evaluated the issue of the respondent’s removability in light of the proper analytical methodology. Thus, the respondent has not shown that he was substantially prejudiced as a result of former counsel’s alleged deficient performance.
 
As we find that the respondent has not demonstrated that his motion is subject to equitable tolling based on ineffective assistance of counsel, his motion is untimely and reopening is not warranted. Lara v. Trominski, supra. Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2013 WL 4925052 (BIA)

2013 WL 4925075 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOSES FLOMO
File: AXXX XX9 240 - Atlanta, GA
September 4, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Yaw Akuoko, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Abby L. Meyer
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Liberia, appeals from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated October 18, 2011, that denied his motion to reconsider the Immigration Judge’s prior July 27, 2011, decision that denied his motion to reopen a prior September 22, 2010, decision that granted the respondent’s application for pre-conclusion voluntary departure under section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(1), (b)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the respondent’s motion to reconsider. On appeal, the respondent has submitted additional evidence to establish that his former counsel’s representation was ineffective. See Respondent’s Brief at 3-5, Exhs. A & B. We construe this evidence and argument as a motion to remand the record. The appeal will be dismissed, and the motion will be denied.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge, with the following notations. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994). The respondent does not contest the Immigration Judge’s finding that his motion to reconsider was untimely because it was not filed within 30 days of the final administrative decision in this case (I.J. at 2; Resp. Br. at 2). See section 240(c)(6)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(c)(6)(B); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). Therefore, we consider the issue waived. See Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260, 261 n.1 (BIA 2007). It follows that the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider because it was time-barred. Id.
 
However, even assuming that the respondent’s motion was timely filed, we uphold the Immigration Judge’s alternative finding denying the respondent’s motion to reconsider on the merits of the claim (I.J. at 1-2). A motion to reconsider requests that the Immigration Judge reexamine his decision in light of the additional legal arguments, a change in the law, or an argument or aspect of the case that was overlooked. This motion, therefore, must specify the errors of fact or law in the Immigration Judge’s decision and be supported by any pertinent precedential authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). The respondent’s Motion to Reconsider does not identify any material error of fact or law in the Immigration Judge’s decision. He has not asserted that there has been a change of law or that an aspect of the case was overlooked. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, supra; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2). Rather, the respondent asserts that his August 30, 2011, Motion to Reconsider and his January 21, 2011, Motion to Reopen, were untimely due to errors in the fees attached to the motions and due to attorney error in ensuring that these motions were timely filed. See Motion to Reconsider, Preliminary Statement & Item 10). However, no contentions regarding ineffective assistance of counsel were explicitly raised before the Immigration Judge.1
 
*2 The only substantive argument raised in the Motion to Reconsider was that the September 2010 decision should be reopened to allow the DHS to exercise prosecutorial discretion. See Motion to Reconsider at Item 15-22. This argument is unavailing as the Immigration Judge and the Board do not have authority to exercise prosecutorial discretion, and the DHS, which has such authority, has consistently and affirmatively opposed the respondent’s attempts to reopen the September 22, 2010, decision. See DHS Opposition to Motion to Reopen (2/11/11); DHS Opposition to Motion to Reconsider (9/12/11); DHS Brief on Appeal (1/5/12).
 
The respondent also contends that the denial of the Motion to Reopen should be reconsidered on the basis that the respondent is eligible for Deferred Enforcement Departure (Temporary Protected Status) for natives and citizens of Liberia (Resp. Br. at 6). See Motion to Reconsider at Items 5-14. However, this issue does not address any error of fact or law in the Immigration Judge’s October 2011 decision denying the motion to reopen due to the motion being approximately 30 days late (I.J. (7/27/11) at 1).2 Thus, the Immigration Judge properly concluded that reconsideration was not warranted because the respondent did not establish that he made any error of fact or law (I.J. at 2).
 
Furthermore, the respondent also has not met his heavy burden to establish that remand of the record for further proceedings is warranted. A motion to remand must conform to the same standard as a motion to reopen when the respondent seeks to present new evidence, but will only be granted if the new evidence is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1); Matter of Ige, 20 I&N Dec. 880, 884 (BIA 1984). In the current case, the respondent has not established that the evidence attached to the brief on appeal could not have been presented before the Immigration Judge.
 
The respondent argues that the deadline for his Motion to Reconsider should be equitably tolled due to ineffective assistance of his prior counsel, Isaac O. Babalola, Esquire.3 However, he has not substantially complied with the requirements to establish an ineffective assistance claim. See Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1222-23 (11th Cir. 2003); Matter of Compean, Bangaly, J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). To establish ineffective assistance, the respondent is required to submit an affidavit attesting to the relevant facts, a statement that sets forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with former counsel, evidence that the former counsel was informed of the allegations and allowed the opportunity to respond (or a report of counsel’s failure or refusal to respond), and a complaint filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding such representation, and if not, why not. Id.
 
*3 The respondent has submitted an affidavit that outlines his agreement with his prior counsel on the scope of the representation that was to be provided and the nature of the alleged ineffective assistance. See Respondent’s Brief, Exh. A. He also submitted a five sentence letter to his former counsel informing him that the respondent intended to file an appeal of the Immigration Judge’s October 2011 decision on the basis of ineffective assistance as “my motion was not filed on time, and the Immigration Judge denied the motion.” See Respondent’s Brief, Exh. B.
 
The letter and affidavit provided by the respondent are insufficient to meet the requirements to establish ineffective assistance by his prior counsel. While the respondent informed his prior counsel that he was submitting an ineffective assistance claim with the Board, he did not inform the attorney of any detailed allegations associated with this claim or invite the attorney to submit a rebuttal. The respondent stated in his affidavit that he was not filing any type of disciplinary complaint against his prior attorney as this attorney explained that the late filings of the motion to reopen and reconsider where due to scheduling errors. We are at a lost to understand how this explanation would justify the respondent’s failure to pursue redress with a disciplinary authority for an admission of ethical violations, which apparently occurred with both the motion to reopen and reconsider, when such behavior if continued in the future could adversely affect other unsuspecting clients. Further, without a formal complaint to the appropriate disciplinary authority, the respondent has not presented evidence sufficient to ensure that the substance of the accusation can be properly evaluated and demonstrate that the respondent’s allegations are not meritless or baseless. See Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra; Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639.
 
Based on the foregoing, the respondent has not met his heavy burden to demonstrate that if the record were remanded for further proceedings it is likely that the outcome of his case would be changed. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD

See Motion to Reconsider at Item 5-6. However, the respondent has not presented any evidence that he has complied with the requirements to establish ineffective assistance by his former attorney.

2

2013 WL 4925084 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUIS GARCIA-VARGAS
File: AXXX XX2 106 - Florence, AZ
September 3, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Judith Seeds Miller, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Lodged Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)] - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 13, 2013. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s request for a further continuance to seek post-conviction relief. The respondent has also filed new documentation on appeal in the nature of a motion to remand. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed and his motion denied.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, and review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues on appeal de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
In his brief on appeal, the respondent argues that his case requires a remand for post-conviction relief because, due to ineffective assistance of counsel, he did not understand the immigration consequences of his guilty plea (Respondent’s Br. at 2; Notice of Appeal at 2; Respondent’s Exhs. A-E). The United States Supreme Court has held that a criminal lawyer representing a non-citizen in connection with a guilty plea has a constitutional duty to advise the defendant that conviction may pose a risk of deportation. Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S. Ct. 1473 (2010). However, a potential collateral attack on a conviction does not impair the validity of an alien’s conviction for immigration purposes unless and until the conviction is overturned. E.g., Montilla v. INS, 926 F.2d 162, 164 (2d Cir. 1991); Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996).
 
After continuing the respondent’s case several times for him to seek post-conviction relief for his aggravated felony theft offense, the Immigration Judge ultimately concluded that the delay was becoming unreasonable particularly in light of the fact that the respondent did not appear to be pursuing this relief in a prompt and diligent fashion (I.J. at 2; Tr. 1-19).
 
The respondent has submitted new documentation on appeal including interim orders and other correspondence for his pending habeas corpus petition (Respondent’s Exhs. A-E). Cf. Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv). However, the record reflects that his initial request for an immediate ruling on his petition has been denied, additional evidence is being sought, and that the proceeding remains unresolved (Respondent’s Exhs. at A, C and D). Although the respondent’s petition for habeas corpus appears to be moving through the state’s court system, we have received no indication that the respondent’s conviction has been vacated or materially modified in any way (Respondent’s Exhs. A-E).
 
*2 While a collateral criminal proceeding challenging his conviction is pending, the case remains in the same posture as the day the Immigration Judge determined that there was no good cause for further continuances in the respondent’s case. See Cabral v. Holder, 632 F.3d 886, 890 (5th Cir. 2011) (holding that the Board is not required to hold removal proceedings in abeyance pending motions for post-conviction relief in state court). As noted above, a collateral attack does not impair the validity of an alien’s conviction. E.g., Montilla v. INS, supra; Matter of Madrigal, supra. While there may be some truly exceptional cases in which an Immigration Judge might justifiably grant a continuance during the pendency of a criminal matter, the respondent does not have a due process right to such a continuance. See United States v. Gonzalez-Valerio, 342 F.3d 1051, 1053 (9th Cir. 2003). Under the circumstances, we have no basis for concluding that the respondent suffered any violation of his due process of law. As such, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not demonstrate good cause for a continuance in his case. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Accordingly, the following orders shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD

2013 WL 4924939 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SANDRA JARA-SALAZAR A.K.A. BLANCA SANDRA JARA SALAZAR
File: AXXX XX0 170 - New York, NY
August 28, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ismael Gonzalez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Ecuador, filed an appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision, dated August 9, 2012, that denied her motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) did not file a brief. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i)-(ii).
 
The record indicates the respondent was ordered removed in absentia on January 16, 2002. On June 1, 2012, the respondent’s attorney filed a motion with the Immigration Judge, requesting that her proceedings be reopened to allow her to adjust status based on a visa petition application filed by her husband, who recently naturalized as a citizen of the United States. She alleges the Motion to Reopen was filed as soon as she was aware of her removal order; however, she also stated that in 2007 she retained another attorney who advised her of the removal order (Respondent’s Br. at 2-3). The respondent argues lack of notice as her reason for not attending the immigration hearing.
 
The Immigration Judge determined, based on the respondent’s representation in the motion, that she did not notify the Court of her new address after venue was changed to New York, New York (I.J. at 3).1 As such, she did not establish that proper notice of the hearing was not provided using her last address. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4); see Bhanot v. Chertoff, 474 F.3d 71, 73 (2d Cir. 2007) (holding that when the government sent notice of petitioner’s changed hearing date to his most recent address, a presumption of receipt applied); see also Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27, 38 (BIA 1995).
 
The notice of hearing provisions are satisfied when notice is sent to the last address provided by the respondent. Bhanot v. Chertoff, supra. In this case, the respondent was personally served with the Notice to Appear containing the address warnings, and the Immigration Court mailed the hearing notice to the last address provided by the respondent to the DHS (I.J. at 2).
 
Additionally, the respondent became aware of her removal order in 2007 (I.J. at 2). The respondent did not comply with the procedural steps for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against the attorney she alleges to have retained at that time (I.J. at 3). 8 C.F.R. § 1003.25(b)(4)(ii); see Debeatham v. Holder, 602 F.3d 481, 484-85 (2d Cir. 2010) (finding that where a motion to reopen is based on ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent must comply with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988)). In light of the foregoing, we agree with the Immigration Judge that she did not establish an ineffective assistance claim on which to base an equitable tolling claim to excuse the untimeliness of the motion. Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 132 (2d Cir. 2008). We agree with the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent’s motion to reopen was untimely filed. She has not demonstrated that she qualifies for any of the exceptions to the deadlines for filing motions to reopen under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4) or that the deadline should be equitably tolled.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent submitted additional evidence concerning her son’s educational and mental health issues. Although we are sympathetic, we do not consider evidence first submitted on appeal. Further, we do not find that this evidence, when considered with the prior motion, provides a sufficient basis to warrant invoking sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a) (providing the Board may reopen, sua sponte, cases where it has rendered a prior decision); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).
 
Although the respondent requests voluntary departure, the circumstances here do not warrant reopening to allow her an opportunity to pursue this relief 10 years after the entry of a final removal order (Respondent’s Br. at 5).
 
The respondent may pursue a request for prosecutorial discretion with the DHS, as neither the Immigration Judge, nor this Board, has authority to exercise prosecutorial discretion in this case. See generally Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348, 349-50 (BIA 1982).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD

We note the Notice to Appear was signed by the respondent and contains a warning concerning keeping the Immigration Court abreast of the respondent’s current mailing address and telephone number (Exh. 1).

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CAMILA DE SOUZA
File: AXXX XX7 384 - Miami, FL
August 19, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Troy D. Harris, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michele Drucker
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Remand; adjustment of status; voluntary departure
 
This is the second time this case is before us. The respondent is a native and citizen of Brazil. The case originally was before us pursuant to a Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) appeal of an April 5, 2011, decision granting the respondent’s adjustment of status application.1 On June 14, 2012, we sustained DHS’s appeal and concluded that the respondent’s former husband was no longer a derivative grandfathered alien and was not eligible to adjust his status in that manner. We remanded the record solely in order for the Immigration Judge to determine eligibility for voluntary departure. The Immigration Judge found the respondent eligible for voluntary departure and granted her that form of relief on August 14, 2012. The Immigration Judge denied respondent’s request for a continuance in order to await the adjudication of an I-130 visa petition filed on her behalf on August 1, 2012, by her second husband, whom she married in March 2012. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal and deny her motion to remand.
 
In her Notice of Appeal, the respondent contends that her former counsel mistakenly concluded a grant of voluntary departure would not have any detrimental effect in Consular processing. The respondent does not appeal the denial of her request for a continuance and we consider that issue abandoned. Matter of Cervantes, 22 I&N Dec. 560, 561 n.1 (BIA 1999). Instead, the respondent asks us to remand the record pending the adjudication of the I-130 visa petition filed on her behalf by her second husband. She includes several documents in support of the bona fides of her marriage.
 
It appears that the respondent is making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim with regard to her former counsel’s assertion below that a grant of voluntary departure would not affect Consular processing of her instant visa petition. An ineffective assistance of counsel claim must be supported by (1) an affidavit from the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel regarding the litigation matters the attorney was retained to address, (2) documentary evidence that the counsel in question was informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond, and (3) proof that a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to the alleged ineffective assistance (and if this is not filed, why it was not). Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). This respondent has not complied with any of the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, and we will deny her claim.
 
*2 The respondent also asks that we remand the proceedings so she can await the adjudication of her instant pending I-130 visa petition. The Board long has held that a motion to remand must meet the same standards as a motion to reopen. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471-72 (BIA 1992). Specifically, the Board may deny a motion to remand where a respondent has not established prima facie eligibility for the relief sought or where the evidence to be submitted in support of the motion was not previously unavailable or material to the relief requested. Id. at 472. Further, the party moving to remand bears a “heavy burden” of proving that the new evidence would change the result in the case if it were remanded. Id. Finally, the Board may deny such a motion where the ultimate relief sought is discretionary and we would not grant the relief as a matter of discretion. Id.
 
The respondent was married while in proceedings and must demonstrate by clear and convincing evidence that her marriage is bona fide. Sections 204(g) and 245(e)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1154(g) and 1255. The respondent does submit some documentation in support of her motion to remand. However, we conclude that the evidence is not sufficient to meet the heavy burden of prima facie eligibility in this instance. Further, the respondent does not have an approved visa petition and has not provided an indication of when the visa petition might be adjudicated. Thus, it is not evident that the outcome of the respondent’s case would change and we will deny the respondent’s motion to remand. Finally, the record does not contain evidence that the respondent paid her voluntary departure bond and we will not reinstate voluntary departure on appeal.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be issued:
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed and her motion to remand is denied.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent was in proceedings with her first husband, Diego Pacheco Miranda (AXXX XXX XXX), and would have adjusted status based on his application if we had not reversed the grant of relief from removal. The respondent’s first husband is not a part of this appeal.

APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The final order of removal in these proceedings was entered by the Board on October 21, 2011, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent filed an untimely motion to reopen his proceedings on May 29, 2013. See sections 240(c)(7)(A), (C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A), (C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent does not dispute the untimeliness of his motion, but requests that the Board exercise its sua sponte authority to reopen his proceedings in the interests of justice. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the motion. The motion will be granted.
 
The respondent has submitted evidence that a state court has vacated his conviction for attempted assault in the second degree in violation of New York Penal Law section 110-120.05-02 and allowed him to plead guilty to menacing in the third degree in violation of New York Penal Law section 120.15. See generally Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003); Matter of Chavez, 24 I&N Dec. 272 (BIA 2007). The record contains the New York state court’s order finding evidence of ineffective assistance of counsel related to the respondent’s guilty plea and ordering a Padilla hearing (Motion, Tab D). The record also contains the hearing transcript for the withdrawal and amended pleas (Motion, Tabs E, F).
 
Considering the entirety of circumstances presented, we will reopen the proceedings on our own motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). We do not express an opinion on the merits of the respondent’s entitlement to relief. On remand, the Immigration Judge may receive any additional evidence he deems appropriate to the full resolution of this matter, including additional or substituted charges of deportability, if any. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.30 and 1240.10(e). Accordingly, the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further consideration of the respondent’s deportability in light of the evidence of the vacated plea and amended conviction, and for further consideration of the respondent’s eligibility for relief from deportation.

2013 WL 5208919 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DELROY GREEN
File: AXXX XX8 277 - Baltimore, MD
August 15, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Loide Rosa Jorge, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Carrie E. Johnston
Senior Attorney
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica and a lawful permanent resident of the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 13, 2013. We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment arising in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo, whereas we review findings of fact in such appeals under a “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under our de novo review, we conclude that the respondent did not show good cause warranting a further continuance to allow him another opportunity to seek post-conviction relief with the hope of having criminal convictions vacated. See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. “[C]ollateral attacks upon the respondent’s conviction do not operate to negate the finality of his conviction unless and until the conviction is overturned.” Matter of Madrigal-Calvo, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). The respondent’s prior attempt to seek post-conviction relief did not succeed, and the future likelihood of the respondent’s success in having his convictions vacated was and is unknown.1
 
The respondent has asserted that prior counsel provided ineffective assistance in the effort to seek post-conviction relief. We do not find that remand is warranted. First, the respondent’s assertion of ineffective assistance in his criminal matters is not a basis for remand in immigration proceedings. Second, the respondent has not shown substantial compliance with the procedures set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In Matter of Lozada, we stated that a motion asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement between the respondent and his legal representative and what actions were to be taken on his behalf, (2) that the counsel whose reputation is being impugned be informed of the allegations against him or her and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) an indication of whether a complaint had been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not. Most importantly, there is no evidence that prior counsel has been informed of the allegations against him and been given an opportunity to respond. Counsel’s assertion that the respondent’s fiancée has made efforts in that regard is not evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980). Nor has the respondent provided evidence as to whether a complaint had been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent has not filed a motion to remand with evidence that his efforts to secure post-conviction relief have been successful.

2013 WL 5208932 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE HUGO AVELAR RIVAS
File: AXXX XX8 898 - Houston, TX
August 14, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eric Avazian, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Bradley A. Sherman
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of September 18, 2012. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia on September 26, 2011. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed an opposition to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent has not established that proceedings should be reopened. There is no issue regarding whether the respondent knew that he had a scheduled removal hearing. Thus, the respondent had notice of his hearing and can seek reopening of proceedings solely upon the basis of exceptional circumstances. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). In this case, the respondent claims that he failed to appear because he mistakenly relied on the advice of a paralegal, Ms. Basmajian, who told the respondent not to appear at his hearing.1 However, the respondent does not claim that Ms. Basmajian held herself out to be an attorney; nor does the respondent claim that he believed Ms. Basmajian was an attorney. We therefore find that the respondent has not established exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear. See Galvez-Vergara v. Gonzalez, 484 F.3d 798 (5th Cir. 2007)(finding that reasonable reliance on counsel’s erroneous advice can constitute “exceptional circumstances” requiring rescission of an order of removal).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues for equitable tolling, but the U.S. Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this case lies, has not issued a published opinion squarely adopting the doctrine of equitable tolling in the context of a motion to reopen immigration proceedings, and so the respondent’s citation to and reliance upon Second and Third Circuit cases is unavailing here.
 
Nevertheless, even if we assume without deciding that such tolling could be available to the respondent, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent failed to exercise due diligence in pursuing his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel by delaying nearly one year in raising the issue. See Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383, 385 n. 2 (5th Cir. 2001). Equitable tolling depends upon the particular facts and circumstances of each case, and the doctrine should be employed only in “rare and exceptional circumstances.” United States v. English, 400 F.3d 273, 275 (5th Cir. 2005) (involving criminal proceedings).
 
*2 Here, the respondent does not specify when he discovered the basis for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, but there is no indication he did not receive the September 26, 2011, in absentia order. At that time, he had reason to inquire, yet he apparently did nothing until he filed the August 2, 2012, motion to reopen. Consequently, the respondent has failed to establish due diligence in order to avail himself of equitable tolling.
 
We find that the respondent has not established an exceptional circumstance herein to warrant the sua sponte reopening of these proceedings. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations).
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We also observe that while the respondent alleges that Ms. Basmajian advised the respondent not to appear at his hearing, no such admission is included in Ms. Basmajian’s affidavit.

2013 WL 5208950 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NGANDIEU SANGO FLORENCE A.K.A. FLORENCE NGANDIEU SANGO
File: AXXX XX5 806 - Baltimore, MD
August 13, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Justin Egeolu, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 18, 2012. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which she was ordered removed in absentia. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent was afforded proper notice of her hearing by the Immigration Court, inasmuch as written notice of the hearing was provided to the respondent’s counsel of record. Notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a).
 
Furthermore, the respondent has not set forth a claim of deficient performance of counsel in accordance with the procedural requirements of our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). We disagree with the respondent’s appellate contention that she need not comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. Contrary to the respondent’s argument on appeal, the record does not clearly indicate that she received ineffective assistance of counsel. Moreover, even if the record had clearly indicated that she received ineffective assistance from her former counsel, it would not excuse the respondent from substantial compliance with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra. See Barry v. Gonzalez, 445 F.3d 741, 746-7 (4th Cir. 2006) (requiring “substantial compliance with the Lozada requirements). In support of the respondent’s contention that she need not comply with Matter of Lozada, supra, the respondent cites to Ray v. Gonzales, 439 F.3d 582 (9th Cir. 2006) and Morales Apolinar v. Mukasey, 514 F.3d 893 (9th Cir. 2008); however, we are not bound by these decisions, inasmuch as they were not issued by the United States Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit, the jurisdiction in which this matter arises. Although the respondent in this matter submitted a sworn statement setting forth her version of the facts in this matter, the respondent did not submit evidence that she informed former counsel of her allegations of ineffective assistance or file a disciplinary complaint with the appropriate authorities in accordance with Matter of Lozada, supra.1 In light of the foregoing, we find that the respondent has not met her burden of proof to demonstrate that she received ineffective assistance of counsel. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent has submitted evidence on appeal that a letter informing her former counsel of the allegations against him was drafted by her current counsel; however, the respondent has not submitted any evidence that the letter was actually mailed. Furthermore, this Board does not consider evidence first offered on appeal. See Matter of Soriano, 19 I&N Dec. 764 (BIA 1988).

2013 WL 4041253 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARCO TULIO RAMIREZ SAMUEL A.K.A. MARCO RAMIREZ
File: AXXX XX8 246 - Lancaster, CA
August 2, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Richard Mendez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jonathon Foerstel
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 On October 5, 2012, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit granted the government’s unopposed motion to remand the record to this Board. The case had previously been before us on November 14, 2011, at which time we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings. Upon remand, both parties have submitted supplemental briefing.
 
In our November 14, 2011, decision, we found that the respondent had not submitted sufficient evidence to support his claim that he was prejudiced by his prior attorney’s alleged ineffective assistance of counsel (Bd. Dec. at 1). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Specifically, we found that the while the respondent argued that his prior attorney erred in not pursuing adjustment of status, his motion did not contain an application for adjustment of status, nor did it contain other evidence that he is currently eligible for such relief (Bd. Dec. at 1). In its motion to remand before the Ninth Circuit, the government noted that the respondent, in his motion, also alleged that he suffered ineffective assistance of counsel because his attorney failed to pursue an application for withholding of removal from Guatemala. The government further noted that our previous analysis regarding prejudice was too restrictive, and that the respondent needed only to demonstrate that his previous counsel’s ineffective assistance may have affected the outcome of the proceedings. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003); Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 646 F.3d 855 (9th Cir. 2004).
 
Given the totality of the circumstances, we will vacate our November 14, 2011, decision and remand the record to the Immigration Court to further assess the respondent’s eligibility for relief from removal. The respondent has submitted evidence in substantial compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, and his motion was filed with due diligence. See Singh v. Ashcroft, 367 F.3d 1182, 1186 (9th Cir. 2004); see also Iturribarria v. INS, supra. Moreover, the respondent’s prior attorney indicated that the respondent wanted to apply for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture at his hearing on March 10, 2011 (Tr. at 7), but no applications were ever presented in court. While we might disagree that the respondent’s prior attorney had a duty to tell the respondent to marry his girlfriend, the fact remains that the respondent may now be eligible for adjustment of status in conjunction with a waiver of inadmissibility pursuant to section 212(h) of the Act. Given these circumstances, we find that the respondent’s prior attorney’s actions may have affected the outcome of his proceedings. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra. As such, we will remand the record to the Immigration Court for further proceedings regarding the respondent’s requests for relief from removal.
 
*2 ORDER: The Board’s November 14, 2011, decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion is granted, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

2013 WL 4041234 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: URIAH SMALL
File: AXXX XX5 011 - New York, NY
July 30, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Pearline Blackwood, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 9, 2012, denying his motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent removed in absentia for his failure to appear for the hearing on December 20, 2002. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in concluding that he did not exercise due diligence in seeking to reopen the proceedings. The respondent has submitted additional evidence on appeal, but has provided no persuasive explanation why such evidence was not previously available to him. The Board ordinarily will not consider any previously available evidence first proffered on appeal, as its review is limited to the record of proceedings before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995).
 
The record reflects that the Notice to Appear was issued on June 20, 2000, and served on the respondent by regular mail on that date. During the proceedings on October 4, 2002, the respondent was given oral and written notice of his next hearing scheduled for December 20, 2002, and he was advised of the consequences of a failure to appear. The respondent’s former attorney, James Elliot Howard, Esquire, filed a motion to continue on December 19, 2002. The respondent did not appear for the hearing on December 20, 2002, and the Immigration Judge issued an in absentia order of removal.
 
The Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent received notice of the hearing scheduled for December 20, 2002. Therefore, the respondent’s motion to reopen was filed untimely whether the respondent is seeking reopening under the rescission provisions pertaining to in absentia orders of removal, or the general motions to reopen provisions. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(4)(ii), 8 C.F.R. 1003.23(b)(1).
 
The respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the alien has satisfied the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
*2 The United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit has determined that the deadline for motions to reopen deportation proceedings may be equitably tolled to accommodate claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 130 (2d Cir. 2000). However, in order to equitably toll the filing deadline for a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must demonstrate that he has exercised due diligence during the entire period he seeks to toll. See Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 132 (2d Cir. 2008). This includes both the period of time before the ineffective assistance of counsel was or should have been discovered and the period from that point until the motion to reopen is filed. Id.; see also Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167 (2d Cir. 2006) (court concluded that aliens had demonstrated that their counsel was ineffective in informing them that they did not need to appear at a hearing, but they had not met their burden of showing why they had not been diligent over a two-year period).
 
The Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent has not demonstrated that he is entitled to equitable tolling, as he has not established that he acted with due diligence in this matter. The respondent states on appeal that Mr. Howard failed to properly file the correct documents in his divorce proceedings and that the divorce was ultimately denied, which is reflected in a document from the Supreme Court of the State of New York, dated March 14, 2003. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Exh. 10. The respondent indicates on appeal that he is unable to recall the exact year that Mr. Howard finally told him his immigration case was closed, but he did not know what being “closed” meant, and he contends that Mr. Howard did not advise the respondent that he had been ordered removed from the United States. However, the respondent has not met his burden of showing that he had been diligent during the entire period he seeks to toll. See Rashid v. Mukasey, supra. He indicates that as an uneducated taxi driver with a ninth-grade education, he could not have deduced the gravity of the fraudulent and unethical behavior of Mr. Howard. While the respondent explains that he did not know that he had been ordered removed in absentia in December 2002, and that he had contact with Mr. Howard in 2003, the respondent also states in his letter to Mr. Howard dated August 24, 2011, that Mr. Howard was not responsive to the respondent’s request for a copy of his files and he never heard from him again. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Exh. 15. The respondent has not stated that he contacted any other attorneys from 2003 until consulting with current counsel and filing a motion to reopen in June 2012. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not exercise due diligence as required for equitable tolling. See Rashid v. Mukasey, supra. Consequently, the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2013 WL 4041234 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FERNANDO GONZALEZ- SANTANA
File: AXXX XX8 353 - Las Vegas, NV
July 29, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paul C. Agu, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Christian Parke
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 17, 2012, denying his motion to reopen proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under a clearly erroneous standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent filed an untimely motion reopen arguing that the time limit for filing the motion should be equitably tolled due to the ineffective assistance of former counsel. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the motion is defective because it does not substantially comply with the procedural requirements outlined in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), or present a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (substantial fulfillment of the procedural requirements of Lozada, supra, is generally necessary to assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel unless the record reflects a clear and obvious case of such). Moreover, the respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by any of prior counsel’s actions or failure to act in this matter. See Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855 (9th Cir. 2004) (a claim of ineffective representation must show that the allegedly deficient performance of former counsel may have affected the outcome in the case). The respondent merely makes vague assertions that his communications with prior counsel were “quite deplorable,” that “nothing meaningful was accomplished” at his one meeting with prior counsel, and that he was not informed of all possible relief available to him in these proceedings. While citing to pertinent case law, the brief submitted by the respondent on appeal reflects a similar lack of detail concerning the attorney-client relationship, and is devoid of any substantive argument to support the claim that the performance of prior counsel was deficient.
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

Genet Getachew, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider the Board’s May 9, 2013, decision dismissing his appeal from the Immigration Judge denial of a motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia order of removal. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); section 240(c)(6) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). The respondent has not identified an error of fact or law in our prior order, but instead reasserts that his failure to appear at the January 17, 2012, hearing was the result of events which were out of his control. Specifically, he asserts that his attorney mailed the hearing notice to his obligor rather than to him. We considered these assertions in or prior order. We emphasized that notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent, and that the respondent did not set forth a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel to constitute exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985). As the respondent has not shown that our prior order contained an error of fact or law, the motion to reconsider is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
