2013 WL 4041214 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ZHE LIU A.K.A. LIU ZHE
File: AXXX XX7 062 - Saipan, MP
July 24, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mun Su Park, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Chandu Latey
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled (not sustained)
 
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)] - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document (sustained)
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of China, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 27, 2011. In that decision, the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent has not shown “good cause” for any further adjournment of the proceedings (I.J. at 3), and the Immigration Judge sustained the charge of inadmissibility under section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) (I.J. at 4-5).1 See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6 (2013). Following the filing of the Notice of Appeal, we established a briefing schedule according to which the respondent’s appeal brief was due on February 10, 2012. The respondent did not submit a brief before the deadline. Instead, the respondent filed a motion to remand on April 11, 2013, in which she contends that her prior counsel by whom she was represented below and who initially represented her in this appeal provided ineffective assistance.2 See generally Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Matter of Rivas, 26 I&N Dec. 130, 135 (BIA 2013) (“Motions to remand, like motions to reopen, are discretionary in nature, and the requirements for a motion to remand are essentially the same as for a motion to reopen.” (citing Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127, 138 (BIA 2009) and Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255, 256-57 (BIA 1985))). However, the respondent’s motion to remand will be denied, and the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent’s Notice of Appeal asserts that the respondent has been “““improperly charged” and argues without citation to evidence in the record of proceeding or the support of meaningful legal analysis that the respondent is not inadmissible under section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) of the Act. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.3(b). The Notice of Appeal reflects that the respondent intended to support her appeal with a subsequent appeal brief but, as already stated, that has not happened. Under these circumstances, we will dismiss the respondent’s appeal. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 820 (9th Cir. 2003) (“If the BIA was forced to decipher general statements of error, unsupported by specific factual or legal references, the BIA would have to spend time and resources reconstructing the proceedings before the IJ and building the petitioner’s legal case, in some instances only to conclude that the appeal was utterly without merit.” (citing Matter of Holguin, 13 I&N Dec 423, 424 (BIA 1969))); Matter of Keyte, 20 I&N Dec. 158, 159 (BIA 1990) (summarily dismissing an appeal where aliens alleged in Notice of Appeal that Immigration Judge’s decision was “unfair,” indicated intent to file a brief or written statement but did not and “neglected to specify whether the alleged error in the immigration judge’s decision lies with his interpretation of the facts or his application of legal standards.”).
 
*2 For the purpose of adjudicating the respondent’s motion to remand based on the claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, we will presume for the time being that the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements for such claims as delineated in Matter of Lozada, supra. See, e.g., Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934, 937 n. 4 (9th Cir. 2003) (“We seldom reject ineffective assistance of counsel claims solely on the basis of Lozada deficiencies.” (citing Melkonian v. Ashcroft, 320 F.3d 1061, 1072 (9th Cir. 2003) and Lata v. INS, 204 F.3d 1241, 1246 (9th Cir. 2000))). According to the respondent’s declaration submitted in support of the motion (“Declaration of Liu, Zhe”), her prior counsel acted ineffectively by failing to: (1) inform the respondent of the availability of withholding of removal or any other alternative relief from removal; (2) file a Petition for Alien Relative of which the respondent is the intended beneficiary with United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”); and (3) submit an appeal brief in support of the instant appeal. We will address each allegation in turn.
 
First, we accept that an attorney’s failure to advise his alien client of relief from removal for with the alien is eligible constitutes ineffective assistance. However, the respondent has not established that she is eligible for withholding of removal under section 241(b)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231(b)(3), or under the regulations implementing the Convention Against Torture. See 8 C.F.R. § 1208.16(c).
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is not a form of relief from removal. Rather, the ineffectiveness claim attempts to show that the respondent’s former counsel prejudiced her ability or opportunity to apply for withholding of removal. However, the prejudice only arises if the respondent can show a prima facie case for relief. In order to do this, the respondent must, at a minimum, claim that she faces a clear probability of prospective persecution or torture in China. Accordingly, she must submit an application for withholding of removal in support of the motion to remand, which “shall be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary material.” See section 240(c)(7)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(B); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The respondent has not supported the motion to reopen with any applications for relief and no evidence relevant to her (theoretical) claim for withholding of removal. Thus, the respondent has not established that she was prejudiced by her former counsel’s assistance in this regard.3 See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir. 2003) (“To show a deprivation of due process caused by ineffective assistance of counsel, the alien must show that counsel’s ineffective performance prejudiced her.”).
 
*3 Second, the former counsel’s alleged failure to file a Petition for Alien Relative before USCIS is beyond the scope of the instant removal proceedings. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 106-07 (BIA 2009). Furthermore, the respondent was not yet married to the United States citizen spouse during the proceedings before the Immigration Judge (Tr. at 24-25).
 
And finally, accepting that the failure to file an appeal brief can constitute ineffective assistance, the respondent has once again not shown prejudice arising from this failure. That is, the respondent, through present counsel, has not elaborated on the allegations of error in the Notice to Appear, or otherwise asserted any independent allegations of error in the Immigration Judge’s September 27, 2011, decision. Without a showing that the Immigration Judge’s decision contained reversible factual or legal error, the respondent has not established prejudice from former counsel’s failure to file a supporting appeal brief. Cf. Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, supra, at 826 (“All the king’s horses and all the king’s men and the very best counsel in the world could not induce a contrary decision on this record on appeal to the BIA.”).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is denied, and the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2013 WL 4041228 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALFREDO LUCAS DELEG
File: AXXX XX6 476 - New York, NY
July 22, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Dominick S. Cardinale, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Dara F. Reid
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; rescission
 
*1 On January 5, 2010, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent’s removal in absentia, and on February 23, 2011, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s February 11, 2010, decision denying his motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order. This case was last before the Board on August 30, 2011, when it denied the respondent’s untimely motion to reconsider its February 23, 2011, decision. On May 23, 2013, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
An alien may file only one motion to reopen and it must be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2); section 240(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §1229a(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i). An exception to the time and number restrictions exists for orders entered in absentia. See Maghradze v. Gonzales, 462 F.3d 150, 152 n. 1 (2d Cir. 2006); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3)(i), 1003.23(b)(4). The exception provides that an order of removal entered in absentia may be rescinded upon the filing of a motion to reopen within 180 days after the date of the order of removal, if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, or upon a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive proper notice of the hearing.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel may satisfy the exceptional circumstances requirement and may also serve to equitably toll the limitations period for filing a motion to reopen. See Twum v. I.N.S., 411 F.3d 54, 59 n. 4 (2d Cir. 2005); Iavorski v. I.N.S., 232 F.3d 124, 134-35 (2d Cir.2000); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (B.I.A.1988). However, “[f]or an untimely claim to receive the benefit of equitable tolling . . . an alien must demonstrate not only that the alien’s constitutional right to due process has been violated by the conduct of counsel, but that the alien has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll.” Iavorski v. I.N.S., supra, at 135; see also Cekic v. I.N.S., 435 F.3d 167, 170 (2d Cir. 2006) (noting that “an alien will not be entitled to equitable tolling unless he can affirmatively demonstrate that he exercised reasonable due diligence during the time period sought to be tolled”).
 
*2 The respondent alleges ineffective assistance of counsel on the part of his former attorney, Dominick Sorrentino, Esquire. He has not, however, shown that he has exercised due diligence in bringing this matter before the Board. See Cekic v. I.N.S., supra. The respondent claims that that “[c]ounsel failed to notify (him) of the date and time of . . . hearing” on January 5, 2010. Upon receipt of the Immigration Judge’s February 11, 2010, decision, however, the respondent was made aware that the Immigration Judge determined that he had received proper notice of the scheduled removal hearing, and that the illness which prevented Attorney Sorrentino from attending the hearing “did not relieve the respondent of attending the hearing in his own right.”1 See Immigration Judge’s February 11, 2010, Decision, at 2. Nevertheless, the respondent did not file the instant motion for more than 2 years after the Immigration Judge’s decision. Further, the respondent has not explained the delay in bringing this motion to reopen and rescind before the Board. Accordingly, equitable tolling of the motions filing period is not warranted in this case, and the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen and rescind is denied.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We also note that prior counsel filed a response to the respondent’s claims in which he states that at a master calendar hearing on March 24, 2009, he and the Immigration Judge told the respondent that next hearing date was January 5, 2010, and that he repeated this notice to the respondent several times thereafter. See Attorney Sorrentino’s Response at 2.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GUSTAVO MENA-FLORES
File: AXXX XX0 632 - Denver, CO
July 22, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mark R. Barr, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lillian Alves
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board’s decision in this matter entered on December 7, 2009, sustained an appeal of the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) and remanded the record to the Immigration Judge for further consideration. The Immigration Judge’s August 2, 2011, decision denied the respondent’s application for adjustment of status. The Board entered the final administrative decision when this matter was last before us on February 14, 2013; our order dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision.1 The respondent filed this motion to reopen his removal proceedings on May 15, 2013. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7). The DHS has filed a memorandum opposing the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The Board may consider a timely motion to reopen if it is supported by evidence of material new facts to be proven at a reopened hearing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The new evidence must have been previously unavailable and must be likely to lead to a different outcome in the respondent’s case. Id., see also Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472-73 (BIA 1988) (explaining that a party who seeks to reopen proceedings before the Immigration Judge, with all the attendant delays, bears a “heavy burden” of proving that the new evidence would likely change the result in the case).
 
The evidence that the respondent offers in support of this motion was available previously, but the motion claims that it was not presented to the Immigration Judge due to ineffective assistance of counsel on the part of Attorney Litman, who represented the respondent at the hearing. The Board held in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), that an alien’s due process rights may be violated when ineffective assistance of counsel prevents the fundamental fairness of the proceeding and the alien shows prejudice.
 
The Immigration Judge found the respondent to be inadmissible, and therefore ineligible for adjustment of status, on the basis of “reasonable, substantial, and probative evidence” giving “reason to believe” that he has been an illicit drug trafficker or “a knowing aider, abettor, assister, conspirator, or colluder” in illicit drug trafficking. See section 212(a)(2)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(C); Decision of the Board, Feb. 14, 2013, at 1. The Immigration Judge relied on specific evidence in the record, including police reports and sworn witness statements, indicating the respondent’s direct involvement in selling methamphetamine and cocaine as part of a network headed by his brother. There were at least five informants or witnesses who stated that the respondent served as a “runner” for his brother, and the statements were consistent and corroborative. See id. at 2; I.J. at 5-7.
 
*2 The respondent denies involvement. He asserts that Attorney Litman was ineffective in not submitting to the Immigration Judge approximately 696 pages of transcripts from the criminal trial in which the respondent was acquitted of several counts of possession or distribution of controlled substances, as well as several character references (who express their regard for the respondent and disbelief that he would be a drug trafficker, but do not rebut the specific information considered by the Immigration Judge). See Motion to Reopen, Tabs A, B.
 
The Immigration Judge and Board were aware of the acquittal. As we noted in our prior decision, the acquittal did not end the inquiry for immigration purposes. The jury in the criminal case considered whether the state had proven certain specific, charged acts of drug possession or distribution beyond a reasonable doubt, based on evidence admitted at trial. The Immigration Judge considered whether there is “reason to believe” that the respondent has been involved in trafficking, based on statements of informants gathered in the course of a law enforcement investigation. The Immigration Judge was required to consider whether the informants’ statements constitute evidence that is “““reasonable, substantial, and probative,” even though such statements may not have been admissible into evidence at a criminal trial.
 
The transcripts of the criminal trial include the testimony of three prosecution witnesses, all of whom testified that the respondent participated in his brother’s drug trade. See, e.g., Motion to Reopen, Tab A at 173-81, 297-98, 356. The motion to reopen focuses on the difficulties the prosecution faced in obtaining a conviction. The transcripts contain information that is significantly damaging to the respondent, however, when viewed from the standpoint of a civil matter in which the respondent must show that he warrants an extraordinary grant of discretionary relief. In considering the present motion, it appears to the Board that Attorney Litman’s decision not to offer the criminal trial transcripts at the hearing before the Immigration Judge may have been a reasonable strategic decision. We cannot say that the decision not to introduce the transcripts was egregious. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639 (noting that litigants are generally bound by the conduct of their attorneys, absent egregious circumstances). The motion fails to demonstrate egregious circumstances, or that the addition of the transcripts to the record before the Immigration Judge would change the outcome of the respondent’s case.
 
The Board concludes that ineffectiveness of counsel and prejudice have not been adequately demonstrated. We do not find that reopening of these proceedings has been shown to be warranted. See Matter of Coelho, supra, at 472-73. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
*3 ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s petition for review of the Board’s February 14, 2013, decision is pending in the United States Court of Appeals for the Tenth Circuit. Mena-Flores v. Holder, No. 13-9532 (10th Cir. filed Mar. 15, 2013).

2013 WL 3899846 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MELANIA ARROLIGA
File: AXXX XX7 254 - Miami, FL
July 19, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Juan C. Blanco, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Maria T. Armas
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Nicaragua, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of January 7, 2013. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings in which she was ordered removed in absentia on December 8, 2010. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed an opposition to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts.
 
The respondent alleges in her December 19, 2012, motion that she received ineffective assistance by Mr. Marlon Alberto Gonzalez, who represented to the respondent that he was an attorney. Specifically, the respondent maintains that Mr. Gonzalez told her not to appear for her December 8, 2010, hearing. The Immigration Judge denied the motion as numerically barred,1 and also noted that it was untimely filed.
 
We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “““exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996). Here, the respondent has partially complied with the Lozada requirements. See Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1222-23 (11th Cir. 2003) (a petitioner seeking to show ineffective assistance must meet each of the three screening requirements of Lozada, even where counsel’s ineffective assistance was clear on the record). We need not address whether the respondent adequately complied with the Lozada requirements or the Immigration Judge’s finding that the motion is numerically barred2 because the motion was properly found untimely.
 
Moreover, the respondent has not shown that she exercised due diligence. See Avila-Santoyo v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 713 F.3d 1357, 1358 (11th 2013) (finding that equitable tolling generally requires litigant to show (1) that he has been pursuing his rights diligently, and (2) that some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way). In her December 19, 2012, motion to reopen, the respondent indicates that she suspected “foul play” from Mr. Gonzalez at least by August 2011. Yet the respondent did not seek new counsel until July 2012, and the motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance by Mr. Gonzalez was not filed until December 19, 2012. Consequently, we find that the respondent failed to exercise due diligence in pursuing her claim of ineffective assistance of counsel by delaying over a year in raising the issue.
 
*2 We find that the respondent has not established an exceptional circumstance herein to warrant the sua sponte reopening of these proceedings. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations). With regard to the respondent’s argument that the DHS should exercise its prosecutorial discretion, the decision of whether to exercise its prosecutorial discretion is a matter solely within the purview of the DHS, and neither the Immigration Judge, nor this Board, has jurisdiction to review such matter. See Matter of Bahta, 22 I&N Dec. 1381, 1392 (BIA 2000).
 
For the foregoing reasons, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Elise Manuel
FOR THE BOARD

2

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

IN RE: DIEGO POLANCO
File: AXXX XX7 112 - New York, NY
July 19, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Dominick S. Cardinale, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Colombia, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated May 25, 2012, denying his motion to reopen.1 We will dismiss the appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. As the respondent filed his motion to reopen on April 10, 2012, more than 11 years after he was ordered removed from the United States on September 13, 2000, his motion to reopen was untimely under the general rule requiring an alien to file a motion to reopen within 90 days of the entry of an order of removal. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). While the respondent alleges that his former counsel provided him with ineffective assistance of counsel, he has not established that he exercised the requisite due diligence needed to warrant equitable tolling of the 90-day filing deadline or that an exceptional situation exists which would warrant sua sponte reopening. See e.g., Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 131-32 (2d Cir. 2008); Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 170 (2d Cir. 2006); Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 134 (2d Cir. 2000); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). As recognized by the Immigration Judge the respondent has not adequately explained the lengthy delay in filing his motion. Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant

A request to remand the record to the Immigration Judge to consider the evidence is untimely and number-barred. See Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 2000).

26 I. & N. Dec. 161 (BIA), Interim Decision 3788, 2013 WL 3781299
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
MATTER OF J-G-, RESPONDENT
Decided July 18, 2013
**1 *161 (1) An alien who is subject to an in absentia removal order need not first rescind the order before seeking reopening of the proceedings to apply for asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions arising in the country of the alien’s nationality or the country to which removal has been ordered.
 
(2) The numerical limitations on filing a motion to reopen in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) (2013) are not applicable to an alien seeking reopening to apply for asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions arising in the country of the alien’s nationality or the country to which removal has been ordered.
 
FOR RESPONDENT:
Gary Yerman, Esquire
New York, New York
FOR THE DEPARTMENT OF HOMELAND SECURITY:
Mark Evans
Associate Regional Counsel
BEFORE: Board Panel: MULLANE and CREPPY, Board Members; LIEBOWITZ, Temporary Board Member.
CREPPY, Board Member:
In a decision dated March 11, 2011, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, finding that it was both time and number barred, and he declined to sua sponte reopen the respondent’s removal proceedings. The respondent has appealed from that decision. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded for further proceedings.
  
I. FACTUAL AND PROCEDURAL HISTORY
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of China who arrived in the United States on March 20, 1999. He was ordered removed in absentia on August 9, 1999, after failing to appear at a hearing. On September 2, 2008, the respondent filed a motion to reopen to rescind the in absentia order of removal based on lack of notice pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(ii) (2006). He also sought reopening based on changed country conditions.
 
On November 12, 2008, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen to rescind the in absentia removal order, concluding that *162 he had received proper notice of the hearing. However, the Immigration Judge did not address the respondent’s assertions regarding changed country conditions. The respondent did not appeal the denial of the motion.
 
On January 2, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider the November 12, 2008, decision, arguing that the Immigration Judge should have considered his claim of changed country conditions. On April 16, 2009, the Immigration Judge denied the motion because it was untimely. The respondent did not appeal the denial of his motion to reconsider.
 
On February 28, 2011, the respondent filed a second motion to reopen seeking to apply for asylum and withholding of removal. First, the respondent requested reopening of his removal proceedings based on a claim of changed country conditions arising in China since the date of his last hearing pursuant to section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act. In support of his request he submitted evidence that he stated was material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the time of his August 1999 hearing. Second, he asserted that his motion was not time barred because he is entitled to equitable tolling as a result of ineffective assistance of counsel and due process violations. Finally, he argued that the Immigration Judge should reopen the proceedings sua sponte.
 
**2 The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s second motion to reopen, concluding that it was both time and number barred. In analyzing the respondent’s request to reopen proceedings based on changed country conditions, the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent could not reopen proceedings to apply for asylum and withholding of removal because he was first required to rescind his in absentia removal order under section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act. The Immigration Judge further determined that even if the respondent could seek reopening based on changed country conditions without first rescinding the in absentia order, his motion would nevertheless be denied as time barred. Since it was not the first motion the respondent filed, the Immigration Judge also found it to be number barred. For these reasons, the Immigration Judge did not reach the merits of the respondent’s motion. Finally, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent did not present any exceptional circumstances that would justify reopening the proceedings sua sponte.
  
II. ISSUES
 
The issues before us are: (1) whether an alien who is subject to an in absentia removal order must first rescind the order before he can seek to reopen removal proceedings to apply for asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions; and (2) whether such a motion to reopen is subject to the general numerical limitations on motions to reopen.
 
*163 We conclude that although the respondent is subject to an in absentia removal order, he does not have to first rescind the order before he can seek reopening to apply for asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions arising in China. We also hold that the numerical limitations on filing a motion to reopen in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) (2013) are not applicable because the respondent seeks reopening to apply for asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions arising in China.
  
III. APPLICABLE LAW
 
Prior to 1990, there were no limitations governing the filing of motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 103.5, 242.22 (1989). However, in 1990 Congress added former section 242B of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b (1994), which provided consequences for an alien’s failure to appear at a deportation hearing and a procedure for rescinding an in absentia deportation order through a motion to reopen. Immigration Act of 1990, Pub. L. No. 101-649, § 545(a), 104 Stat. 4978, 5061-62 (“IMMACT 90”). Section 242B(e) provided that an alien who was subject to an in absentia deportation order was ineligible for certain forms of discretionary relief for a period of 5 years. Notably, asylum was not one of the forms of relief for which an alien would remain ineligible for the 5-year period. Furthermore, the statute did not specify that an alien was required to first rescind an in absentia order before applying for other available relief. It also did not indicate whether an order of exclusion could be entered in absentia, and it included no procedure to govern the reopening of an in absentia order entered in an exclusion hearing. See former section 236(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1226(a) (1994).
 
**3 As part of IMMACT 90, Congress also instructed the Attorney General to ““issue regulations with respect to . . . the period of time in which motions to reopen and to reconsider may be offered in deportation proceedings, which regulations include a limitation on the number of such motions that may be filed and a maximum time period for the filing of such motions.” IMMACT 90 § 545(d), 104 Stat. at 5066. In response to this mandate, the Attorney General promulgated regulations providing that “a party may file only one motion to reopen proceedings . . . and that motion must be filed no later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered.” 8 C.F.R. §§ 3.2(c)(2), 3.23b)(4)(i) (1997).
 
The regulations also created exceptions to the 90-day filing deadline for: (1) motions to reopen deportation proceedings to rescind an in absentia order of deportation; (2) motions to reopen to apply for asylum or withholding of deportation based on changed country conditions; and *164 (3) motions jointly filed by both parties. 8 C.F.R. §§ 3.2(c)(3)(i)-(iii), 3.23(b)(4)(i)-(iii). On July 1, 1996, the Attorney General implemented the final regulations governing motions to reopen. Executive Office for Immigration Review; Motions and Appeals in Immigration Proceedings; Correction, 61 Fed. Reg. 32,924 (June 26, 1996).
 
In 1996, Congress enacted the language of the current statute, which codified the restrictions on motions to reopen to rescind an in absentia order of removal. Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996, Div. C, Pub. L. No. 104-208, § 304(a)(3), 110 Stat. 3009-546, 3009-590, 3009-593 (“IIRIRA”). At that time, the statute provided the following exceptions to the 90-day deadline: (1) no time limit for motions to reopen to apply for asylum and withholding based on changed country conditions; (2) 180 days after the entry of the final order of removal for motions to reopen to rescind an in absentia order if failure to appear was based on exceptional circumstances; and (3) no time limit for motions to reopen to rescind an in absentia order if the alien did not receive notice. Sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i)-(ii), (c)(6)(C)(ii)-(iii) of the Act. The statute was later redesignated as section 240(c)(7) of the Act. REAL ID Act of 2005, Div. B, Pub. L. No. 109-13, § 101(d)(1), 119 Stat. 302, 304.
  
III. ANALYSIS
  
A. Reopening To Apply for Asylum and Withholding of Removal Based on Changed Country Conditions
 
With this background in mind, we now turn to the question whether the current statute and regulations allow an alien who is subject to an in absentia removal order to reopen the proceedings to apply for asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions without also seeking to rescind the removal order. As in any case involving statutory interpretation, the touchstone of our analysis is the plain language of the statute. Lamie v. U.S. Trustee, 540 U.S. 526, 534 (2004) (citing Hughes Aircraft Co. v. Jacobson, 525 U.S. 432, 438 (1999)).
 
**4 Generally, a motion to reopen removal proceedings must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of a final order of removal, and only one motion may be filed. Sections 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(i) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b)(1) (2013). The relevant statutory exceptions to the filing deadlines are articulated in sections 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) and (iii) of the Act. According to section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii), the 90-day deadline does not apply if the basis of the motion is to apply for asylum or withholding of removal and is based on changed country conditions arising in the country of nationality, if the evidence to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the previous *165 proceeding. See also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3)(ii), 1003.23(b)(4)(i) (stating that the time and numerical limits are inapplicable if the basis for reopening is to apply for asylum or withholding of removal based on changed country conditions). Section 240(c)(7)(C)(iii) of the Act provides that the filing deadlines for a motion to reopen an order entered in absentia are listed in section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, which states that an in absentia order of removal may only be rescinded:
(i) upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances (as defined in subsection (e)(1)), or
 
(ii) upon a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that the alien did not receive notice in accordance with paragraph (1) or (2) of section 239(a) or the alien demonstrates that the alien was in Federal or State custody and the failure to appear was through no fault of the alien.
 
See also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3)(i), 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) (providing that only one motion to reopen seeking rescission of an in absentia order entered in removal proceedings may be filed).
 
Thus, the only way an alien can rescind an in absentia order is by demonstrating (1) that there were exceptional circumstances; (2) that the alien did not receive notice; or (3) that he or she was in Federal or State custody and failed to appear through no fault of his or her own. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Although the statute outlines how an alien may rescind an in absentia order, nothing in either the statute or the regulations governing motions to reopen prohibits an alien from seeking to reopen proceedings for the limited purpose of applying for asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions without rescinding the in absentia order of removal.
 
**5 The textual structure of the Act supports our conclusion that rescission is not required to reopen proceedings to apply for asylum and withholding of removal. Congress listed the bases for seeking reopening in separate and independent sections of the Act. See sections 240(c)(7)(C)(ii)-(iv) of the Act (entitled “Asylum,” “Failure to Appear,” and “Special Rule for Battered Spouses, Children, and Parents”). Thus, the filing deadlines for reopening are different depending on whether the reason for reopening is to apply for asylum, rescind an in absentia order, or seek relief as a result of being battered. Id.; see also section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act.
 
The legislative history of section 240 of the Act provides no meaningful guidance on this question because the explanatory material simply paraphrases the language of the statute. See H.R. Conf. Rep. No. 104-828 (1996), available at 1996 WL 563320; H.R. Rep. No. 104-469(I) (1996), available at 1996 WL 168955. In examining the legislative history that accompanies the statute creating the filing deadlines and exceptions *166 governing motions to reopen, we find nothing that requires aliens who seek to reopen proceedings to apply for asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions to first rescind an in absentia order.
 
Our holding today is consistent with our prior precedent, which held that, in certain circumstances, an alien who is subject to an in absentia order could seek to reopen deportation or exclusion proceedings without also rescinding the in absentia order. In Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349, 356-57 (BIA 1998) (en banc), which interpreted former section 242B(c)(3)(A) of the Act in the context of deportation proceedings, we concluded that an order of deportation entered in absentia need not always be rescinded before the proceedings may be reopened.1 In reaching that conclusion, we relied on the fact that nothing in the Act or regulations specifically required an in absentia order to be rescinded before proceedings could be reopened, noting that the statute merely described the procedure for rescinding such an order. Id. at 352, 356.
 
Although there was no provision in either the statute or regulations that addressed in absentia orders in exclusion proceedings, we held in Matter of A-N- & R-M-N-, 22 I&N Dec. 953, 955-56 (BIA 1999), that exclusion proceedings conducted in absentia could be reopened in the absence of a showing of ““reasonable cause” where the alien sought reopening to apply for asylum or withholding of deportation and demonstrated changed country conditions that indicated a reasonable likelihood of success on the merits. See 8 C.F.R. § 3.23(b)(4)(iii)(B) (1998) (providing that a motion to reopen exclusion proceedings on the basis that an Immigration Judge improperly entered an in absentia order must show that the alien had “reasonable cause” for failing to appear). In so finding, we were persuaded by the fact that the aliens were not challenging the propriety of the in absentia order. Id. at 956.
 
**6 Given that the language in section 240(c)(7) of the Act is substantially similar to former section 242B, we find that the reasoning in Matter of M-S- is applicable in the context of removal proceedings. Section 242B(e)(1) provided that an alien subject to an in absentia deportation order was ineligible for certain forms of discretionary relief for a period of 5 years. We noted in Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. at 355, that if Congress intended to require an alien who was subject to an in absentia deportation order to *167 rescind that order before applying for relief, it would render the language of former section 242B(e)(1) surplusage.2
 
The same reasoning applies in removal proceedings. Section 240(b)(7) of the Act explicitly bars an alien with an in absentia removal order from reopening his or her case to apply for certain forms of discretionary relief within 10 years from the date of the final order of removal. According to section 240(b)(7),
Any alien against whom a final order of removal is entered in absentia under this subsection and who . . . was provided oral notice, either in the alien’s native language or in another language the alien understands, of the time and place of the proceedings and of the consequences under this paragraph of failing, other than because of exceptional circumstances . . ., to attend a proceeding under this section, shall not be eligible for relief under section 240A, 240B, 245, 248, or 249 for a period of 10 years after the date of the entry of the final order of removal.
 
 
It is well settled that no provision of law should be construed so as to render superfluous other provisions in the same statute. Kungys v. United States, 485 U.S. 759, 778 (1988). Thus, section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act must be read together with section 240(b)(7) to ascertain its meaning. If we determined that rescission of an in absentia removal order is a condition precedent to reopening removal proceedings, then section 240(b)(7) would be surplusage, because the effect of rescinding an in absentia removal order is that an alien essentially “starts over.” Thus, there are no bars to discretionary relief once an in absentia order is rescinded.
 
If section 240(b)(7) of the Act is to be given some effect, therefore, it must be applied to an alien who is subject to an in absentia removal order, but who seeks reopening without also rescinding the order. In those situations, section 240(b)(7) would apply to bar the alien from applying for the enumerated forms of discretionary relief for a period of 10 years, as long as the alien was given the required notice for failure to appear. We note, however, that asylum was not one of the forms of relief for which an alien subject to an in absentia order would remain ineligible for the 5-year period under former section 242B(e) of the Act or for the 10-year period under section 240(b)(7).
 
**7 Finally, requiring an alien to first rescind an in absentia order of removal before reopening to apply for asylum or withholding of removal would effectively bar an alien who met the requirements for reopening *168 under section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act from having an asylum or withholding of removal claim adjudicated.
 
In sum, based on our analysis of the past and present statutes, we hold that an alien who is subject to an in absentia removal order need not first rescind the order before seeking reopening of the proceedings to apply for asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions arising in the country of the alien’s nationality or the country to which removal has been ordered.3
  
B. Numerical Limitations on Motions To Reopen
 
The Immigration Judge also determined that, even assuming that an alien who is subject to an in absentia removal order can reopen proceedings to apply for asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions without rescinding the removal order, the respondent’s current motion is barred by the numerical limitation on filing motions to reopen because he had previously filed another motion. In so holding, the Immigration Judge concluded that section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act only waives the time limitation on motions to reopen, but not the numerical limitation of section 240(c)(7)(A).
 
The statute is silent regarding any numerical exception for motions to reopen to apply for asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions. However, we and the Immigration Judges are bound by the current regulations, which specifically waive both the time and numerical limitations for such motions. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3)(ii), 1003.23(b)(4)(i).4 There is no indication in the legislative history of section 240(c)(7) that in enacting the IIRIRA, Congress did not intend to also *169 waive the numerical limitation, as the regulations had previously done. See 8 C.F.R. § § 3.2(c)(2)(ii), 3.23b)(4)(ii) (1997).
 
The respondent filed a second motion to reopen to apply for asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions in China. Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i), such a motion is not subject to the numerical limitations in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). We therefore conclude that the Immigration Judge improperly determined that the respondent’s motion to reopen was numerically barred.
  
C. Respondent’s Motion To Reopen
 
Turning to the respondent’s motion to reopen, we note that in seeking to reopen to apply for asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions, an alien must meet the requirements set forth in section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act and 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i). Therefore, in addition to showing changed country conditions, an alien must submit evidence that is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the prior hearing. Additionally, an alien seeking reopening must establish prima facie eligibility for the underlying relief sought. See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314, 323 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104-05 (1988). Thus, the alien bears a heavy burden to show that his proffered evidence is material, reflects changed country conditions arising in the country of nationality, and supports a prima facie case for a grant of asylum. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472 (BIA 1992). Finally, where the ultimate relief is discretionary, a moving party must also show that a favorable exercise of discretion is warranted. See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. at 323.
 
**8 The respondent filed a motion to reopen for the purpose of applying for asylum and withholding of removal based on his religious conversion and the alleged increase in persecution of underground Catholic churches in China. Based on the above analysis, we conclude that the Immigration Judge had jurisdiction to consider the respondent’s motion and that the respondent was not required to first rescind the in absentia removal order before seeking to reopen his removal proceedings. Moreover, since the basis of the respondent’s motion is to apply for asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions, the numerical limitations in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) do not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i).
 
The Immigration Judge did not address the merits of the respondent’s argument that reopening of the proceedings is warranted because of changed country conditions in China. Nor did he evaluate whether the evidence the respondent submitted in support of his motion is material and *170 was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the prior hearing. We therefore find that a remand of the record is appropriate.5 Section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i). On remand, the Immigration Judge should evaluate whether the evidence submitted by the respondent indicates a reasonable likelihood that his application will succeed on the merits if the proceedings are reopened.
 
In view of our disposition of the respondent’s appeal, it is not necessary to address his remaining contentions. See INS v. Bagamasbad, 429 U.S. 24, 25-26 (1976) (stating that, as a general rule, courts and agencies are not required to make findings on issues the decision of which is unnecessary to the results they reach); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462, 465 (BIA 2002).
  
V. CONCLUSION
 
In sum, we conclude that an alien who is subject to an in absentia removal order need not first rescind the order before seeking reopening of the proceedings to apply for asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions arising in the country of the alien’s nationality or the country to which removal has been ordered. We further hold that the numerical limitations on filing a motion to reopen in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) are not applicable to an alien seeking reopening to apply for asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions arising in the country of the alien’s nationality or the country to which removal has been ordered. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
**9 ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.

Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. at 356-57.

*1 This matter was last before the Board on May 7, 2013, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s January 26, 2012, decision. At that time, we also declined to reinstate the period of voluntary departure that had been granted by the Immigration Judge, finding that the respondent failed to present proof of payment of the voluntary departure bond within 30 days of filing the appeal, as required by regulations that became effective on January 20, 2009. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(c)(3)(ii). The respondent’s unopposed motion to reopen will be granted.

FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the DHS. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.

See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he has remained outside of the United States. Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TYRONE ANTHONY ROBERTS
File: AXXX XX6 167 - Atlanta, GA
July 9, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nita Dobroshi, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Randall Duncan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 On May 7, 2012, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed from the United States, and appeal was waived. See I.J. dec. at 2; Tr. at 8. Nevertheless, the respondent filed two Notices of Appeal forms through different counsel. We dismissed the respondent’s appeal for lack of jurisdiction on August 23, 2012, noting that the respondent did not assert or argue that the decision to waive appeal was not knowingly and intelligently made. We also observed that the respondent did not contend that he was provided with ineffective assistance of counsel at the hearing, nor had he demonstrated compliance with the threshold requirements for making such a claim. The respondent’s motion to reopen, alleging ineffective assistance by two prior attorneys in the waiver of the appeal and prior representation before the Board, was received on May 21, 2013. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
The pending motion is untimely. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The United States Court of Appeals has held that equitable tolling of the time limit may be available in certain circumstances where there is ineffective assistance. See Ruiz-Turcios v. U.S. Att’y Gen., __ F.3d. __, 2013 WL 2262470 (11th Cir. 2013). In this case, the respondent claims ineffective assistance was provided by his former attorneys in conjunction with the waiver of appeal. However, the respondent has not shown substantial compliance with the requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Dakane v. United States Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005). Although the respondent has filed complaints against two attorneys with the Office of the General Counsel (OGC), he has not shown that he has filed any complaint against either attorney with the applicable state bar association. In addition, while each former attorney was notified that he filed an Immigration Practitioner Complaint form with the OGC, neither notification letter specified that the respondent was seeking reopening before the Board based on ineffective assistance allegations. Consequently, the respondent’s former attorneys were not made aware of the pending motion or their ability to respond to the allegations before the Board. Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied. The Board’s May 24, 2013, order granting a stay of removal is vacated.
 
*2 Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2013 WL 3899814 (BIA)
End of Document

2013 WL 3899812 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAROFF EL HAJ SIDIKI OUEDRAOGO A.K.A. MAROFF OUEDRAOGO
File: AXXX XX7 569 - West Valley, UT
July 9, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
German T. Flores, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jeffrey D. Clark
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native of Ivory Coast and citizen of Burkina Faso, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 5, 2013. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable and found him ineligible for relief from removal. On appeal, the respondent argues that he was denied his right to counsel. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On July 13, 2012, the respondent was convicted of Possession of a Controlled Substance (Marijuana) with Intent to Manufacture or Deliver, in violation of Idaho law. That conviction was not appealed. The detained respondent was placed in removal proceedings on July 16, 2012. His first hearing was on February 5, 2013. At that time, the respondent told the Immigration Judge that he had been consulting an attorney in Utah (Mr. Flores), and was told to ask for a 2-week continuance to enable counsel to represent him. The Immigration Judge granted him a 2-week continuance (Tr. at 8-9). On February 19, 2013, the respondent told the Immigration Judge that he had negotiated an agreement with an attorney (Mr. Flores), and he was having money wired to him that week. On that basis, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent a 1-week continuance, but he told the respondent that, if the attorney did not appear, he would have to represent himself (Tr. at 11-12).
 
On February 25, 2013, nothing was said about the Utah attorney (Mr. Flores). The respondent provided the Immigration Judge with the card of an attorney in Idaho (Mr. Sutton) and asked him to call him. When he did so, the attorney was not there, and the receptionist said that the attorney had not yet been retained. The Immigration Judge gave the respondent another 1-week continuance to enable counsel to file his notice of appearance (Tr. at 18-20). On March 4, 2013, the respondent told a new Immigration Judge that he had hired an attorney in Idaho to help him pursue post-conviction relief. Nothing was said about the Utah attorney (Mr. Flores). The Immigration Judge considered giving him another continuance to hire an attorney, but the attorney for the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) advised her that the respondent had already received 3 continuances. The Immigration Judge then concluded that he had been allowed sufficient time to hire an attorney and would be required to represent himself because he had received 3 continuances, because he was in custody, and because he was a mandatory detainee (Tr. at 22-23). The Immigration Judge then proceeded to take pleadings, found the respondent removable, found him ineligible for relief from removal, and ordered him removed (Tr. at 23-27).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent argues that he was given insufficient time to retain counsel and was thereby denied his right to counsel.
 
Respondents in immigration proceedings have the statutory and regulatory “““privilege of being represented,” at no expense to the Government, by counsel of their choosing. See sections 240(b)(4)(A), 292 of the Act; 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(b)(4)(A), 1362. See also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.16(b), 1240.3, 1240.10(a), 1240.11(c)(1)(iii); Matter of C-B-, 25 I&N Dec. 888 (BIA 2012). Where the statutory and regulatory privilege of legal representation is not expressly waived, as is the case here, in order to meaningfully effectuate the privilege, the Immigration Judge must grant a reasonable and realistic amount of time and provide a fair opportunity for a respondent to seek, speak with, and retain counsel. See Matter of C-B-, supra. Moreover, an Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.298, 1240.6. See also Matter of C-B-, supra. Cf. Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not show good cause for another continuance. He had 3 continuances amounting to a 1-month period. In light of the facts presented, we conclude that he was granted a reasonable and realistic amount of time and provided a fair opportunity to seek, speak with, and retain counsel.
 
The respondent is represented on appeal by the attorney who he was attempting to retain since his first hearing on February 5, 2013. While he had been in contact with counsel from February 5, 2013, counsel did not submit an entry of appearance during the course of that month. Moreover, counsel has not explained to the Immigration Judge or this Board why he did not file an entry of appearance during the course of that month.
 
On appeal, the respondent also requests remand on the grounds that the conviction, which serves as the basis for removal, and which renders him ineligible for relief from removal, is being challenged before the criminal court pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010).1 The respondent alleges that he was not advised of the immigration consequences of his conviction, and he has filed a petition to vacate it.
 
*3 We note that the petition was not even filed until April 5, 2013, a month after the respondent’s final hearing, and the respondent does not indicate when the petition will be heard by the state court. The respondent has submitted a copy of the petition and its docket history with the appeal. However, the respondent has not submitted an affidavit from his prior criminal attorney admitting that he failed to advise the respondent concerning the immigration consequences of the plea, or misadvised him, as the respondent alleges. Nor has the respondent submitted his own affidavit indicating, under penalty of perjury, what his prior criminal attorney advised him concerning the immigration consequences of the plea. Consequently, this Board has insufficient evidence to conclude that the conviction will likely be vacated.
 
While the respondent is free to pursue the post-conviction petition, it does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). The post-conviction petition to vacate the conviction does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In Padilla v. Kentucky, the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel. See also Chaidez v. United States, 568 U.S. ___, 133 S.Ct. 1103 (2013) (holding that Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, which requires defense counsel to advise a defendant about the risk of deportation arising from a guilty plea, does not apply retroactively).

IN RE: SONIA MARITZA PONCE-HERNANDEZ
File: AXXX XX8 245 - Orlando, FL
July 8, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David H. Stoller, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Manuel Ramirez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On December 6, 2007, the Immigration Judge issued an order of removal in absentia. On June 27, 2011, the respondent filed a motion to reopen, alleging that she did not receive notice of the hearing. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen on the ground that notice of the hearing was mailed to the respondent’s last known address of record and that this notice was sufficient. The respondent filed a timely Notice of Appeal. On July 11, 2012, the Board dismissed the appeal.
 
On April 26, 2013, the respondent filed a second motion to reopen. With new counsel, she alleges that proceedings should be reopened because her former counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel. In support of this argument, the respondent presents additional evidence in support of her assertion that she did not receive the notice of hearing. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the motion.
 
In reviewing the record in light of the current allegations, the respondent’s affidavit, and Board precedent, we find that there is a legitimate basis to conclude that the respondent overcame the presumption of delivery regarding the notice of his December 6, 2007, hearing. See Matter of Anyelo, 25 I&N Dec. 337, 339 (BIA 2010); Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665 (BIA 2008) (presumption of delivery as to regular mail weaker than presumption that applies to certified mail); Matter of C-R-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 677 (BIA 2008) (presumption of delivery overcome where alien asserts he did not receive notice, he continued to reside at same address, and had no reason to not appear).
 
Moreover, we find that the respondent has overcome the lower presumption of delivery that attaches to regular mail. In particular, we are persuaded by the affidavits of the respondent and her brother, reflecting that they, nor anyone at their residence, received the Notice of Hearing (“NOH”). See Matter of Anyelo, supra; Matter of M-R-A-, supra; Matter of C-R-C-, supra. Under the circumstances, we conclude that the evidence of record sufficiently overcomes the presumption that the respondent received the NOH sent to her by regular mail and that her removal proceedings should be reopened. Accordingly, the following orders shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The prior decisions are vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and the entry of a new decision.
 
*2 Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

2013 WL 3899754 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JANET GERTRUDIS CASILLAS
File: AXXX XX7 330 - Chicago, IL
July 5, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Charles M. Shepherd, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF SERVICE:
 
Marc A. Jones
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Peru, was ordered removed from the United States, following her failure to appear for a hearing on October 17, 2007. On December 2, 2009, she filed a motion to reopen pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). Her motion to reopen was granted on December 22, 2009, and her case was rescheduled for a new master calendar hearing. On August 18, 2010, the respondent and her attorney of record failed to appear. The Immigration Judge reinstated the original in absentia order of removal and directed the respondent’s removal to Peru based on the charges contained in the Notice to Appear (“NTA”). On September 1, 2011, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. In a decision dated October 3, 2011, the Immigration Judge denied reopening. This appeal follows. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews findings of fact by an Immigration Judge only to determine whether they are clearly erroneous. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462, 464-65 (BIA 2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We will affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying reopening. The respondent does not dispute that she received the NTA on August 30, 2007, which contained the consequences for failing to appear at a scheduled removal hearing. She contends, however, that she never received notice of the August 18, 2010, hearing date and at no time did her attorney inform her that her matter had been set for that date. See December 2, 2009, Motion to Reopen. We have held, however, that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1995). When the court sent notice of the August 18, 2010, hearing date, it sent it to the respondent’s attorney of record, Mr. Solomon Gilliam.1 The respondent’s failure to appear for her hearing is not excused for lack of proper notice. While a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel based on a failure of counsel to inform his client of the time and date of the hearing can constitute exceptional circumstances, the respondent did not comply with the procedural requirements for making a proper ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Marinov v. Holder, 687 F.3d 365 (7th Cir. 2012); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988) (setting forth defined procedural requirements to prove a due process violation based on the performance of counsel). See also Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 505 (BIA 1980) (the assertions of counsel do not constitute evidence). Further, contrary to the respondent’s contention on appeal, the Immigration Judge did not find that her prior counsel appeared at the August 18, 2010, hearing. Rather, he noted that counsel withdrew after that date.
 
*2 Accordingly, we will dismiss the appeal.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD

1

Following entry of the in absentia order of removal, attorney Gilliam filed a “Motion to Withdraw as Attorney of Record for Respondent” on August 31, 2010. His motion was granted on September 10, 2010.

2013 WL 3899774 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BOLIVAR PATRICIO YUNGA
File: AXXX XX6 403 - New York, NY
July 2, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ruchi Thaker, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Paula J. Donnolo
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was before the Board on February 25, 2013, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s July 22, 2011, decision denying his application for adjustment of status. The respondent filed this motion to reopen his removal proceedings on May 24, 2013, to re-apply for adjustment of status. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7). The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
The Board may consider a timely motion to reopen if the motion states the new facts that would be proven at a new hearing, and is supported by affidavits or other evidence that is material and was not available at the time of the prior hearing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). In Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472-73 (BIA 1988), the Board explained that a party who seeks to reopen proceedings bears a “heavy burden” of proving that the new evidence would likely change the result in the case.
 
As the Board noted in the prior decision, adjustment of status is an extraordinary form of relief which was not designed to supersede the regular consular process of visa issuance abroad, and may be granted only where a favorable exercised of discretion is shown to be warranted. In this case, the Immigration Judge noted that the respondent “has accumulated a lengthy criminal history since his arrival in the U.S.” and has “continually flouted the laws of the U.S.,” with a record that includes multiple convictions for the same and similar offenses. See I.J. at 6. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent failed to show that the positive equities in his case outweigh the negative factors. Id.
 
In support of reopening, the respondent offers voluminous evidence, including a psychological report dated May 2013 concerning his progress in rehabilitation and recovery from alcohol dependence. See Motion to Reopen, Tab R. The report itself is new but relies extensively on information existing previously. The respondent claims in his motion that this evidence and the other evidence now presented was not obtained and presented previously due to ineffective assistance of counsel. The Board held in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), that due process rights may be violated when ineffective assistance of counsel prevents an alien from reasonably presenting his case. In order to demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel, the alien must demonstrate that he was prejudiced.
 
*2 The Board concludes that the respondent received a full and fair hearing at which the Immigration Judge properly considered and evaluated his evidence (including the respondent’s claim to sobriety since 2005) and correctly determined the legal issues. The respondent’s continued progress toward rehabilitation and recovery is commendable. However, even considering the new evidence submitted with the motion and the entire record, the Board finds no basis for reopening and remanding the case for further proceedings. The respondent’s equities were weighed against the respondent’s lengthy criminal history. The respondent’s motion does not meet his “heavy burden” to show that proffered new evidence would likely change the result in his case. See Matter of Coelho, supra. Prejudice from the claimed ineffectiveness of counsel has not been shown. See Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
The Board concludes that the final removal order in this matter is not subject to reopening, and the respondent’s motion will be denied
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen and motion for a stay are denied. All other pending motions in this matter are denied as moot.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD

2013 WL 3899767 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSEPH ENNEVOR ALLEN, JR.
File: AXXX XX7 875 - Orlando, FL
June 28, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rusten C. Hurd, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on February 20, 2013, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s application for adjustment of status as a matter of discretion. In the prior decision, the Board affirmed this decision on de novo review. See Matter of Blas, 15 I&N Dec. 626 (BIA 1974); Matter of Arai, 13 I&N Dec. 494 (BIA 1970). Among the negative factors noted by the Immigration Judge and the Board was the respondent’s 2003 conviction for improper exhibition of a firearm, which stemmed from a 2001 arrest where he was charged by the police with an attempted home invasion robbery, shooting into an occupied dwelling, and use of a firearm during a felony.
 
In the motion, the respondent argues that his case should be reopened based on the ineffective assistance of counsel by attorney Nashid Sabir, who represented him before the Immigration Judge and the Board (the “former counsel”). The respondent argues that the former counsel provided ineffective assistance by failing to submit to the Immigration Judge or the Board copies of the deposition transcripts of two witnesses in his criminal case that resulted in his 2003 conviction, even though he gave these documents to the former counsel before his hearing. The respondent argues that these documents show that these witnesses did not positively identify him as the person who was involved in the robbery attempt and shooting at the house of Lincoln Samuels, the victim. He suggests that these documents would have contradicted the police detective’s report submitted in his case (Exh. 6) and would have supported his contention that he was not the person involved in this incident.
 
However, the Immigration Judge and the Board are not in the position to relitigate the respondent’s criminal case leading to his 2003 conviction. Furthermore, the respondent does not deny in the motion, as the Board noted in the prior decision, that he has been arrested multiple times, although not all of the arrests resulted in criminal convictions. The respondent likewise does not deny that he actually pled no contest to a reduced charge of improper exhibition of a firearm, a first degree misdemeanor. In any event, while the respondent points out that the deposition transcripts of Arlene Frances McCormick indicated that she was not sure whether the gunman’s appearance matched that of a photograph shown to her (Motion, at 5-6; Motion Exh. C, at 16, 201), the police detective’s report showed that there were other witnesses who positively identified the respondent and provided sworn statements (Exh. 6). The respondent also points out that the deposition transcripts of Keino Samuels indicated that he had “no idea who did the shooting” (Motion, at 6; Motion Exh. D, at 28). However, both the police detective’s report and the deposition transcripts show that Keino Samuels was the victim’s younger son and he was in the bedroom in the house when the shooting occurred (Exh. 6; Motion Exh. D, at 27-28).
 
*2 Based on the above, we do not find that the former attorney’s failure to submit the deposition transcripts rendered his assistance “deficient to the point where the hearing was fundamentally unfair and [the respondent] was unable to reasonably present his case,” such that he was prejudiced by this failure. Dakane v. U.S. Attorney General, 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005). Since the respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by the claimed ineffective assistance of counsel, we do not find that reopening is warranted based on ineffective assistance of counsel. Furthermore, even if we were to consider the documents submitted with the motion in determining whether the respondent should be granted adjustment of status as a matter of discretion, on de novo review we would not find that they would warrant a different outcome in the respondent’s case. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The deposition transcripts submitted with the motion have two sets of page numbering, and we have used the consecutive numbering at the bottom center of each page.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
