IN RE: JORGE MIKE ORDONEZ-DIAZ
File: AXXX XX2 395 - Harlingen, TX
June 28, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michelle Mendez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
David Delgado
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On May 9, 2013, the respondent submitted this third, untimely motion to reopen his removal proceedings. The final administrative decision remains the Immigration Judge’s in absentia removal order of October 10, 2002. This motion is both untimely and number-barred. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent alleges that his prior counsel rendered ineffective assistance in the creation and submission of his second, untimely motion to reopen, filed with the Immigration Judge on May 11, 2010. He contends prior counsel’s representation was also deficient when appealing the denial of the motion to reopen to the Board, and while filing a motion to reconsider the subsequent Board dismissal. Finally, the respondent contends prior counsel erroneously charged him filing fees and pocketed the money, and failed to file applications and petitions with United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) despite being paid to do so.
 
The respondent’s motion does not fall within any exception to the time and number limitations on motions to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(i)-(iv).
 
To the extent that the respondent alleges equitable tolling of the time and number limits is warranted, we are not persuaded that equitable tolling is warranted. See Ramos-Bonilla v. Mukasey, 543 F.3d 216, 220 (5th Cir. 2008) (holding a request for “equitable tolling of a time- or number-barred motion to reopen on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel is in essence an argument that the BIA should have exercised its discretion to reopen the proceeding sua sponte based upon the doctrine of equitable tolling”) (internal citations omitted); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (noting the Board’s discretionary authority to reopen sua sponte is reserved for rare, “exceptional” situations).
 
We note the untimely and number-barred nature of this motion does not appear related to the ineffective assistance in filing the second motion to reopen, the subsequent appeal, or the motion to reconsider.1
 
Additionally, the respondent has not established he was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance rendered in this case. We understand the respondent’s concerns regarding the financial malfeasance alleged in this case, both with respect to the filing fees and the attorney’s failure to file applications and petitions before USCIS, but those allegations do not show the respondent suffered any prejudice within his removal proceedings. Insofar as the respondent alleges the second motion to reopen was “perfunctory and did not meet the standard of work product of a competent immigration attorney” (Motion at 7), we also conclude the respondent has not established he was prejudiced by the denial of the second motion. The respondent acknowledges that the motion was both untimely and number barred, and he has not proffered the additional arguments or evidence which should ostensibly have been presented to establish the motion fell within any exception to the time or number limits. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(i)-(iv).
 
*2 Upon examination of the evidence, arguments, and record in this case, we find no “exceptional” situation which would warrant exercise of our discretionary authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte. Matter of J-J-, supra. This motion will be denied as untimely and number-barred.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent’s first motion to reopen, submitted on December 2, 2009, was filed by a separate attorney who has not been accused of ineffective assistance. That motion, which alleged that the respondent had not received notice of his hearing of October 10, 2002, was denied by the Immigration Judge because the respondent had been personally served with the hearing notice contained on the Notice to Appear.

IN RE: MARIA IRERI RIVAS
File: AXXX XX3 062 - Reno, NV
June 24, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lisa Weissman-Ward, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Wayne H. Price
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(D)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i)] - Conditional resident status terminated
 
Sec. 237(a)(3)(D)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(3)(D)(i)] - False claim of United States citizenship
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)] - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(6)(C)(ii)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(ii)(I)] - False claim of United States citizenship
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand; stay of removal
 
The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen her removal proceedings to challenge the removability charges under sections 237(a)(3)(D)(i) and (a)(1)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1227(a)(3)(D)(i) and (a)(1)(A), and to have the Immigration Judge review the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services’ (“USCIS”) denial of her application for a hardship waiver under section 216(c)(4)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1186a(c)(4)(B). In our decision dated January 30, 2013, we dismissed her appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision which denied her motion to suppress and found her removable as charged above. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes her motion. The motion will be granted.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen filed on April 29, 2013, is timely. She alleges ineffective assistance of former counsel (F. Woodside Wright). In Matter of Compean, Bangaly, & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009), the Attorney General directed the Board to continue to apply the previously established standards for reviewing motions to reopen based on claims of ineffective assistance of counsel (pending the outcome of a rulemaking process).
 
The respondent meets the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), to allege ineffective assistance of former counsel. She presents her declaration, counsel’s letter to Mr. Wright, and a copy of the complaint filed against Mr. Wright (Motion Exhs. at 55-89).
 
The respondent shows prejudice. See Lin v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 1014, 1027 (9th Cir. 2004) (the court does not require that an alien’s representation be brilliant, but it cannot serve to make the immigration hearing so fundamentally unfair that the alien was prevented from reasonably presenting his case). She presents extensive evidence to support her contention that she entered into her first marriage in good faith (Motion Exhs. at 145-260). Mr. Wright, by not seeking review by the Immigration Judge of the USCIS’s denial of her second Form I-751 (application for a waiver under section 216(c)(4)(B) of the Act), prevented her from reasonably presenting her case. This may have affected the outcome of the case. See Correa-Rivera v. Holder, 706 F.3d 1128, 1133 (9th Cir. 2013) (the court will find prejudice when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings).
 
*2 The respondent also shows prejudice regarding the two removability charges she challenges. See Correa-Rivera v. Holder, supra. Section 237(a)(3)(D)(i) of the Act provides, in pertinent part, that any alien who has falsely represented himself or herself to be a U.S. citizen for any purpose or benefit under this Act (including section 274A) or any Federal or State law is deportable. Section 212(a)(6)(C)(ii)(I) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(ii)(I), uses the same language except that the alien is “““inadmissible” instead of deportable. She presents evidence in support of her contention that stating that she was a U.S. citizen on her application for admission to the University of Nevada, Reno (Exhs. 2, 3), was not for any purpose or benefit under Nevada law (Motion Exhs. at 90-144). Mr. Wright did not present any evidence on this issue (other than in support of the motion to suppress). Her evidence may have affected the outcome of the case. We conclude that she shows prejudice under the standard in Correa-Rivera v. Holder, supra.1
 
Reopening and remand to the Immigration Judge are warranted because the respondent meets the requirements in Matter of Lozada, supra, and shows prejudice. Since her motion to reopen is timely filed, due diligence is not an issue. Because we will grant her motion, her stay request has been rendered moot.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, and the proceedings are reopened.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

On remand the respondent and the DHS may submit additional evidence and present legal arguments regarding this issue.

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TOMAS VELAZQUEZ-RAMIREZ
File: AXXX XX6 644 - Charlotte, NC
June 20, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Yesenia Polanco, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Melissa K. Metz
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
On April 19, 2011, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed from the United States in absentia, because he failed to appear for his hearing. The respondent filed a timely motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge on May 9, 2011, but that request was denied on May 10, 2011. The respondent has appealed from that decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The record reflects that the respondent was served with the Notice to Appear on January 6, 2011, in person. His counsel was notified by regular mail of the April 19, 2011, removal hearing, on February 15, 2011, by regular mail. There is no support for the statement by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) in its brief that the notice was mailed to both the respondent and to his attorney. The motion to reopen maintained that the respondent did not receive notice of the hearing. The respondent’s attorney asked that proceedings be reopened and the respondent be given an opportunity to apply for cancellation of removal or that a continuance be granted for his wife to be naturalized. The respondent noted that he has an approved Form I-130 filed by his now United States citizen wife, and that he is eligible to apply for cancellation of removal and for voluntary departure, in the alternative.
 
As correctly indicated in the Immigration Judge’s decision, a Notice of Hearing sent to an alien’s attorney of record is considered constructive notice to the alien. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.26(c), 1292.5; Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255, 258 (BIA 1985). The respondent did not raise the issue of ineffective assistance of counsel to establish “exceptional circumstances” for his failure to appear at the hearing. The respondent also did not comply with the procedural requirements for filing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as enunciated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Barry v. Gonzales, 445 F.3d 741 (4th Cir. 2006) finding no compliance with Lozada), cert. denied, 549 U.S. 1182 (2007). Accordingly, that issue is not before us.
 
*2 On appeal the respondent filed an affidavit from his wife stating that no notice of the hearing date for her husband came to their house by mail and that she and their two United States citizen children would suffer hardship, if he is removed. The respondent submitted an affidavit stating that he did not receive notice of the court date. The respondent’s attorney maintains in her affidavit that she did not receive notice of the hearing and that the hearing was not listed on the court’s telephonic calendar. She states that the Immigration Judge notified her in a phone conversation on the day of the hearing that the respondent had not appeared for his hearing, and subsequently the Immigration Judge issued an in absentia order of removal. She also alleges that his wife has naturalized and he is eligible for adjustment of status through consular processing.
 
We presume that a properly addressed notice sent via regular mail is received by the addressee. See Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665 (BIA 2008). The burden of proof for rebutting that presumption is weaker when items are sent by regular mail as opposed to certified mail. See Matter of M-R-A-, supra. According to the record, the respondent’s counsel was sent a notice of the hearing and that notice was not returned. The respondent’s attorney did not give an explanation of the circumstances which might lead to a failure to deliver the notice and which led to the in absentia hearing. The respondent also did not demonstrate that he is eligible for new relief which was not available at the time of the hearing. Section 240(c)(7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3); Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). He does not allege and it does not appear that he can adjust his status in the United States, even though his wife is a United States citizen. Section 245(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a). See Hussain v. Gonzales, 477 F.3d 153, 156-57 (4th Cir. 2007). Therefore, the Immigration Judge correctly denied the motion. The Board is not authorized to grant the respondent a stay of removal beyond the time necessary to reach a decision on the motion. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d), 1003.2(f).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

ORDER:
 
*1 The appeal will be dismissed. The record reflects that the respondent, through counsel, waived appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5. On February 14, 2013, the respondent nonetheless filed a Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) with the Board. While the respondent’s filing includes conclusory unsworn statements in English that he did not intelligently and voluntarily agree to voluntary departure and did not have a clear understanding of the consequences of voluntary departure,1 the respondent does not reference his counsel or comply with any of the requirements for raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Moreover, this is not a case in which any ineffective assistance by counsel is apparent on the face of the record. Consequently, upon review, we find that this case is not properly before us. Thus, the Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of the right to appeal through counsel. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a)(1) (final sentence), 1003.39; Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320 (BIA 2000); Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993). Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further Board action.
 
David B. Holmes

2013 WL 3899696 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: STANLEY AGUSTIN POLANCO A.K.A. STANLEY CRUZ-POLANCO A.K.A. PABLO RIVERA A.K.A. PABLO RIVERA-BALES
File: AXXX XX5 792 - Boston, MA
June 19, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey B. Rubin, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 26, 2013. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and found him ineligible for relief from removal based on his criminal conviction.1 On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent requests remand on the grounds that the conviction, which serves as the basis for removal, and which renders him ineligible for relief from removal, is being challenged before the criminal court pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010).2 The respondent alleges that he was not advised of the immigration consequences of his conviction, and he has filed a motion to vacate it. However, the conviction has not been vacated.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009). We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately considered the relevant factors to determine whether good cause for a continuance was shown. We conclude that good cause was not shown.
 
While the respondent is free to pursue the post-conviction motion, such a motion does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). The post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Removability and relief from removal are not at issue on appeal.

2

In Padilla v. Kentucky, the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel. See also Chaidez v. United States, 568 U.S. ___, 133 S.Ct. 1103 (2013) (holding that Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, which requires defense counsel to advise a defendant about the risk of deportation arising from a guilty plea, does not apply retroactively).

2013 WL 3899687 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MICHAEL MWANGI
File: AXXX XX1 886 - Buffalo, New York
June 19, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Olusegun Asekun, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Brandi M. Lohr
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Kenya, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s February 7, 2013, written decision which denied his second motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a brief in opposition. The appeal will be dismissed.1
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including the credibility determination, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues involving questions of law, judgment and discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in federal or state custody and failed to appear through no fault of her own. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C).
 
The respondent received personal service on February 24, 2012, of the Notice to Appear which bears his signature (Exh. 1). After he was released from immigration custody, a copy of a hearing notice dated March 16, 2012, which scheduled his hearing for September 12, 2012, was mailed to him. He has conceded that he was aware of his September 12, 2012, hearing.
 
Following a hearing conducted in absentia on September 12, 2012, at which the respondent failed to appear, an Immigration Judge found him subject to removal as charged, determined that he had abandoned all potential applications for relief, and ordered him removed from the United States. See generally section 240 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a. The in absentia removal order was mailed to the respondent and it was not returned by the postal service.
 
On October 5, 2012, the respondent, through attorney Sanjay Mathur, filed a motion to reopen which alleged that he had forgotten to appear on September 12, 2012, although his counsel had instructed him to appear unless a motion to change venue from Buffalo to Dallas, Texas had been granted. Attached to the motion was a signed and sworn 2-page affidavit from the respondent dated September 28, 2012, which indicated that he had consulted with Mr. Mathur in March 2012, that he formally retained him in August 2012, and that he forgot to appear for his hearing on September 12, 2012.
 
*2 On October 29, 2012, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen because the respondent’s failure to appear based on forgetting his hearing did not constitute an exceptional circumstance. He also declined to reopen the respondent’s case on his own authority. The respondent did not appeal this decision and it is now final.
 
On December 26, 2012, the respondent, through new counsel, filed a second motion to reopen. He now alleged that his previous attorney had falsely informed him that he had filed a motion to change venue. The respondent also submitted an affidavit dated December 16, 2012, in which he alleged that Mr. Mathur told him he had filed a motion to change venue. Respondent believed he did not need to appear in Buffalo on September 12, 2012. The respondent alleged that after he signed the affidavit on September 28, 2012, Mr. Mathur changed the text on the first page to indicate that Mr. Mathur had instructed him of the necessity of appearing on September 12, 2012, but that he failed to appear for his hearing because he had forgotten his attorney’s instructions. He attached a copy of a bar complaint he filed with state authorities on December 10, 2012, along with a copy of his retainer agreement with Mr. Mathur dated August 14, 2012.
 
The DHS filed a response in opposition, noting that it was the respondent’s second motion to reopen which was number barred, that he only filed a bar complaint after the denial of his first motion, and that he had not provided Mr. Mathur with an opportunity to respond to the bar complaint prior to filing the motion to reopen because he filed the motion very soon after filing the bar complaint.
 
On February 7, 2013, the Immigration Judge denied the second motion to reopen because the alleged ineffective assistance of his prior attorney was not newly discovered evidence insofar as it existed at the time the respondent filed the first motion, he failed to appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his first motion, which meant that it was a final order, and he was limited to one motion to reopen.
 
We will affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision.2 The respondent has failed to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), to establish ineffective assistance of counsel because he has not indicated whether a response has been received by Mr. Mathur or whether he has declined to respond. Furthermore, he conceded that he was aware of his obligation to attend the September 2012 hearing and he has not alleged in his second affidavit that Mr. Mathur told him not to attend the hearing. Therefore, he has not established ineffective assistance on this record.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s counsel indicated in his appeal brief that the respondent was removed from the United States on March 25, 2013. Therefore, his motion for a stay of removal is moot.

2

We acknowledge receipt on May 13, 2013, of a copy of an approval notice for the respondent’s immediate relative petition forwarded by counsel.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OLUTAYO SHOLANA
File: AXXX XX0 408 - Chicago, IL
June 17, 2013
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Larry E. Adkison, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kristin Musto
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)] - Entered U.S. without inspection
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Nigeria, appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 18, 2011, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen deportation proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was granted voluntary departure in lieu of deportation on January 10, 1997, and waived appeal (I.J. at 1). On April 3, 1997, the respondent filed a timely motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge based on ineffective assistance of counsel, which was denied on April 22, 1997. The respondent appealed the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion and the Board dismissed his appeal on December 14, 2001, finding he did not comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and he did not demonstrate that he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s alleged errors (BIA at 1-2). The respondent filed the present motion on March 15, 2011 with the Immigration Judge, alleging the same basis for reopening: namely, ineffective assistance of counsel of his first attorney as well as the attorney who filed an appeal with the Board and a subsequent attorney who was allegedly retained approximately 7 years after the respondent was ordered deported.
 
The respondent does not dispute the fact that his motion is untimely. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) (a motion to reopen any case previously the subject of a final administrative order must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision). Rather, the respondent argues that his untimely filing should be equitably tolled because he received ineffective assistance of former counsel(s). But to benefit from equitable tolling, a respondent must show that he exercised due diligence and that he could not have reasonably been expected to file his motion earlier. See El-Gazawy v. Holder, 690 F.3d 852, 859 (7th Cir. 2012); Johnson v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d 795, 799 (7th Cir. 2007) (stating that “[i]t is a rare occasion that we reverse a decision on the basis that a time limit should have been equitably tolled . . . it must be shown that the claimant could not have been expected to file earlier.”).
 
*2 Assuming that the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not demonstrated that equitable tolling applies, as he has not shown due diligence in pursuing his claim (I.J. at 2). As noted by the Immigration Judge, the respondent was aware of the possibility of ineffective assistance, as his second attorney raised this issue in her brief before the Board in August 1997 (I.J. at 2). The Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal in 2001, and the respondent has offered no reasonable explanation for waiting more than 9 years from the date of the Board’s final decision in this case to file the present motion. Thus, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not demonstrated that he acted with due diligence in pursuing his alleged ineffective assistance of counsel claim.1
 
The record reflects that the respondent is the beneficiary of an approved visa petition, has been married to a United States citizen since January 1997, and has a child with his wife (Respondent’s March 15, 2011 Motion, tabs A, C, D). Moreover, the respondent has submitted medical records that indicate that he has been diagnosed with serious cardiovascular issues (Respondent’s March 15, 2011 Motion, tab U). To the extent that the respondent requests sua sponte reopening to apply for adjustment of status based on exceptional circumstances, we note that becoming eligible for relief from deportation after a final administrative order has been entered, as the respondent has done, is common and does not, in itself, constitute an exceptional circumstance warranting our consideration of an untimely motion. To hold otherwise would vitiate the statutory and regulatory deadlines, which are designed to bring finality to immigration proceedings. INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992) (motions to reopen are especially disfavored in immigration proceedings because every delay works to the advantage of the deportable alien).
 
Moreover, we note that even assuming the respondent presents exceptional circumstances, we would decline to exercise our sua sponte authority, as the record does not indicate that the respondent is statutorily eligible for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a), as he conceded that he entered without inspection (Exh. 1; Tr. at 11-12). Section 245(a) of the Act. Moreover, the record also does not indicate that the respondent is statutorily eligible for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act, because although the respondent claims that he is grandfathered under section 245(i) of the Act, the I-130 in the record reflects a priority date of July 5, 2009, and no other visa petition appears in the administrative record (Respondent’s March 15, 2011 Motion at tabs B & E). See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.10(a)(1), (3). As such, we conclude that the circumstances that the respondent described would not warrant reopening on a sua sponte basis. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KAREN LOUISE DIFEO
File: AXXX XX3 925 - Miami, FL
June 14, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marcela Gyires, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Patricia B. Kelly Le Bienvenu
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; termination of proceedings
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Canada, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 26, 2012. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and found her ineligible for relief from removal based on her criminal convictions.1 On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of a continuance and denial of a request to terminate removal proceedings. The record will be remanded.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Before the Immigration Judge, the respondent requested continuances to enable her to challenge her criminal convictions before the criminal court pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010)2 and to pursue termination of proceedings through prosecutorial discretion by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). The Immigration Judge granted continuances from May 19, 2011, until January 26, 2012, but declined to grant a further continuance. The respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in denying a further continuance. However, the respondent does not indicate whether the convictions have been vacated.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009).3 We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately considered the relevant factors to determine whether good cause for a continuance was shown. We conclude that good cause was not shown. While the respondent is free to pursue a collateral attack on the convictions, such a motion does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). A post-conviction motion to vacate convictions does not render the convictions non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
*2 Also at the January 26, 2012, hearing, the respondent requested termination of proceedings to pursue naturalization. The Immigration Judge denied that request. The respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in denying the motion to terminate proceedings to allow the respondent to naturalize. She argues that she is in a regulatory catch-22, because the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS), a part of the DHS, will not adjudicate her naturalization application while she is in removal proceedings, and the Immigration Judge will not terminate proceedings without an acknowledgment of facie eligibility for naturalization from the USCIS. The DHS responds that the Immigration Judge cannot terminate proceedings without an affirmative communication of prima facie eligibility from the USCIS and that neither the Immigration Judge nor this Board can compel the USCIS to respond to a request for such an affirmative communication. The DHS also points out that the respondent submitted the request for acknowledgment of prima facie eligibility for naturalization to the Immigration and Customs Enforcement (ICE), also a part of DHS, instead of USCIS, and the request was made only a few days before the scheduled hearing.
 
The Board of Immigration Appeals and Immigration Judges lack jurisdiction to adjudicate applications for naturalization. In order to pursue naturalization, the respondent must file a Form N-400, along with all relevant evidence, with the USCIS. Generally, an Immigration Judge cannot terminate proceedings to allow an alien to pursue that process. An Immigration Judge can only terminate proceedings when an alien has “established prima facie eligibility for naturalization” and the matter “involves exceptionally appealing or humanitarian factors.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1239.2(f). We decline to reach the respondent’s argument that we should revisit our holding in Matter of Acosta-Hidalgo, 24 I&N Dec. 103 (BIA 2007), in this case where the Immigration Judge has not addressed whether the respondent’s case “involves exceptionally appealing or humanitarian factors.” We find it appropriate to remand the record to the Immigration Judge to address, in the first instance, whether the “““exceptionally appealing or humanitarian factors” prong of § 1239.2(f) is satisfied.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and entry of a new decision.
 
Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Removability and relief from removal are not at issue on appeal.

2

In Padilla v. Kentucky, the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel. See also Chaidez v. United States, 568 U.S. ___, 133 S.Ct. 1103 (2013) (holding that Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, which requires defense counsel to advise a defendant about the risk of deportation arising from a guilty plea, does not apply retroactively).

3

In determining whether good cause exists to continue removal proceedings, a variety of factors may be considered, including, but not limited to: the Department of Homeland Security’s response to the motion to continue and the reason for the continuance and any other relevant procedural factors. See Matter of Hashmi, supra.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YASMIN PUGA-MEZA
File: AXXX XX8 060 - Oklahoma City, OK
June 13, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Margaret M. Price
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed an Immigration Judge’s January 24, 2012, decision which granted the respondent “pre-conclusion” voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a). The respondent now maintains that she was provided ineffective assistance of counsel and requests a remand. The appeal will be dismissed.1
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error; but we review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals before us, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
To be eligible for pre-conclusion voluntary departure, a respondent must concede removability and waive any right to appeal that decision. Section 240B(a) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i)(D); see also Matter of Ocampo, 22 I&N Dec. 1301 (BIA 2000).
 
The respondent’s removability is not in dispute. The Immigration Judge granted the respondent voluntary departure in lieu of having a full hearing on the merits, as the respondent indicated that she understood and sought this form of relief (Tr. at 12-13). Although the respondent may have been uncertain, at first, about the requirements to obtain this form of voluntary departure, she then specifically indicated that she was asking for this relief, accepted that the Immigration Judge’s decision would be final, and agreed to waive appeal (Tr. at 12-14). She stated that she understood that accepting the Immigration Judge’s decision meant she had to prepare to leave the country (Tr. at 13-14).
 
On appeal, the respondent now maintains that she wished to pursue other unstated relief. She avers that because of a fee dispute with her former attorney, the attorney improperly acceded to voluntary departure for the respondent in addition to waiving appeal. We note, however, that the respondent does not directly dispute that she, herself, agreed to depart voluntarily and to the appeal waiver (Tr. at 12-13). Although she asserts she does not speak English, she did not--and has not--objected to the translation which was provided at the hearing. In light of the respondent’s direct answers to the Immigration Judge on these issues, we find that the respondent’s waiver of appeal was knowing and intelligent.2 See Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320, 1322 (BIA 2000).
 
*2 The respondent has also not attempted to explain in any meaningful detail the nature of her alleged dispute with her former attorney, including what the attorney agreed to do. While we recognize that the respondent is now appearing pro se, to successfully show ineffective assistance of counsel, she would need to specify the nature of her agreement with former counsel, describing in detail what representations counsel may have made. She would also generally be expected to file a complaint with the attorney’s licensing authority or explain why she has not done so. Additionally, the attorney must be afforded an opportunity to respond to her allegations. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent has not attempted to meet any of these criteria for showing ineffective assistance of counsel. Moreover, the respondent has not specified what other relief counsel was to have sought for her, nor whether she may actually be eligible for any other relief. Thus, she has not shown that she was prejudiced by her former counsel’s actions. See, e.g., Ochieng v. Mukasey, 520 F.3d 1110, 1115 (10th Cir. 2008) (showing of prejudice necessary to succeed on ineffective assistance of counsel claim). Consequently, remanding the record would serve no apparent purpose.
 
In light of the foregoing, the Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of her right to appeal. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a)(1) (final sentence), 1003.39 (finality of order); Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, supra; Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993). As the respondent waived appeal, we have no further jurisdiction in this matter. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action.
 
Elise Manuel
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s fee-waiver request is granted. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8.

2

We acknowledge that initially the respondent may have been confused about what pre-conclusion voluntary departure entails (Tr. at 12). The Immigration Judge, however, clarified to the respondent the nature of this relief and explained the finality of the decision he made (Tr. at 13-14).

IN RE: MARIA THEN A.K.A. MARIA INMACULADA THEN

June 13, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marilyn Labrada Dume, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, mailed August 24, 2012, denying her timely motion to reopen. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent seeks to reopen these removal proceedings to pursue an application for adjustment of status of status under section 245(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i). For the reasons set forth below, we conclude that the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion because she has not demonstrated that she is prima facie eligible for adjustment of status (I.J. at 1). See INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988); Sevoian v. Ashcroft, 290 F.3d 166, 170, 174 (3d Cir. 2002). In particular, the respondent has not demonstrated that she is a “grandfathered alien” for purposes of section 245(i) of the Act. See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.10(a)(1)(i).
 
The respondent has presented evidence that her biological father was admitted to this country as an “F-24” lawful permanent resident (unmarried son or daughter (21 years of age or older) of a lawful permanent resident alien) in 1992 (Respondent’s Motion at 6, 9). See United States Citizenship and Immigration Services Adjudicator’s Field Manual, Appendix 23-7: Class of Admission under the Immigrant Laws, available online at http:// www.uscis.gov/ilink/docView/AFM/HTML/AFM/0-0-0-1.html. While not clear, the admission of the respondent’s biological father as an F-24 in 1992 tends to indicate that he was the beneficiary of an immigrant visa petition filed on or before April 30, 2001. However, the respondent has not demonstrated that, within the meaning of section 101(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(b)(1), she was considered a derivative beneficiary “child” of the immigrant visa petition. For example, she does not claim that she was born in wedlock, legitimated in accordance with Dominican law, or, prior to her 21st birthday, shared a bona fide father / child relationship with her biological father. See sections 101(b)(1)(A), (C), (D) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 204.2(d)(2); Matter of Cabrera, 21 I&N Dec. 589, 591-92 (1996) (“Thus, a child residing or domiciled in the Dominican Republic may qualify as a legitimated child under section 101(b)(1)(C) [of the Act] as soon as his father acknowledges paternity in accordance with Dominican law.”); Matter of Vizcaino, 19 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1988); Matter of Annang, 14 I&N Dec. 502 (BIA 1973) (holding that the content of foreign law is a question of fact that must be proven by the party relying on it).
 
*2 To the extent that the respondent claims that the attorney who represented her before the Immigration Judge provided her with ineffective assistance of counsel by failing to address her eligibility for adjustment of status, we observe that the respondent has not complied with the evidentiary requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), nor established that she was prejudiced by her former counsel’s handling of this matter. Accordingly, we will not disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. The following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Elise Manuel
FOR THE BOARD

APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the February 21, 2012, decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s motion to reopen as untimely. An Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent’s removal from the United States, on October 7, 2011. The respondent filed an untimely motion to reopen on February 3, 2012, but he raised claims of ineffective assistance of counsel and equitable tolling. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen as untimely without addressing either of these issues. We find a remand is warranted to allow the Immigration Judge to address the issues of ineffective assistance of counsel and equitable tolling in light of applicable case law and determine if the 90-day deadline should be equitably tolled. Accordingly, the record will be remanded for further proceedings and the entry of a new decision.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings and the entry of a new decision consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Edward R. Grant

2013 WL 3200575 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PASCUAL LUCIANO TOMAS-GASPAR
File: AXXX XX4 779 - San Diego, CA
June 10, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
John David Mansfield, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
J.L. Woodmansee
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on April 11, 2002, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider and reopen. On April 15, 2013, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order entered against him. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) filed a brief opposing the respondent’s motion, and, on June 3, 2013, the respondent filed a response to the Department of Homeland Security’s opposition. The respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind will be denied.
  
I. MOTION TO RESCIND
 
Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C), states that an in absentia order may be rescinded “upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances (as defined in subsection (e)(1)).”1 The Immigration Judge entered an in absentia order of removal against the respondent on October 11, 2000. The respondent, however, did not file his current motion to rescind until 12 ½ years later. The respondent’s motion to rescind based on exceptional circumstances therefore is untimely.
 
In addition, the regulations pertaining to the filing of motions to rescind in absentia orders entered in removal proceedings state that an alien may only file one motion to rescind. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The respondent filed a motion to rescind with the Immigration Judge on January 8, 2001. The Immigration Judge denied this motion on February 26, 2001, and we upheld the Immigration Judge’s decision on August 22, 2001. The respondent’s current motion to rescind therefore is the respondent’s second motion of this type and is numerically barred.
 
The respondent claims in his motion that the time and numeric limits for filing motions to rescind should be equitably tolled because he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. He contends that his first attorney, Thomas Mix, did not remind him of his hearing on October 11, 2000, and therefore was responsible for his failure to appear. The respondent also contends that his attempts to rectify and explain this situation were compromised by the ineffective representation of his subsequent attorney, Manuel Gonzalez. The respondent has submitted copies of complaints that he claims to have filed against each of these attorneys with the relevant disciplinary authorities. The respondent also has submitted a declaration and a supplemental declaration, documentation pertaining to his eligibility for adjustment of status, and the prior rulings in his case.
 
*2 The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the circuit in which the respondent’s case arises, has held that equitable tolling of the time limit for a motion to reopen is available “when a petitioner is prevented from filing because of deception, fraud, or error, as long as the petitioner acts with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error.” See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897-98 (9th Cir. 2003); see also Fajardo v. INS, 300 F.3d 1018 (9th Cir. 2002) (stating that the 180-day deadline for filing a motion to rescind an in absentia order based on a claim of exceptional circumstances can be equitably tolled due to ineffective assistance of an attorney or a non-attorney). The Ninth Circuit further has indicated that it considered three issues in determining whether an alien exercised due diligence: (1) if and when a reasonable person in the alien’s position would suspect the specific fraud or error underlying her petition, (2) whether the alien took reasonable steps to investigate the suspected fraud or error or, if the alien was unaware of the error, to pursue relief, and (3) when the alien definitively learned of the harm resulting from counsel’s deficiency. Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672, 680 (9th Cir. 2011).
 
In this case, the respondent has not presented sufficient evidence to establish that he is entitled to equitable tolling of the time or numeric limits on his motion to rescind. In particular, the respondent has not met the procedural requirements necessary for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, he has not presented sufficient evidence to establish that ineffective assistance of counsel caused the entry of the in absentia order against him or prevented him from obtaining rescission, and he has not established that he has acted diligently in discovering any ineffective assistance or in pursuing relief.
  
A. Matter of Lozada Requirements
 
To obtain reopening on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must meet certain procedural requirements. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). These requirements also are applicable to the respondent’s situation. Cf. Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec 472 (BIA 1996) (finding that, under former statute, alien met exceptional circumstances requirement when he established that his failure to appear was due to ineffective assistance of counsel). The respondent, however, has not met these requirements for either of his former attorneys.
 
The respondent has discussed his arrangement with Mr. Mix in the declaration he submitted with his motion, and he has submitted a bar complaint against Mr. Mix which he claims to have filed with the relevant authorities. Nevertheless, the respondent has not offered evidence to establish that he has notified Mr. Mix of the charges against him or explained why he is unable to do so.2
 
*3 Further, the respondent has not presented an affidavit setting forth his agreement with his second attorney, Mr. Gonzalez. The bar complaint the respondent claims to have filed against Mr. Gonzalez contains vague statements regarding their agreement, and the record contains an entry of appearance Mr. Gonzalez filed with the DHS in 2006, but this information is not sufficient to establish that the respondent retained Mr. Gonzalez to represent him for any purpose before the immigration courts or this Board. See, e.g., Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004) (finding that erroneous advice received outside and in advance of immigration proceedings did not affect fairness of proceedings and did not provide basis for reopening due to ineffective assistance of counsel); cf. Morales Apolinar v. Mukasey, 514 F.3d 893, 896 (9th Cir. 2008) (finding attorney-client arrangement evident from on the record appearances). In addition, the respondent has not presented evidence to indicate that he has attempted to notify Mr. Gonzalez of the charges he is making against him.3 The respondent therefore has not submitted sufficient evidence to establish that he has met the procedural requirements necessary to make a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
  
B. Ineffective Assistance
 
Moreover, even if we were to conclude that the respondent has substantially complied with Matter of Lozada, supra, the respondent has not submitted sufficient evidence to establish that the ineffective assistance of his former attorneys led to the entry of the in absentia order or his inability to obtain rescission of this order. The respondent now claims that he did not appear at his hearing on October 11, 2000, because Mr Mix’s office did not remind him of his hearing date.4 This claim, however, conflicts with the statement he submitted with a motion to reopen and reconsider filed with this Board on October 9, 2001. In that statement, the respondent said that he did not appear because he mistakenly believed that his hearing was the following week and his father did not remind him of the correct hearing date until it was too late for him to appear in court.
 
The respondent has attempted to explain the discrepancies in his statements by submitting an additional declaration. See Respondent’s Reply to DHS’ Opposition to Respondent’s Motion To Reopen Removal Proceedings. In this most recent statement, the respondent claims that he did not read the statement that Mr. Mix’ assistant gave him to sign in 2001, and he appears to allege that the assistant was trying to shift the blame for respondent’s non-appearance from herself to the respondent’s father.
 
Even if we accept the respondent’s most recent statement as true, the record shows that the respondent received both oral and written notice of his hearing date and of the consequences for failing to appear from the Immigration Judge (I.J. 2/26/01 at 6). The respondent therefore had sufficient information to allow him to appear in court as required, and he bears some responsibility for not appearing or for not contacting his attorney to ask if he was required to appear. The information of record is not sufficient to establish that the oversight or error of Mr Mix’s office amounted to ineffective assistance.
 
*4 In addition, the respondent has not provided sufficient information regarding the duties Mr. Gonzalez owed to him to establish that any errors on the part of Mr. Gonzalez constituted ineffective assistance. The bar complaints the respondent has prepared against Mr. Mix and Mr. Gonzalez indicate that the respondent only worked with Mr. Gonzalez from 2006 to 2007, and they do not contain sufficient information to establish what Mr. Gonzalez was supposed to accomplish for the respondent during that time. The respondent therefore has not met his burden of establishing that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel in his immigration proceedings.
  
C. Due Diligence
 
Finally, even if we were to conclude that the respondent had met the Lozada requirements and established that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel that affected the outcome of his proceedings, the respondent has not addressed the issue of due diligence in his motion. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra (indicating that, to obtain equitable tolling of a filing deadline, an alien must act with due diligence in discovering the fraud or error that led to missing the deadline). The respondent states in his March 11, 2013, declaration that Mr. Mix called him on October 11, 2000, after he failed to appear for the hearing, but he does not state what steps he then took to address the alleged error on the part of this attorney or to pursue relief. Further, the record shows that Mr. Mix filed a motion to rescind with the Immigration Judge on January 9, 2001, and that the Immigration Judge denied the motion on February 26, 2001. Mr. Mix appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision and then filed a motion to reconsider with this Board. The documents that Mr. Mix submitted to the Immigration Judge and this Board indicated that the respondent had failed to appear due to a mistake on the respondent’s part. At some point during this process, the respondent should have become aware of the representations Mr. Mix was making on his behalf. The respondent, however, has not explained adequately why he waited until 2013 to file a motion to rescind based on the ineffective assistance of Mr. Mix and Mr. Gonzalez. The respondent therefore has not met his burden of establishing that he is entitled to equitable tolling of the deadline for filing a motion to rescind based on exceptional circumstances.
  
II. SUA SPONTE REOPENING
 
We further find that the respondent has not presented sufficient evidence to establish that reopening pursuant to our sua sponte authority is appropriate. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that our discretion to reopen sua sponte is limited to cases where exceptional circumstances are demonstrated). Due to the passage of time, the respondent now is eligible for an exception to the inadmissibility ground contained in section 212(a)(9)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(9)(C)(i). See section 212(a)(9)(C)(ii) of the Act. The respondent, however, has not established that the Secretary of Homeland Security has consented to his reapplying for admission. Id. The respondent therefore remains statutorily ineligible for adjustment of status, the relief he is seeking in reopened proceedings, and reopening to allow him to pursue this relief is not warranted. Cf. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471-73 (BIA 1992) (indicating that motions to reopen are disfavored and that this Board may deny reopening when an alien has not established prima facie eligibility for the relief he is seeking). We therefore decline to reopen the respondent’s proceedings pursuant to our sua sponte authority.
 
*5 ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent has not claimed a lack of notice, and the Immigration Judge found that the respondent received both oral and written notice of the hearing at which he failed to appear (I.J. 2/26/01 at 6).

2

The respondent then states that, because he did not receive a reminder, he reasoned that his hearing must have been for a later date. 2013 WL 3200525 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DEVON BRIAN BROWN
File: AXXX XX8 697 - York, PA
June 10, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jeffrey T. Bubier
Senior Attorney
*1 The respondent’s motion to reopen filed on April 29, 2013, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”), will be denied.
 
The Board issued a final order in this case on November 26, 2012, and denied a motion to reopen on April 8, 2013. The pending motion is untimely and barred by numerical limitations for motions to reopen. Sections 240(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A) & (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The Board could only reopen proceedings under its sua sponte authority at 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(a). This action is taken only in “exceptional situations”, which are not presented here. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
The respondent was convicted in 2011 of possession with intent to deliver marijuana. The respondent contends that this conviction is not final. He attaches a “Pre-Trial Order of Court-Criminal”, which suggests that the respondent’s appellate rights were reinstated in that case. However, the respondent argued before the Immigration Judge that he was appealing that conviction (Tr. at 7). As the Immigration Judge properly considered, the respondent was nevertheless also convicted in March, 2000, for delivery or possession with intent to deliver cocaine, a drug trafficking aggravated felony (Tr. at 7-8). See also I.J. at 2; DHS Opp.; section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act.
 
The respondent also argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel concerning criminal cases, and cites Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356 (2010). The ruling in Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, does not change the finality of a conviction for immigration purposes, unless and until it is overturned by a criminal court. See Matter of Ponce de Leon, 21 I&N Dec. 154, 156-67 (A.G. 1997; BIA 1997, 1996). Moreover, the Supreme Court has concluded that Padilla is not entitled to retroactive application. See Chaidez v. United States, 133 S.Ct. 1103 (2013).
 
The respondent also argues that he did not receive “timely notice of the dismissal decision” from the Board. Yet the Board promptly mailed both of its prior decisions to the respondent at the address where he is still located, and the decisions were not returned as undeliverable.
 
*2 The motion to reopen is, therefore, denied, and the request for a stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

2013 WL 2608334 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SERGIO IBARRA-AMAYA
File: AXXX XX6 122 - El Paso, TX
May 28, 2013
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Hakeem Ishola, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jaime Diaz
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(D)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(D)(i)] - Failure to appear for an interview to remove conditional basis of alien lawfully admitted for permanent residence on a conditional basis
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; stay of removal
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 31, 2010. In his decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) filed a motion for summary affirmance. The appeal will be dismissed, and the stay will be denied.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii). The respondent’s case is not governed by the provisions of the REAL ID Act. See Matter of S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 42 (BIA 2006).
 
The procedural history in the present case is as follows. The respondent was served with an Order to Show Cause (Form I-862) on May 29, 1992 (Exh. 1). On June 8, 1993, a Notice of Hearing was mailed via certified mail to the address the respondent provided to the government upon his release from detention. The Notice of Hearing informed the respondent that his hearing was scheduled for July 29, 1993 (Exh. 2). The respondent’s sister signed the domestic return receipt on June 9, 1993 (Exh. 2). On July 27, 1993, the respondent filed a change of address form and a letter requesting postponement of his hearing date and a change of venue to Salt Lake City, Utah (Exh. 2A). On August 2, 1993, the Immigration Judge issued a decision denying the respondent’s request for a continuance and to change venue. She further ordered the respondent deported in absentia.
 
On October 26, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to rescind his in absentia deportation order. In it, he alleged lack of notice and exceptional circumstances in that he received ineffective assistance from a notario who held himself out to be a lawyer. The DHS filed a memorandum in opposition. On March 31, 2010, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. He concluded the respondent received proper notice of his hearing. Regarding the “exceptional circumstances” issue, the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent’s motion was untimely and that the Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit (the “Fifth Circuit”) does not recognize the doctrine of equitable tolling in such circumstances. The respondent timely appealed to this Board.
 
*2 We concur with the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. Contrary to the respondent’s assertion on appeal, the Immigration Judge properly applied section 242B of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b, to the respondent’s motion to reopen because the applicable Notice of Hearing was issued on or about June 8, 1993, approximately 1 year after section 242B of the Act became effective (Resp.’s Br. at 2-3; Exh. 2). See Magdaleno de Morales v. INS, 116 F.3d 145, fn. 4 (5th Cir. 1997) (The amended statute applies if notice of the hearing was provided after June 13, 1992); see also Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I & N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993); Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503, fn. 2 (BIA 1996) (Section 242B of the Act applies to a deportation hearing where notice of the scheduled hearing date is issued on or after June 13, 1992).
 
Moreover, notwithstanding the respondent’s assertions to the contrary, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s decision finding that the respondent received proper notice of his deportation hearing (Resp.’s Br. at 8-11). See generally Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27, 32 (BIA 1995) (discussing notice requirements for Orders to Show Cause (OSC) governed by section 242B of the Act). Nor do we find clear error in the factual findings leading up to that determination. The respondent’s Notice of Hearing was mailed via certified mail to the address he provided to the government upon his release from detention, and his sister signed the domestic return receipt. See generally Maknojiya v. Gonzales, 432 F.3d 588, 589 (5th Cir. 2005) (when the notice is sent by certified mail through the United States Postal Service and there is proof of attempted delivery and notification of certified mail, a strong presumption of effective service arises that may be overcome only by the affirmative defense of nondelivery or improper delivery by the Postal Service); see also Matter of Grijalva, supra, and Matter of M-D-, 23 I&N Dec. 540 (BIA 2002). The respondent did not file his change of address form until after the notice of hearing was mailed to him, and he clearly had notice of his hearing because he acknowledged the date of his hearing in his request for a continuance and to change venue (Exh. 2A). We disagree with the respondent’s assertion that the Immigration Judge should have delayed proceedings and issued a new notice of hearing to the respondent’s new address, especially since the respondent did not file such request until 2 days before his scheduled hearing (Resp.’s Br. at 11).
 
*3 The respondent also received proper notice advising him of the consequences of his failure to appear (Resp.’s Br. at 11-14).1 Both the respondent’s Order to Show Cause and the Change of Address Form (Form EOIR-33) he received upon release from detention contained this information (Exh. 1). There is no requirement that the advisals be made orally or in person (Resp.’s Br. at 11-14). Thus, the respondent was properly advised of the consequences of failing to appear.
 
Additionally, we disagree with the respondent’s assertion that his request for continuance and change of venue filed 2 days before his hearing vitiates his in absentia hearing (Resp.’s Br. at 12-14). The respondent’s request for continuance and change of venue did not relieve him of his obligation to attend his deportation hearing. Until the respondent’s request for continuance and change of venue was decided, he was required to attend his scheduled hearing in El Paso. See Magdaleno de Morales v. INS, supra; see also Patel v. US INS, 803 F.2d 804, 806 (5th Cir. 1986) (the mere submission of a motion for continuance does not relieve an alien or his counsel of the obligation to appear for a noticed hearing). As the Immigration Judge properly determined in her in absentia order, the respondent chose to move from El Paso, was aware of his scheduled hearing, and chose not to appear (I.J. order dated 08/02/93 at 4). Patel v. US INS, supra. The Immigration Judge’s in absentia order was proper.
 
We further disagree with the respondent’s assertion that his case should be reopened based on exceptional circumstances, in that his notario, who he claims falsely held himself out to be an attorney, improperly told him that he did not need to attend his deportation hearing (Resp.’s Br. at 15-24). Former section 242B(c)(3) of the Act provides that an in absentia order will be rescinded upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days of the order if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances. We do not agree with the respondent’s assertion that his notario’s incorrect advice constituted an exceptional circumstance. However, even if we did, the respondent waited more than 16 years to file his motion to reopen. Therefore, his motion is untimely. See section 242B(c)(3) of the Act.
 
The respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge erred by not equitably tolling the 180-day deadline (Resp.’s Br. at 18, 24-25). We disagree. The Immigration Judge correctly determined that the Fifth Circuit has not adopted the doctrine of equitable tolling in this context. See generally Ramos-Bonilla v. Mukasey, 543 F.3d 216, 218 (5th Cir. 2008). We further find insufficient reason to reopen the respondent’s case pursuant to our sua sponte discretionary authority. Id. (alien assertion that he was entitled to equitable tolling of the time and number limitations that applied to motions to reopen because of ineffective assistance of counsel was equivalent to claim that the Board should have exercised its sua sponte discretion to reopen); see also Rodriguez-Manzano v. Holder, 666 F.3d 948 (5th Cir. 2012); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
*4 Turning to the respondent’s request to reopen deportation proceedings to pursue suspension of deportation, we disagree with his assertion that he is eligible for suspension of deportation under former section 244 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1254, in accordance with our holding in Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998), despite the fact that he did not receive oral warning for the consequences of failing to appear for a deportation proceeding (Resp.’s Br. at 25-27). In that regard, we have recently held that the 90-day time limitation for filing a motion to reopen in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) applies to motions to reopen in absentia deportation orders, whether filed before or after the 1996 promulgation of the regulations; we further held that the 5-year limitation on discretionary relief for failure to appear at deportation proceedings under former section 242B(e)(1) of the Act is not in conflict with, and does not provide an exception to, the 90-day deadline for filing a motion to reopen in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). See Matter of Monges-Garcia, 25 I&N Dec. 246 (BIA 2010). As previously noted, the respondent waited more than 16 years to file his motion to reopen. Therefore, his motion is untimely. Id.; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).
 
Finally, we decline to reopen the respondent’s proceedings for “repapering” (Resp.’s Br. at 30-32). Repapering is not within our authority and is not warranted in any event in part for reasons that sua sponte reopening is not warranted.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed, and his stay is denied.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent cites Matter of G-Y-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 181 (BIA 2001), to support his assertion that he did not receive proper notice advising him of the consequences of failing to appear. We note that Matter of G-Y-R- applies to removal proceedings whereas the respondent is in deportation proceedings. However, even if we applied the reasoning contained in Matter of G-Y-R- to the facts contained in the present case, it would not alter the outcome of the case.

2013 WL 2608360 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BENJAMIN ROQUE-SABINO
File: AXXX XX2 383 - Memphis, TN
May 22, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jason A. Flora, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
William A. Lund
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 5, 2012, denying his motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for his failure to appear for the hearing on October 18, 2011. On appeal, the respondent concedes that he received legally sufficient notice of his hearing, as notice was served on his attorney of record, but contends that his failure to appear was due to established exceptional circumstances, the ineffective assistance of his former counsel. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reopen for failure to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (I.J. at 3). The respondent cites to decisions from the United States Court of Appeals for the First and Ninth Circuits in support of his assertion that the proceedings should be reopened despite his failure to comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra. However, such case law is not controlling in the instant matter, which arises in the United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit; rather, Lozada continues to be controlling. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not met the evidentiary requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and he has failed to establish “exceptional circumstances” for his failure to appear (I.J. at 3). See Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996) (holding that an alien seeking to reopen in absentia deportation proceedings based on her unsuccessful communications with her attorney and her alleged non-receipt of notification from her attorney regarding the hearing date did not establish “exceptional circumstances” where she failed to satisfy all of the requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim set out in Matter of Lozada, supra).
 
Furthermore, we are unpersuaded by the respondent’s assertion that the Immigration Judge erred in not allowing him to supplement his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel before denying his motion to reopen. We note that the motion did not indicate that the respondent intended to file any supplemental evidence, nor has such evidence been submitted on appeal.
 
*2 Finally, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in not reopening his proceedings sua sponte. However, we conclude that the respondent has not established that sua sponte reopening is warranted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Even accepting the respondent’s assertion that his former attorney did not provide him with actual notice of his hearing date, upon consideration of the record as a whole, we hold that he has presented insufficient evidence of circumstances that would justify a discretionary grant of his untimely motion to reopen and the extraordinary remedy of reopening his removal proceedings sua sponte. See, e.g., Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (discretion to reopen a case sua sponte is “an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (sua sponte reopening is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Elise L. Manuel
FOR THE BOARD
2013 WL 2608360 (BIA)
End of Document
