2013 WL 2608364 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SIRPRUD VILLAN
File: AXXX XX0 693 - Los Angeles, CA
May 22, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Russell L. Marshak, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of the Philippines, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 15, 2012, denying his motion to reopen his proceeding.1 The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the Immigration Judge’s determination of credibility. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s motion. Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994). The respondent was granted pre-conclusion voluntary departure on November 17, 2011. On February 14, 2012, the respondent filed a motion to reopen to allow him to seek adjustment of status. The respondent contends that he would not have accepted voluntary departure if his former attorneys had informed him that he could adjust his status by continuing to take and by passing the English language test required for a visa in his field or by seeking a new Form I-140 in a non-healthcare-related field. Respondent’s application for adjustment of status has been denied three times for lack of a Visa Screen certification because he has not passed the English language exam. Respondent had not taken the test for several years before his hearing, and has not submitted any evidence that he has passed the exam. In making his apparent ineffective assistance of counsel claim, the respondent has not substantially complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Singh v. Holder, 658 F.3d 879, 884 (9th Cir. 2011).
 
Even if, as the respondent contends, we found that the record sufficiently established the respondent’s claim for ineffective assistance without meeting the requirements of Matter of Lozada, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion as he has not demonstrated prima facie eligibility for the relief sought (I.J. at 3). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1); see also Lin v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 1014, 1027 (9th Cir. 2004); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). The respondent has not presented an application for adjustment of status or an approved visa petition. The respondent’s claim that he could adjust his status through an, as yet unfiled, visa petition from a different employer is purely speculative and insufficient to warrant reopening his proceedings. We further reject the respondent’s contention that the Immigration Judge ignored the ““““primary basis” for his motion to reopen, that his lack of knowledge of his ““““alternative rights and remedies” prevented him from providing a knowing and intelligent waiver of his appeal rights in accepting pre-conclusion voluntary departure (Br. at 17-19). See section 240B(a) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b). As the respondent was represented by counsel, this argument is properly construed as a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, as addressed by the Immigration Judge and in this order. Furthermore, as the respondent has not presented any evidence that he is eligible for relief under his “alternative rights and remedies,” we cannot conclude that his lack of information precluded a knowing and intelligent waiver.
 
*2 Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz

APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on December 17, 2012, when it dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s October 12, 2010, decision. At that time, we also declined to reinstate the 60-day period of voluntary departure that the Immigration Judge had granted because the respondent did not present proof of payment of the voluntary departure bond within 30 days of filing the appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(c)(3)(ii). On March 18, 2013, the respondent filed a timely motion to reopen. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The respondent claims that he paid the $500 voluntary departure bond on October 13, 2010, and that his former attorney, who has resigned from the practice of law for disciplinary reasons, rendered ineffective assistance of counsel when he failed to advise the respondent of the need to submit proof of payment of the bond to the Board with the Notice of Appeal. The Department of Homeland Security does not oppose reopening the proceedings for the limited purpose of reinstating the grant of voluntary departure. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g). The motion will be granted.

FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the DHS. See section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).

2013 WL 2608402 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARTURO ELOHIM GUERRERO GARCIA
File: AXXX XX9 799 - Harlingen, TX
May 20, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kyle D. Brown, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Richard I. Newman
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 12, 2011, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On May 4, 2011, the Immigration Judge entered an order granting the respondent the privilege of voluntarily departing the United States on or before September 1, 2011. On September 1, 2011, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge alleging ineffective assistance by his former counsel and requesting that proceedings be reopened to await adjudication of the U visa petition filed by his wife. On October 12, 2011, the Immigration Judge denied the motion as untimely and declined to exercise his sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings.
 
We are not persuaded that the Immigration Judge’s decision should be reversed. There is no dispute that the respondent’s motion to reopen, which was filed more than 90 days after the Immigration Judge entered his final administrative order, is untimely. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1299a(c)(7)(C)(i); see also Matter of Goolcharan, 23 I&N Dec. 5, 8 (BIA 2001) (noting that the date on which a period of voluntary departure expires is inconsequential to the timely filing of a motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge). Moreover, the respondent does not assert that his motion falls within any of the exceptions to the filing deadlines for motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4). Rather, he argues that the filing deadline should be equitably tolled in light of his prior attorney’s ineffective assistance of counsel (Respondent’s Br. at 4-8). Alternatively, he argues that the Immigration Judge erred in not reopening proceedings sua sponte (Respondent’s Br. at 8-9).
 
To the extent the respondent requests equitable tolling of the filing deadline, we note that the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit has not addressed in a published decision whether the motion time limitations may be equitably tolled in cases claiming ineffective assistance of counsel. See Ramos-Bonilla v. Mukasey, 543 F.3d 216, 219-220 (5th Cir. 2008) (holding that a request for equitable tolling of a time or number-barred motion to reopen on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel is “in essence an argument that the BIA should have sua sponte reopened the proceeding based upon the doctrine of equitable tolling”). An alien seeking equitable tolling of a motion to reopen period based on ineffective assistance of counsel must affirmatively demonstrate how he acted with due diligence during the entire period he seeks to toll, and the respondent has not done so. See, e.g., Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127 (2d Cir. 2008); Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167 (2d Cir. 2006). According to the respondent’s affidavit, he raised the issue of his wife’s U visa with his former attorney prior to the May 4, 2011, hearing (Motion to Reopen, Tab 9). The respondent and his wife decided to file a new Form I-918, Petition for U Nonimmigrant Status, on the day of the hearing, which they ultimately did on August 8, 2011 (Id.) The respondent has not explained why he waited more than 100 days after the Immigration Judge entered his decision to retain a new attorney or to seek reopening of his proceedings. Under these circumstances, we cannot conclude that the respondent acted with due diligence during the time he seeks to toll.
 
*2 We note that even if the respondent had shown that he acted with due diligence, he has not sufficiently complied with the evidentiary requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). An alien seeking to establish ineffective assistance of counsel must (1) provide an affidavit attesting to the relevant facts, including a detailed description of the agreement with former counsel, (2) establish that former counsel has been informed of the allegations and provided with an opportunity to respond, and (3) indicate whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authority, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, supra. The respondent has not complied with the second and third requirements. There is no evidence that the respondent’s prior attorney has been informed of the specific allegations being made against him or that he has been given an opportunity to respond. Additionally, the respondent has not indicated whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authority or explained why such a complaint has not been filed. Accordingly, the respondent has not established that his prior attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
We are not persuaded by the respondent’s appellate arguments that the Immigration Judge erred in declining to reopening proceedings sua sponte. Immigration Judges may exercise sua sponte authority to reopen in “truly exceptional situations” where the interests of justice would be served. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999). Proceedings will generally not be reopened sua sponte where an alien seeks to pursue relief over which the Board and the Immigration Judges have no jurisdiction. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103 (BIA 2009) (declining to reopen and remand proceedings to allow an arriving alien to seek adjustment of status before USCIS). As the Immigration Judge noted, the USCIS has sole jurisdiction over all petitions for U nonimmigrant status. See 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(1). Moreover, the entry of a final order of removal does not preclude the USCIS from adjudicating a petition, and the respondent may request a stay of removal with the USCIS pursuant to 8 CFR § 241.6(a). See 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(f)(2)(ii). Accordingly, we will affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. The following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
*3 Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD
2013 WL 2608402 (BIA)
End of Document

2013 WL 2608445 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NAZO GAPRINDASHVILI A.K.A. NAZO GAPRINDASVILI A.K.A. NATO GAPRINOASHVILI A.K.A. ANNA GAPRINDASHVILI A.K.A. NAZO MODEBADZE
File: AXXX XX6 763 - New York, NY
May 15, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Irina Kogan, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s September 12, 2012, decision denying her request for a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The respondent conceded that she is removable as charged under section 237(a)(2)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i). Before the Immigration Judge, she did not dispute the fact of the conviction that forms the basis for her removability. However, the respondent contended that she received ineffective assistance of counsel in her state criminal proceedings because her lawyer did not advise her of the adverse immigration consequences that might result from pleading guilty. Accordingly, she sought post-conviction relief in state court based on Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S. Ct. 1473 (2010), and requested a continuance of her removal proceedings pending the state court’s decision.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent’s desire to await the outcome of her motion to vacate her 2007 New York state conviction for petty larceny did not amount to “good cause” for a continuance. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS), which was opposed to the respondent’s request for a continuance, issued the Notice to Appear in this matter on November 23, 2010 (I.J. at 1, 3; Exh. 1). The Immigration Judge continued these proceedings in March 2011, December 2011, June 2012, and July 2012 (I.J. at 3-4; Tr. at 12-13, 20, 25-26). The Immigration Judge gave the respondent a specific deadline by which to present evidence that she had filed a motion to vacate, but the respondent did not comply (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 13). Rather, she filed the motion to vacate her conviction on September 7, 2012, 5 days prior to her final hearing before the Immigration Judge (I.J. at 4). On appeal, the respondent asserts that she could not afford to file a motion earlier due to her recent legal expenses. See Resp. Brief at 2.
 
The likelihood of success on the respondent’s motion to vacate is speculative. Even though the respondent apparently is relying upon the 2010 decision in Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, in support of her claims to vacate her New York state conviction, the United States Supreme Court has recently determined that, with respect to federal convictions, the decision in Padilla does not apply retroactively. Chaidez v. United States, 133 S.Ct. 1103 (2013). Nonetheless, we acknowledge that there may be an independent and adequate ground under New York state law upon which the respondent may be able to pursue her post-conviction claims. See Michigan v. Long, 463 U.S. 1032, 1040-1042 (1983).
 
*2 Overall, considering the significant amount of time that the respondent was provided to pursue her post-conviction relief claims, her lack of diligence in filing the motion to vacate, the DHS’s opposition to her request, and the speculative nature of post-conviction relief claims, we conclude that the respondent did not establish “good cause” for a further continuance of proceedings. See Matter of Sanchez-Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807, 815 (BIA 2012) (recognizing that an alien should not be granted a continuance “as a dilatory tactic to forestall the conclusion of removal proceedings”).
 
The respondent’s conviction evidently remains undisturbed, as she has yet to indicate that she has received a vacatur. Therefore, her conviction is valid for the purpose of these removal proceedings. Further, the continuing validity of the respondent’s conviction despite the passage of approximately 20 months since the Immigration Judge’s decision, is sufficient to establish that the denial of the respondent’s continuance request was not prejudicial, as it must be in order to constitute a due process violation. See Waldron v. INS, 17 F.3d 511 (2d Cir. 1994), cert. denied, 513 U.S. 1014 (1994).
 
The Immigration Judge was presented with a facially valid removal charge; even considering the humanitarian considerations present, he need not have adjourned removal proceedings pending the outcome of collateral criminal proceedings. In conclusion, the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s request for a continuance and ordered her removed from the United States. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
2013 WL 2608445 (BIA)
End of Document

2013 WL 2608470 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LOURDES SOBREPENA SUTTON A.K.A. LOURDES SOBREPENA MADULID A.K.A. LOURDES SOBREPENA CORPUZ
File: AXXX XX5 827 - Detroit, MI
May 13, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mary Carmen R. Madrid-Crost, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mark Jebson
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen her removal proceedings, alleging she was the victim of ineffective assistance during her prior proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security opposes reopening. The motion will be denied.
 
The party requesting reopening bears the burden of establishing the existence of new, previously unavailable, and material evidence which would likely affect the outcome of the proceedings. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). If an alien alleges reopening is warranted due to ineffective assistance of counsel, the alien must establish that prior counsel’s conduct was deficient, must demonstrate that the deficiencies prejudiced the alien, and must substantially comply with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
We are not persuaded that reopening is warranted. The respondent’s motion does not substantially comply with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra. Hamid v. Ashcroft, 336 F.3d 465, 469 (6th Cir. 2003). The respondent has not submitted any evidence regarding whether a complaint was filed with the appropriate disciplinary committee or whether prior counsel has been informed of these allegations. Additionally, the respondent has not submitted her own affidavit. Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980) (noting uncorroborated statements by counsel in a brief or motion do not constitute evidence). As the respondent’s motion does not comply with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, we do not address the claims that prior counsel’s conduct was deficient or that the respondent was prejudiced.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2013 WL 2608470 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARTURO PEREZ-LEON
File: AXXX XX2 367 - Aurora, CO
May 13, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lisa A. Guerra, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kathy French Giraitis
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 30, 2013. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and found him ineligible for relief from removal based on his criminal conviction.1 On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
On appeal, the respondent requests remand on the grounds that the conviction, which renders him ineligible for relief from removal, is being challenged before the criminal court pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010).2 The respondent alleges that he was not advised of the immigration consequences of his conviction, and he has filed a motion to vacate it. However, the conviction has not been vacated.
 
First, we note that the respondent did not request a continuance on the record. Consequently, the Immigration Judge did not rule on the request on the record or in his decision. Furthermore, while the respondent is free to pursue the post-conviction motion, such a motion does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). The post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Respondent (through counsel) admitted the factual allegations in the Notice to Appear and conceded removability for entering the United States without authorization (Tr. at 24; Exh. 1). Removability and relief from removal are not at issue on appeal.

2

In Padilla v. Kentucky, the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel. See also Chaidez v. United States, 568 U.S. ___, 133 S.Ct. 1103 (2013) (holding that Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, which requires defense counsel to advise a defendant about the risk of deportation arising from a guilty plea, does not apply retroactively).

2013 WL 2608473 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CHRISTOPHER GAYLE DOWNER
File: AXXX XX6 769 - Buffalo, NY
May 13, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Denise C. Hochul
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 10, 2013, denying his motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent removed in absentia for his failure to appear for the hearing on October 23, 2012. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal the respondent asserts that he did not receive notice of the hearing. The record reflects that the respondent was served by regular mail with a Notice to Appear. The Notice to Appear advised the respondent that he must notify the Immigration Court immediately by using Form EOIR-33 whenever he changed his address or telephone number during the course of the proceeding. In addition, the record reflects that the respondent appeared pro se at several Mater Calendar Hearings and appeared with his attorney, Stephen K. Tills, at hearings on February 3, 2011, and April 5, 2011. Furthermore, on February 2, 2012, a notice of hearing was sent to the respondent’s attorney, Stephen K. Tills, Esquire. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255, 259 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a) (stating that whenever an alien is required to be given notice, such notice shall be given to the attorney of record). It appears that the respondent failed to stay in contact with his attorney. The record contains a motion to withdraw from Stephen K. Tills, Esquire, filed on April 19, 2012, requesting that he be allowed to withdraw because of lack of communication with the respondent. A notice of hearing was sent to the respondent at his last known address on May 11, 2012. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, finding that the hearing notice was served upon the respondent’s attorney of record.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the notice of hearing provided to the respondent’s attorney of record, at the time, constitutes notice to the respondent. Matter of Barocio, supra. Furthermore, the Immigration Judge properly found that the respondent did not file a change of address with the Immigration Court, nor did he stay in contact with his attorney and provide contact information for himself.
 
*2 Moreover, to the extent the respondent alleges, for the first time, ineffective assistance of former counsel, we find no compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In that case, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned is informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. Here, however, the respondent has failed to even minimally fulfill any of the above-noted requirements. See Jian Yun Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 46-47 (2d Cir. 2005) (finding that Lozada procedural requirements are reasonable, and that, at a minimum, “substantial, if not exact, compliance” with Lozada is required). Moreover, the respondent has not established prejudice. See Debeatham v. Holder, 602 F.3d 481 (2d Cir. 2010) (an alien claiming ineffective assistance of counsel must show prejudice resulting from counsel’s alleged deficiencies). As such, reopening on this basis is not warranted. The respondent has not established any other exceptional circumstance for his failure to appear. Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
2013 WL 2608473 (BIA)
End of Document

2013 WL 2608492 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WILLIAM ENRIQUE ALVARADO MELENDEZ
File: AXXX XX5 564 - Dallas, TX
May 10, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
INTERLOCUTORY APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jessica Vargas-Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Dan Gividen
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, and the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) have each filed an interlocutory appeal from the Immigration Judge’s January 29, 2013, decision denying their joint motion to terminate.
 
Although this Board does not ordinarily entertain interlocutory appeals, we have on occasion ruled on the merits of interlocutory appeals where we deemed it necessary to address important jurisdictional questions regarding the administration of the immigration laws, or to correct recurring problems in the handling of cases by Immigration Judges. See, e.g., Matter of Guevara, 20 I&N Dec. 238 (BIA 1990, 1991); Matter of Dobere, 20 I&N Dec. 188 (BIA 1990). This matter involves a recurring problem in the handling of cases by Immigration Judges. We will therefore accept this case by certification and address the merits of these interlocutory appeals. Matter of Ruiz-Campuzano, 17 I&N Dec. 108 (BIA 1979); Matter of Ku, 15 I&N Dec. 712 (BIA 1976); Matter of Guevara, 20 I&N Dec. 238 (BIA 1990, 1991); Matter of Dobere, 20 I&N Dec. 188 (BIA 1990). The appeals will be sustained.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We will sustain the parties’ appeals because we agree that the respondent’s conviction, which was vacated pursuant to the Supreme Court’s decision in Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356 (2010) no longer constitutes a conviction within the meaning of section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(48)(A).
 
Our conclusion in this case is consistent with our decision in Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003), in which we concluded that a conviction vacated on the merits because of a legal or constitutional defect is not a conviction under section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Act but a conviction vacated solely because of its immigration consequences remains a conviction under section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Act. We recognize, however, that while the application of Pickering to this case is urged by both the respondent and the DHS, the rule in Pickering has not been adopted by the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit. See Matter of Anselmo, 20 I&N Dec. 25, 31 (BIA 1989) (explaining that the Board historically follows a court’s precedent in cases arising in that circuit). As the Immigration Judge noted, the general rule in the Fifth Circuit is that a conviction falls within the meaning of section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Act even if it is subsequently vacated for any reason. See Renteria-Gonzalez v. INS, 322 F.3d 804 (5th Cir. 2002); see also Garcia-Maldonado v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 284, 291 (5th Cir. 2007).
 
*2 We conclude, however, that in this case the Supreme Court’s decision in Padilla precludes strict application of Renteria. In Padilla, the Supreme Court held that an attorney’s failure to properly advise an alien of the immigration consequences of a guilty plea constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel in violation of the Sixth Amendment. The respondent’s conviction was vacated on this basis. Applying Renteria to the instant case would render the Supreme Court’s decision in Padilla meaningless because notwithstanding a judicial determination that the respondent’s conviction was constitutionally infirm and that the conviction was indeed vacated, no meaningful remedy would be available to the respondent, contrary to what the Supreme Court contemplated in its decision. Accordingly, we conclude that the respondent’s vacated conviction does not constitute a conviction within the meaning of section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Act.
 
In light of our determination that the respondent’s conviction does not remain a conviction within the meaning of section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Act, we will sustain the parties’ appeals and vacate the Immigration Judge’s January 29, 2013, decision.
 
ORDER: The appeals are sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s January 29, 2013, order is vacated, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
2013 WL 2608492 (BIA)

2013 WL 2610032 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YESY MARLENY LANDAVERDE A.K.A. YESY LANDAVERDE FLORES
File: AXXX XX3 241 - Boston, MA
May 10, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Desmond P. FitzGerald, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
*1 This matter was last before us on February 24, 2012, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal and remanded the record to the Immigration Judge for the limited purpose of providing the respondent with the required voluntary departure advisals pursuant to Matter of Gamero, 25 I&N Dec. 164 (BIA 2010). On May 16, 2012, the Immigration Judge provided the respondent with the required advisals and granted her until May 23, 2012, to depart the United States voluntarily. On June 15, 2012, the respondent filed a timely appeal. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge denied her due process at her May 16, 2012, hearing. She indicates that her primary attorney was unavailable to represent her on May 16, 2012, because he was obligated to appear in a criminal case. Consequently, her primary attorney arranged for a substitute attorney to represent the respondent. The respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge denied her the right to counsel by requiring her to proceed without her primary attorney and by not continuing her case. She requests that the Board remand these proceedings to the Immigration Judge to provide her with a new hearing in which she is represented by her primary attorney.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s request for a continuance of her removal proceedings to await the availability of her primary counsel. See section 240(b)(4)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(4)(A); Matter of C-B-, 25 I&N Dec. 888 (BIA 2012). The respondent has not demonstrated that she was denied a full and fair hearing by the Immigration Judge’s denial of her request for a continuance. See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987) (the decision to grant or deny a continuance is within the discretion of the Immigration Judge, if good cause is shown, and that decision will not be overturned on appeal unless it appears that the respondent was deprived of a full and fair hearing). The respondent’s removal proceedings were remanded by the Board solely to provide the respondent with the required voluntary departure advisals and to reinstate her voluntary departure period. The record reflects that the respondent was represented by a substitute counsel at her May 16, 2012, hearing. Hence, we find that the respondent was not denied her right to representation. Moreover, the respondent does not allege that her substitute counsel provided her with ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Thus, the respondent has not established that she was prejudiced by having a substitute counsel represent her at the hearing. See Pulisir v. Mukasey, 524 F.3d 302 (1st Cir. 2008) (prejudice occurs when it is shown that an abridgement of due process is likely to have affected the outcome of the proceedings); Matter of C-B-, 25 I&N Dec. 888 (BIA 2012). In light of the foregoing, we agree with the Immigration Judge that “good cause” did not exist for a further continuance of these removal proceedings.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
*3 Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

2013 WL 2610048 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GENARO BENITEZ-HERNANDEZ
File: AXXX XX9 176 - Buffalo, NY
May 9, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 3, 2012. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. On appeal, the respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel, which constitutes exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not demonstrated exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear at his August 17, 2007, hearing. A motion based upon exceptional circumstances must be filed within 180 days of the Immigration Judge’s in absentia order of removal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely, inasmuch as it was not filed until February 23, 2012.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that he acted with due diligence in filing his motion to reopen and that the filing deadline for his motion to reopen should be equitably tolled. The respondent has the burden to demonstrate that he exercised due diligence during the “entire” time he seeks to have tolled, which “includes both the period of time before the ineffective assistance of counsel was or should have been discovered and the period from that point until the motion to reopen is filed.” See Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 132 (2d Cir. 2008); Cekic v INS, 435 F.3d 167, 170 (2d Cir. 2006) (“[N]o matter how egregiously ineffective counsel’s assistance may have been, an alien will not be entitled to equitable tolling unless he can affirmatively demonstrate that he exercised reasonable due diligence during the time period sought to be tolled.”) In this matter, the record reflects that the respondent admits that he was aware that his appeal of the Immigration Judge’s in absentia order of removal was returned to the Immigration Court by this Board for lack of jurisdiction on April 29, 2009 (Resp. Br. at 3).1 However, the respondent did not properly file his motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge until February 23, 2012. According to the respondent, he relied upon the statements of his prior counsel, who did not suggest that the respondent take further action to rescind his in absentia order of removal (Resp. Br. at 3). The respondent also argues on appeal that he endured “emotional distress/severe depression” and other medical issues during this time period (Resp. Br. at 2).
 
*2 We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not demonstrated that he exercised due diligence during the time period he seeks to have equitably tolled. The record reflects that the respondent waited nearly 3 years after learning that his appeal was dismissed for lack of jurisdiction by this Board before consulting with a new attorney. The respondent has not demonstrated that his reliance on the advice of his former attorney to “wait for a new law” was reasonable, where the respondent was aware that his former counsel should have filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Court to rescind his in absentia order of removal. Furthermore, even though the respondent has submitted receipts, on appeal, for medical services he received during the time period he seeks to have equitably tolled, the respondent has not submitted his medical records or statements from his physical or mental healthcare providers indicating the nature or severity of his conditions. Hence, this Board is unable to properly evaluate the impact that his physical or mental health problems may have had on the respondent’s ability to seek further advice regarding his immigration case. Under these circumstances, we find that the respondent has not met his burden of proof to demonstrate that he exercised due diligence for purposes of equitable tolling of the deadline for filing a motion to reopen to rescind his in absentia order of removal. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(b)(5)(C), provides that “an in absentia order of removal may only be rescinded by filing a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge.”

IN RE: SEAN CURTIS DEFREITAS A.K.A. KENNETH FREEMAN A.K.A. CHARLES FREEMAN A.K.A. FREEMAN CHARLES

MOTION

Adam N. Crandell, Esquire
*1 This case was last before us on February 22, 2013, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen proceedings. The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reconsider this decision on March 25, 2013. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion fails to indicate any error in law or fact which would persuade this Board to disturb our prior decision. See generally Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). In his motion, the respondent requests that this Board reconsider his sua sponte request for reopening based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel; in so doing, he argues that exceptional circumstances exist because his domestic violence conviction does not meet the definition set forth under section 237(a)(2)(E)(i).
 
Notwithstanding the respondent’s reliance on Matter of Velasquez, 25 I&N Dec. 278, we find no error in our previous conclusion that he has not carried his burden of demonstrating that the Immigration Judge erred in finding him removable. See Matter of Beckford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216 (BIA 2000). Accordingly, the motion will be denied.

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: IL TAEK OH A.K.A. HUI JUAN WANG
File: AXXX XX7 560 - Los Angeles, CA
May 6, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steve S. Chang, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on July 11, 2012, when the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying post-conclusion voluntary departure. This motion, submitted on March 8, 2013, is untimely filed. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to this motion, which will be denied.
 
The motion contends the time limits should be subject to equitable tolling due to ineffective assistance of counsel. Specifically, counsel was referencing another alien’s file when crafting the brief. Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785, 793-94 (9th Cir. 2005).
 
While the representation rendered was deficient, the respondent has not established that the deficiencies warrant reopening. Reopening is only warranted on the basis of ineffective assistance if the deficiencies may have affected the outcome of the proceedings. Mohammed v. Gonzales, supra (addressing the need for prejudice if reopening is warranted); see also Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992) (noting the party requesting reopening bears the burden of establishing the new evidence is likely to affect the outcome of the proceedings). As the respondent has not proffered the arguments and claims which should have been submitted on appeal, she has not shown she may have succeeded in her appeal had the brief properly focused on the facts of her proceedings. Additionally, the Board’s decision addressed the respondent’s case carefully and in detail, and we are not persuaded the respondent’s appeal may have been sustained had additional arguments been presented.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2013 WL 2610080 (BIA)
End of Document

2013 WL 2610098 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANASTACIO RAMIREZ-VENEGAS
File: AXXX XX3 933 - San Diego, CA
May 3, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William Baker, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening, reissuance
 
*1 This case was last before us on September 15, 2005, when we affirmed an Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to rescind the in absentia order entered against him. On March 4, 2013, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and reissue asking us to reissue our prior ruling in his case so that he may file a timely petition for review with the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. The respondent’s motion to reopen and reissue will be denied.
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico. In his current motion, he states that he failed to appear for his hearing before an Immigration Judge on January 6, 2005, because he had been removed by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). He further claims that he and his wife did not file a petition for review with the Ninth Circuit after we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to rescind the in absentia order entered against him because his attorney did not inform him that he could do so. He asks that we reissue our prior decision in light of these circumstances so that he can file a petition for review. In the alternative, he asks that we reopen his proceedings sua sponte.
 
The respondent has not presented sufficient evidence to establish that reissuance or reopening is warranted in his case. The respondent’s motion to reopen and reissue is untimely. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i) (stating that motions to reopen “shall be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order of removal”). Equitable tolling of the filing deadline for a motion to reopen is available when an alien is prevented from filing because of deception, fraud, or error, as long as the petitioner acts with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897-98 (9th Cir. 2003). The respondent, however, has not provided sufficient information to establish that he acted with due diligence during the 7 ½ years between the issuance of our prior order and the filing of his current motion.1 See Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672, 680 (9th Cir. 2011).
 
In addition, the respondent has provided conflicting information regarding whether he was, in fact, the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. In his motion, the respondent states that his prior attorney was not necessarily ineffective because he and his wife understood that the case was over after this Board dismissed his appeal. The respondent further states that he has not filed a complaint against his former attorney because he does not think there has been an ethical violation. In the statements attached to the respondent’s motion, however, the respondent and his wife indicate that they would have filed a petition for review if their former attorney had told them about this option. With these apparent conflicts and the absence of a formal bar complaint, it is not clear that the respondent has presented sufficient evidence to meet the procedural requirements necessary to establish that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); cf. Dearinger ex rel. Volkova v. Reno, 232 F.3d 1042, 1045 (9th Cir. 2000) (finding that prejudice is presumed when former attorney filed untimely petition for review which prevented alien from pursuing petition).2
 
*2 Finally, the respondent has not provided sufficient information to establish that exceptional circumstances justify sua sponte reopening of his proceedings. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that our discretion to reopen sua sponte is limited to cases where exceptional circumstances are demonstrated). More than 7 years have passed since we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to rescind and the respondent has not provided much detail regarding what he has done to pursue his case during that time. In addition, there is a significant discrepancy between the statement the respondent submitted with his motion to rescind the in absentia order and the statement he has submitted with his current motion. In his current statement, the respondent indicates that he did not appear for his hearing because he had been removed. In his original statement, however, the respondent stated that he did not appear for his hearing because he thought the Immigration Judge had told him not to appear if he did not have an attorney. The respondent has not acknowledged this discrepancy or offered an explanation for it. He also has not provided any information to support his assertion that he was improperly removed before his hearing on January 6, 2005. Accordingly, based on the totality of the circumstances, the respondent has not provided sufficient evidence to establish that sua sponte reopening and reissuance of our prior ruling in his case is warranted. We therefore deny the respondent’s motion.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen and reissue is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s wife states that, over the years, she consulted with two different attorneys who told her nothing could be done in the respondent’s case. The respondent’s wife, however, does not indicate when she consulted these attorneys. The respondent’s wife also does not indicate when she consulted her current attorney or what prompted her to do so. The respondent therefore has not presented sufficient information to establish that he acted diligently in discovering what he alleges is the error of his prior attorney or in pursuing relief from this error.

2

The issues of due diligence and compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, were not discussed in Dearinger ex rel. Volkova v. Reno, and the alien sought a remedy less than 3 years after the ineffective assistance occurred.

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAUL MEJIA-GOMEZ A.K.A. RAUL MEJIA-COMEZ
File: AXXX XX7 378 - Dallas, TX
May 3, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lisa Koop, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This case comes before the Board on remand from the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit. Raul Mejia-Comez, No. 12-60365 (5th Cir. 2013).
 
In an order dated May 10, 2011, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s application for relief and ordered his removal to Mexico. The respondent filed a timely motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge based on ineffective assistance of counsel, and argued, inter alia, that he was eligible for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a). The Immigration Judge denied the motion on June 14, 2011, and the respondent appealed. In a decision dated December 2, 2011, the Board found that the Immigration Judge erred in determining that the respondent was ineligible for relief under section 245(a) of the Act, and it remand the case to provide the respondent an opportunity for a hearing on his application for adjustment of status.
 
This case was last before the Board on April 10, 2012, when it granted a motion by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) to reconsider its December 2, 2011, decision. See section 240(c)(6) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). Citing the regulation at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(e), the Board granted the motion to reconsider based on evidence that the respondent was removed from the United States on June 14, 2011, prior to the respondent’s filing of the appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen.
 
The respondent petitioned the circuit court for review, and in a decision dated January 2, 2013, the circuit court granted a motion by the Attorney General to remand the case to the Board for further consideration of the motion to reconsider, as well as the respondent’s appeal, in light of recent precedent decisions, including Garcia-Carias v. Holder, 697 F.3d 257 (5th Cir. 2012). Following remand, the respondent filed a timely brief with the Board. The DHS has not filed a brief or statement subsequent to the circuit court’s decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g).
 
In Garcia-Carias v. Holder, supra, the circuit court held that the regulations that administratively barred motions to reopen filed by or on behalf of aliens who had departed from the United States impermissibly conflicted with provision of the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act. Given the decision in Garcia-Carias v. Holder, supra, and the history and posture of this case, we will deny the motion to reconsider filed by the DHS. Accordingly, the Board’s decision dated December 2, 2011, remains viable, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge to provide the respondent an opportunity for a hearing on his application for adjustment of status.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the Board’s December 2, 2011, decision.1
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

2013 WL 2610112 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LORENZA SILVERIO-PONCIANO
File: AXXX XX1 545 - Memphis, TN
May 2, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Barry J. McWhirter, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Scot A. Bearup
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the December 9, 2011, decision of the Immigration Judge denying her motion to reopen her removal proceedings conducted in absentia on April 6, 2011. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact, including the Immigration Judge’s determination of credibility, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent’s order of removal may be rescinded if she demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i). “The term ‘exceptional circumstances’ refers to exceptional circumstances (such as serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of an immediate relative of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances) beyond the control of the alien.” Section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
The respondent acknowledges that she received notice of her hearing to be held in Memphis, at 9:00 a.m. on April 6, 2011, at which she was ordered removed in absentia. However, after departing from home in Knoxville at 6:00 a.m. on the 6-hour drive to Memphis, the respondent contacted her former counsel. The respondent claims that she did not continue on her journey, which she knew would end at about noon, because her former counsel told her that she had already been removed (Resp. Motion at Affidavit; I.J. at 2).
 
We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision with the following notations. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994).
 
The respondent does not dispute that she had notice of her hearing to be held at 9:00 a.m. on April 6, 2011. The notice was mailed on August 19, 2010, more than 7 months prior to the date of the hearing. The respondent has not described any serious illness or death that caused her to delay departure until 6:00 a.m., or otherwise caused her to miss her hearing. The respondent’s departure time, date, and mode of transportation were entirely within her control. The advice the respondent received from her counsel to return home, after the respondent had already created a situation resulting in her absence, is neither compelling nor does it take the circumstances outside the respondent’s control.1 Therefore, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not demonstrated exceptional circumstances.
 
*2 Due to the nature of our disposition of this matter, we need not address the respondent’s other contentions on appeal and we find no basis to remand this case for further proceedings. INS v. Bagamasbad, 429 U.S. 24, 25-26 (1976) (as a general rule, courts and agencies are not required to make findings on issues the decision of which is unnecessary to the results they reach). Finally, we are not persuaded that these circumstances require the exercise of our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent does not claim ineffective assistance of counsel on appeal (Resp. Br. at 5).

IN RE: ALADDIN ARGAMASO ATIENZA A.K.A ALADDIN A. ATIENZA A.K.A. ALADDIN ARGAMASO ATIENZA
File: AXXX XX9 810 - Chicago, IL
April 30, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Elizabeth Treacy
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(R) of the Act
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)] - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
 
APPLICATION: Termination of proceedings; continuance; motion to remand
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s January 4, 2013, decision finding him removable as charged and ordering him removed from the United States. During the pendency of his appeal, the respondent filed a motion to remand. The motion will be denied, and the appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of the Philippines, was admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident on June 26, 2001 (Exh. 1; I.J. at 2). On September 21, 2010, the respondent was convicted for the offenses of possession of methamphetamine, less than 5 grams; and, burglary, in violation of 720 ILCS sections 646/16(a) and 5/19-1(a), respectively, and for the offense of forgery digital signature in violation of 720 ILCS section 5/17-2(a)(4)(2) (I.J. at 3; Exh. 2). For each of these offenses, the respondent was sentenced to 3 years in prison (I.J. at 3; Exh. 2).
 
The respondent does not dispute the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that he is removable as charged and ineligible for relief from removal as a result of his convictions (I.J. at 3). The respondent did not file any applications for relief (I.J. at 4). Rather, through his appeal, the respondent argues that he has been the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel in these proceedings because his former attorney did not make the argument that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel during his criminal proceedings.
 
In this regard, we first note that the respondent has not complied with the requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim pursuant to our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), such that he can succeed. Briefly, the Lozada requirements are (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit detailing counsel’s failings, (2) that counsel be informed of the allegations, and (3) that the motion show that disciplinary charges have been filed with the appropriate authority. Id. Second, the alien “must also show that he or she was prejudiced by the actions or inactions of counsel.” See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556 (BIA 2003). Further, the record reflects that respondent’s counsel did in fact argue the respondent was a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel during his criminal proceedings (I.J. at 4-5). However, as found by the Immigration Judge, the respondent did not provide evidence establishing he had filed for or was granted any post-conviction relief by the criminal court. Thus, he did not establish that his convictions were not final for immigration purposes (I.J. at 4-5). See Matter of Chavez, 24 I&N Dec. 272 (BIA 2007).
 
*2 Through his motion to remand, the respondent argues that his case should be remanded to the Immigration Judge in accordance with Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010), because he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel during his criminal proceedings relating to advisals regarding the immigration consequences of his conviction and that he has filed for post-conviction relief collaterally attacking his convictions. The respondent has submitted a copy of a motion he says was filed with the criminal court regarding his collateral attack in support of his request for a remand.1 Notwithstanding the respondent’s assertions, he has not submitted any evidence that the criminal court changed the disposition of his conviction. Without evidence that his conviction has been vacated or otherwise altered by the criminal court, his convictions remain final for immigration purposes. See Matter of Chavez, supra. Therefore, we find no reason to remand the respondent’s proceedings. The respondent does not otherwise dispute the Immigration Judge’s decision in this case.2
 
Accordingly, the motion will be denied, and the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied, and the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

While, in this letter, the criminal attorney advises the respondent that he will have to seek post-conviction relief arguing ineffective assistance of counsel, the attorney maintains that he did his best during the criminal proceedings. In any event, the letter does not establish that the respondent’s convictions have been vacated by the criminal court.

IN RE: HAI VAN HO AKA VAN HO

April 30, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Arthur V. Cook, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 16, 2012, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen the removal proceedings. The Immigration Judge previously ordered the respondent’s removal from the United States to Vietnam, following the respondent’s failure to appear on time for a hearing on May 16, 2012. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to this appeal. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded for further proceedings.1
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The respondent contends that his failure to appear on time for the hearing on May 16, 2012, was due to an error made by his former counsel. In the respondent’s motion to reopen, filed with the Immigration Court, the respondent’s former counsel admits he made a mistake which resulted in him not appearing in court with the respondent, which was the reason the respondent was late in entering the courtroom for his hearing. Based on this assertion, it appears that the respondent is claiming that he did not appear on time at his scheduled hearing due to ineffective assistance of counsel. In Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996), we held that an alien alleging ineffective assistance of counsel who satisfied the requirements set out by this Board in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), had established exceptional circumstances for purposes of section 242B(c)(3) of the Act.
 
In the case at bar, the respondent has not complied with all of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. The motion is not supported by an affidavit or declaration by the respondent regarding the arrangement that was entered into with his former attorney, nor is there any evidence that the respondent filed a disciplinary complaint against his former attorney. However, because the respondent’s former attorney of record represented the respondent during the filing of the motion to reopen, the attorney had notice of the ineffective assistance of counsel claim and admitted that his mistake resulted in the respondent’s late arrival in the courtroom for his hearing.
 
The record in the instant case strongly indicates that there may have been ineffective assistance of counsel. Despite the respondent’s failure to meet the formal requirements of Matter of Lozada, we find that the respondent has presented a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and the record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
*2 The respondent did not have a motive for avoiding his hearing. In fact, the evidence indicates that he was at the hearing but entered the courtroom late because he was waiting for his attorney to join him. Likewise, his efforts in seeking reopening of these proceedings without undue delay further demonstrate his intention to appear for his hearing. Accordingly, the following orders shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained and the record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD

On appeal, the respondent argues that he did not receive notice of adjudication of his Form I-751, and that it still has not been ruled on so that the Immigration Court lacked jurisdiction in the case to order the respondent removed. However, evidence of record indicates that the Form I-751 was denied on February 18, 2010. As the case is being reopened and remanded, the respondent will have the opportunity to raise that issue before the Immigration Court.

IN RE: MARIA GUADALUPE OCHOA-ALCANTAR

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Xavier Rosas, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, was ordered removed in absentia on February 22, 2001. On December 16, 2011, the respondent filed a motion to reopen proceedings, which the Immigration Judge denied on March 2, 2012. The respondent filed a timely appeal of that decision. The appeal will be sustained, proceedings will be reopened and the record will be remanded.
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen finding that her motion was untimely and did not warrant sua sponte reopening. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied.
 
In light of the unique circumstances presented in this case, including evidence reflecting that the respondent’s former counsel provided ineffective assistance which resulted in the in absentia removal order because of his direction to the respondent not to appear at her February 22, 2001, hearing, an exceptional situation is present to warrant sua sponte reopening of these proceedings. In reaching this conclusion, we note that the respondent’s ability to demonstrate due diligence has been extremely hindered by the ongoing extensive and serious health issues of her lawful permanent resident husband, and we also note that that the respondent appears eligible for cancellation of removal.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, proceedings are reopened and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
Sharon Hoffman

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA GABRIELA RAMIREZ-LOPEZ
File: AXXX XX3 236 - San Antonio, TX
April 30, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Philip A. Barr
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of December 10, 2012. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which she was ordered removed in absentia on September 11, 2012. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a motion for summary affirmance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts.
 
Upon de novo review, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent received proper notice of the date, time, and location of her scheduled hearing before the Immigration Judge.
 
The Immigration Judge correctly found that notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent (I.J. at 2; Exh. 2). See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 3.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 292.5(a). The respondent admitted in her motion that she was given verbal notice of her September 11, 2012, hearing, but because her former counsel, Ms. Caballero, kept the written hearing notice, the respondent alleges she forgot the hearing date. On appeal, the respondent alleges that Ms. Caballero never notified the respondent of her hearing date, and failed to return her phone calls.
 
We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “““exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996). Here, however, the respondent has failed to comply with the Lozada requirements. See Lara v. Trominiski, 216 F.3d 487; 498 (5th Cir. 2000) (the general application of the Lozada requirements does not constitute an abuse of discretion). Consequently, the respondent has failed to perfect a claim of ineffective assistance of former counsel, and there is no basis to reopen under “exceptional circumstances”.
 
*2 We find that the respondent has not established an exceptional circumstance herein to warrant the sua sponte reopening of these proceedings. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations).
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: JUAN AUGUSTIN COSUELO-QUINTERO A.K.A. JUAN AUGUSTINE CONSUELO-QUINTERO A.K.A. JUAN AUGUSTIN CONSUELO-QUINTERO
File: AXXX XX9 866 - San Diego, CA
