IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Tina N. Malek, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Scott Simpson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board’s order dated October 8, 2008, remanded this matter to the Immigration Judge for further fact-finding. The Immigration Judge’s August 27, 2009, decision on remand denied the respondent’s application for adjustment of status and ordered him removed to Mexico. The Board entered the final administrative order on November 5, 2010, when we summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal for failure to file a statement of reasons for the appeal and unexplained failure to file a brief.1 The respondent filed this motion to reopen his removal proceedings on January 15, 2013. The motion is filed nearly 2 years after the filing deadline. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i) (90-day time limit for filing motion to reopen). The Department of Homeland Security has filed a memorandum opposing the motion, which will be denied.
 
In bringing this untimely motion to reopen, the respondent claims ineffective assistance of counsel. The Board held in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), that due process rights may be violated when ineffective assistance of counsel prevents an alien from reasonably presenting his case, and the time for filing a motion to reopen may be tolled in cases of ineffectiveness of counsel. The alien must, however, show that he was prejudiced by counsel’s performance. In Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 826 (9th Cir. 2003), the Ninth Circuit held that an alien failed to show prejudice when he could not “show that his admissibility arguments might have been successful on appeal to the BIA.” Since prejudice had not been shown, the court rejected an ineffective assistance of counsel claim in that case and upheld the Board’s summary dismissal of the appeal even though counsel failed to file a brief or statement of reasons for the appeal.2
 
Thus, in Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, supra, and Matter of Lozada, supra, counsels’ failure to file statements of reasons or briefs on appeal, although resulting in summary dismissal of the appeals, did not amount to deprivation of due process and was not prejudicial. In each case, the alien received a full and fair hearing at which the Immigration Judge properly considered and evaluated the alien’s evidence and correctly determined the legal issues. The same reasoning applies in the present matter. This respondent, prior to being admitted as a lawful permanent resident, was convicted under the California Penal Code of assault with a deadly weapon; subsequently, he was convicted of using or being under the influence of a controlled substance. The Immigration Judge properly determined that he never lawfully acquired permanent resident status, and was ineligible for waivers under sections 212(c), 212(h) and 212(i) of the Act. See I.J. at 5-6; see also Tr., Oct. 13, 2005, at 3-5. The respondent’s motion, which does not include a proposed appellate brief or offer the arguments that would be contained in a brief, does not persuade the Board that the Immigration Judge’s decision was incorrect or could have been overturned on appeal if counsel had filed a brief, or that the respondent could establish eligibility for any relief in a reopened hearing.
 
*2 The motion to reopen, therefore, does not establish prejudice from counsel’s performance. The respondent’s untimely motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen and motion for a stay are denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit dismissed in part and denied in part the respondent’s petition for review of the Board’s order. Cosuelo-Quintero v. Holder, 497 Fed.Appx. 756 (9th Cir. 2012).

2

The Rojas-Garcia decision distinguished Dearinger ex rel. Volkova v. Reno, 232 F.3d 1042 (9th Cir. 2000), in which the court recognized an ineffective assistance of counsel claim where the alien’s attorney failed to file a timely notice of appeal, resulting in summary dismissal of the appeal. Dearinger found a rebuttable presumption of prejudice, acknowledging that the alien was required to demonstrate a “plausible ground for relief to justify reopening for ineffective assistance of counsel. Id. at 1046. The alien met that standard.

IN RE: FAWZI SALIM NICOLAS
File: AXXX XX5 832 - Boston, MA
April 15, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen his removal proceedings on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reopen must establish the existence of new, previously unavailable, material evidence. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The party requesting reopening bears the burden of establishing that reopening is warranted and that the new evidence would likely affect the outcome of the proceedings. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N 464 (BIA 1992). In the context of a claim of ineffective assistance, the alien must establish that prior counsel’s representation was defective and that the deficiencies affected the outcome of the removal proceedings. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
The respondent’s assertions do not demonstrate that reopening is warranted. The attorney who allegedly committed ineffective assistance of counsel did not represent the respondent during the course of his removal proceedings or during a subsequent appeal to the Board.1 Rather, the respondent alleges the attorney committed ineffective assistance during the course of a filing of an I-130 family-based visa petition, an interview associated with the I-130 petition, and the filing of an application for advance parole. These alleged deficiencies took place in the context of separate proceedings which occurred prior to the initiation of removal proceedings against the respondent and which involve matters over which the Immigration Judge and this Board lack jurisdiction.2 The respondent has not demonstrated that his removal proceedings were affected by ineffective assistance of counsel in any respect. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

We note that the respondent was represented during the initial removal hearings, and the respondent has not accused the attorney who represented him below of ineffective assistance.

2

APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Immigration Judge did not prepare a separate oral or written decision in this matter fully addressing the issues raised in the respondent’s motion to reopen and, therefore, the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for preparation of a full decision. See Matter of M-P-, 20 I&N Dec. 786 (BIA 1994); Matter of A-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 468 (BIA 1999). The Immigration Judge’s form order dated February 13, 2012, states that the respondent’s motion is denied “for all the reasons set out in DHS Supplemental response.” The form order also notes that “ineffective assistance of counsel argument inapposite when actions complained of were pre-proceeding.” However, the order does not address the respondent’s contention that his prior attorney rendered ineffective assistance by failing to advise him that he had been placed in removal proceedings and had a hearing scheduled. Further, while the form order cites to the respondent’s “failure to corroborate mail receipt problems at his acknowledged address,” it does not acknowledge the respondent’s declaration, or sufficiently analyze whether notice was proper. Upon preparation of the full decision, the Immigration Judge shall issue an order administratively returning the record to the Board. 2013 WL 1933942 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GILBERTO AGUILAR-SALAZAR A.K.A. OSCAR REYES
File: AXXX XX7 656 - El Paso, TX
March 18, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marlene Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Judith F. Bonilla
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico who was admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident in 2009, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated December 7, 2012, denying his request for a continuance and ordering his removal from this country. The respondent’s appeal, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”), will be dismissed.1
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent is subject to removal from the United States under the provisions of section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i), as a result of his 2011 Texas conviction for possession of a controlled substance (cocaine) (I.J. at 1; Tr. at 21-22; Exh. 1). The respondent does not allege that, in light of his aforementioned criminal conviction, he is currently eligible for any form of relief from removal which can be granted by an Immigration Judge or this Board.
 
The respondent’s desire to pursue a post-conviction relief claim with the Texas courts did not amount to “good cause” warranting a continuance of these removal proceedings.2 See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. Even though the respondent, through counsel, has apparently made claims before the Texas courts that his criminal defense attorney provided him with ineffective assistance of counsel, and, through his present counsel, he makes such claims before this Board, it is well-established that the statements of counsel are not evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980). On appeal, the respondent does not identify any actual evidence, i.e., personal affidavits from himself or other competent witnesses, which is contained in the record of these proceedings and supports his counsels’ claims that he has a meritorious post-conviction relief claim under Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010), and Strickland v. Washington, 466 U.S. 668 (1984). Considering the absence of actual and persuasive evidence to support his claims, we conclude that the respondent has not demonstrated a likelihood of success on the merits of his claims before the Texas courts. Additionally, given that the respondent’s post-conviction relief claims are currently pending before the Texas Court of Appeals for the Eighth Circuit, his claims have already been denied by a district court (Respondent’s Br. at 9). See Ex Parte: Gilberto Aguilar, No. 08-12-00369-CR (Tex. App. - El Paso filed Dec. 18, 2012) (appeal from the 243rd District Court of El Paso Country, Texas). The district court’s denial of the respondent’s claims significantly undermines the likelihood that the respondent will eventually prevail on the merits of his claims.
 
*2 Overall, considering the lack of evidence presented by the respondent which substantiates the merits of his post-conviction relief claims and his claims have already been rejected by a Texas district court, we conclude that the respondent did not demonstrate that “good cause” existed for a further continuance of these removal proceedings. See Milton v. Holder, 425 F.App’x. 407 (5th Cir. 2011) (recognizing that the Supreme Court’s decision in Padilla v. Kentucky “does not suggest that immigration proceedings must be stayed while a Padilla-type claim is being pursued in postconviction proceedings.”); Cabral v. Holder, 632 F.3d 886, 890 (5th Cir. 2011); Matter of Sanchez-Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807, 815 (BIA 2012) (recognizing that an alien should not be granted a continuance where the continuance is being requested as a “dilatory tactic to forestall the conclusion of removal proceedings”); Cf. Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009) (recognizing that a continuance may be warranted where an alien has demonstrated that he is the beneficiary of a pending immigrant visa petition and established a likelihood of success on an application for adjustment of status). Accordingly, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to conclude these proceedings by entering an order of removal. The following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Elise L. Manuel
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Even though the transcript of proceedings does not contain a transcript of the hearings which took place after May 21, 2012, the respondent does not allege that the present transcript is insufficient to allow for a meaningful appellate review of the issues presented in this case. See Claudio v. Holder, 601 F.3d 316, 319 (5th Cir. 2010) (holding that an alien’s appeal brief is the “operative document” through which any issues that he wishes to have considered must be raised). Moreover, transcripts are not required, regardless of need, in every case. Matter of Ambrosio, 14 I&N Dec. 381, 382-83 (BIA 1973).

2

We acknowledge that this Board has not issued a precedential decision which explicitly addresses the factors that should be addressed when alien requests a continuance of removal proceedings while he pursues a claim to post-conviction relief. Nonetheless, we conclude that such a precedential decision is not called for in order to adequately address the respondent’s claims. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(g).

IN RE: JUNIOR DUNKLEY
File: AXXX XX1 594 - Atlanta, GA
February 13, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on January 4, 2013, when we found that the respondent had waived his right to appeal through counsel. The respondent had not established that his waiver was not knowing and intelligent or that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and we declined to accept the appeal by certification. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider, and now asserts that his waiver was not knowing and intelligent because his counsel had not adequately advised him of his rights, including his right to appeal. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent raises an ineffective assistance of counsel argument, but has not shown compliance with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), and this is not a case was inadequate representation is apparent from the record. See also Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005). The Immigration Judge specifically confirmed with the respondent at the outset of the final hearing that the “gentleman seated next to [him]” was his attorney and authorized to speak on his behalf. The respondent’s best language was English. Thereafter, in the respondent’s presence, his counsel indicated that it had been determined that the respondent could not proceed with an application for adjustment of status and that “we will agree to removal in this case.”1 Respondent’s counsel stated that he did not think there were any “available applications for relief” and agreed that the detained respondent wanted to take an order of removal. The Immigration Judge asked whether “this is a final order” and respondent’s counsel answered “yes.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a) (regarding action by an attorney of record).
 
While the respondent now states that his attorney did not explain the outcome of the Immigration Judge’s decision to him and that it came as a “surprise” that his appeal rights had been waived, his present claims in this regard are not supported by affidavit or otherwise. And, of equal significance, the respondent has not provided his prior counsel with any notice of these claims and an opportunity to respond. See id. Further, there is nothing presented with this motion that would cause us to reconsider the prior conclusion that further review of the Immigration Judge’s decision by certification has not been shown to be warranted.2 Under these circumstances, we are not persuaded that reconsideration of our January 14, 2013, decision is warranted. Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay is denied as moot.
 
David B. Holmes

IN RE: NOWEL Q. DELA CRUZ A.K.A. NOWEL QUITO-DELA CRUZ
File: AXXX XX6 093 - Imperial, CA

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Melanie Cristol, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
William P. Hollerich
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was previously before the Board on March 27, 2009, when we affirmed without opinion an Immigration Judge’s decision finding the respondent removable as charged. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has now filed the instant timely motion to reopen the removal proceedings, based on the fact that the 2007 conviction for inflicting corporal injury on a spouse, which formed the basis of the respondent’s removability, has been vacated due to ineffective assistance of counsel. The DHS asks the Board to remand this matter to the Immigration Judge so that it may lodge an additional charge of removability against the respondent, based on an independent and still viable 2007 conviction for contempt of court for willful violation of a protection order. The respondent agrees that reopening is warranted, but asks the Board instead to terminate these proceedings without prejudice so that he may be released from DHS custody more expeditiously. The DHS’s motion will be granted, and the record will be remanded.
 
Considering the evidence proffered with the parties’ filings, the proceedings are reopened. See Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003) (holding that a conviction that has been vacated by the criminal court based upon a procedural or substantive defect in the underlying proceedings is no longer a conviction for immigration purposes); see also Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010) (holding that a criminal lawyer representing a non-citizen in connection with a guilty plea has a constitutional duty to advise the defendant that conviction may pose a risk of deportation). Inasmuch as the DHS has stated unequivocally that it intends to lodge an additional charge of removability against the respondent, we find it more prudent to remand this matter to the Immigration Judge, rather than terminate proceedings without prejudice. The respondent may still pursue release from custody from the DHS and/or the Immigration Court, as appropriate. See generally section 236 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1226; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.19, 1236.1.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The DHS’s motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for proceedings not inconsistent with the foregoing decision.
 
David B. Holmes

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HILARIO TLASECA SILVA
File: AXXX XX6 275 - Los Angeles, CA
January 22, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marcelo Gondim, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on February 27, 2009, when the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s March 11, 2008, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind the January 28, 2008, removal order entered in absentia. On October 9, 2012, the respondent filed the pending motion to reopen. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent does not dispute the fact that his motion is untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Rather, alleging ineffective assistance of former counsel(s), he requests that the applicable time limits be equitably tolled. However, the respondent has not sufficiently shown patent error nor substantially complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, including the requirement that he show prejudice as a result of the alleged ineffective assistance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra; Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003). See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process). The respondent has submitted no evidence beyond what was previously before both the Immigration Judge and the Board to support his claim that exceptional circumstances, i.e., injuries from a traffic accident, prevented him from attending his scheduled hearing. The submitted insurance report does not reflect the respondent’s alleged injuries. Likewise, he has submitted no evidence to support his alleged claim that he ultimately did appear as scheduled, albeit late.
 
Moreover, on this record, we do not find that the respondent has sufficiently demonstrated due diligence in pursuing the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra; see, e.g., Singh v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 1090 (9th Cir. 2007) (explaining that to warrant equitable tolling of a filing deadline, the petitioner must show that he has acted with due diligence). The doctrine of equitable tolling applies when “despite all due diligence, [the party invoking equitable tolling] is unable to obtain vital information bearing on the existence of the claim.” Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176, 1193 (9th Cir. 2001) (internal quotations omitted). Despite his asserted inability to read and speak English, the respondent does not dispute that he was, at the very least, aware of both the Immigration Judge’s and the Board’s decisions in this case. Other than contacting current counsel, for which the respondent provides no dates or details, the respondent has offered no reasonable explanation for waiting more than 3 years from the Board’s final decision in this case to file the present motion.
 
*2 On this record, the respondent has not provided an adequate basis to excuse the untimeliness of his motion. Nor has he demonstrated an exceptional situation that would warrant reopening of these proceedings in the exercise of discretion under the Board’s sua sponte authority. Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SIGIFREDO BUENO-GARCIA
File: AXXX XX4 502 - Seattle, WA
January 18, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jonathan Love
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 13, 2010, denying his request for a continuance of proceedings pending action on a collateral attack to his criminal conviction. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Immigration Judge sustained the charge of removability based on the respondent’s February 24, 2005, aggravated felony conviction for conspiracy to deliver a controlled substance in violation of Revised Code of Washington §§ 69.50.401(a)(1)(ii) and 69.50.407 (I.J. at 2; Exh. 2). The Immigration further determined that there was not good cause to continue the case pending action in state court on the respondent’s appeal from the denial of his motion to withdraw his guilty plea and vacate his criminal conviction (I.J. at 3-4). The respondent asserts on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in denying him a continuance of proceedings, argues that his immigration and criminal defense attorneys rendered ineffective assistance, and states that he was not guilty of the drug charge.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a motion for continuance “for good cause shown.” See 8 C.F.R. §§ 3.29, 240.6; Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983). In determining whether an alien has demonstrated “good cause,” an Immigration Judge has broad discretionary authority. See Matter of Sibrun, supra, at 355-56. Indeed, we have held that “an Immigration Judge’s decision denying [a] motion for continuance will not be reversed unless the alien establishes that [the] denial caused him actual prejudice and harm and materially affected the outcome of his case.” Id. at 356-57. A conviction is final for immigration purposes despite the possibility of discretionary review or collateral attack. See Matter of Polanco, 20 I&N Dec. 894 (BIA 1994).
 
We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s decision denying a continuance of proceedings pending resolution in state court of the respondent’s appeal from the denial of his request to collaterally attack his criminal conviction. On the day of the merits hearing, a motion to withdraw the respondent’s guilty plea had been denied by the state court. We agree that a further continuance pending adjudication of a state court appeal was not warranted.
 
*2 As for the respondent’s claims of ineffective assistance, we find no basis to remand for further proceedings. As an initial matter, we note that the respondent has not complied with the evidentiary requirements for asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against his immigration attorney outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988).1 Moreover, he has not established that he was prejudiced by immigration counsel’s actions. Although more than 2 years have passed since the Immigration Judge’s decision, the respondent has not presented any evidence to establish that his state court appeal was successful and that his criminal conviction has been vacated. See Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir. 1999) (finding that prejudice must be shown for a due process challenge and that prejudice is found when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected outcome); Mohsseni Behbahani v. INS, 796 F.2d 249, 251 (9th Cir. 1986) (finding that to prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim there must be a showing of prejudice by counsel’s performance).
 
As for the respondent’s challenge to the actions of his criminal defense attorney and his claim of innocence to the criminal charge, these matters are within the jurisdiction of the state court, not the Immigration Judge or this Board. See Matter of Onyido, 22 I&N Dec. 552, 558 (BIA 1999) (it is impermissible for Immigration Judges or the Board to go behind a conviction and reassess an alien’s guilt or innocence). Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Under Matter of Lozada, supra, a motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel should be supported by an affidavit, reflect whether a complaint had been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and include a response to the allegations by former counsel or report counsel’s failure or refusal to respond. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639.

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    1  Review Granted, Cause Remanded by Urcino v. Sessions, 9th Cir., December 21, 2017
2013 WL 416261 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUIS MARTIN URCINO A.K.A. LUIS URCINO
File: AXXX XX1 156 - Los Angeles, CA
January 14, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mercedes Victoria Castillo, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 20, 2012. The respondent does not contest removability on appeal, but challenges denial of a continuance. The respondent’s appeal fee waiver request is approved. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent requested a continuance to collaterally attack a criminal conviction pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. _, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010).1 An Immigration Judge granted several continuances, for several reasons, over the course of the months May through September, 2012, but on September 20, 2012, a request for a further continuance was denied. I.J. at 2.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009).2 We conclude that good cause was not shown.
 
While the respondent is free to pursue his post-conviction motion, such a motion does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). An alien may not collaterally attack the legitimacy of his otherwise valid state or federal criminal conviction in immigration proceedings. See Matter of Ponce De Leon-Ruiz, 21 I&N Dec. 154 (BIA 1996, 1997; AG 1997); Matter of Gabryelsky, 20 I&N Dec. 750 (BIA 1993); Matter of Fortis, 14 I&N Dec. 576, 577 (BIA 1974). Neither the Immigration Judge nor this Board has the authority to look beyond the record of conviction to determine the guilt or innocence of an alien in immigration proceedings. See Matter of Reyes, 20 I&N Dec. 789 (BIA 1994); Matter of Fortis, supra. Rather, such collateral attacks must be addressed to the appropriate criminal court.
 
*2 Though the respondent has filed a post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction, it does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992). The conviction became final for immigration purposes when the respondent failed to appeal his conviction, allowed the appeal period to lapse, waived his right to a direct appeal, or exhausted the direct appeal of his conviction. See 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(48)(A); Matter of Abreu, supra.
 
On appeal, the respondent describes several procedural difficulties that arose during the course of these proceedings with respect to communications between counsel and the respondent. While we sympathize with the inconvenience and difficulties faced by counsel in representing the respondent, there have been no facts alleged that persuade us that the respondent did not receive a full and fair hearing.
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In Padilla v. Kentucky, the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in his deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates his right to counsel.

2

In determining whether good cause exists to continue removal proceedings, a variety of factors may be considered, including, but not limited to; the Department of Homeland Security’s response to the motion to continue and the reason for the continuance and any other relevant procedural factors. See Matter of Hashmi, supra.

2013 WL 416257 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JURATE VIRGYCIO MILUTYTE
File: AXXX XX1 053 - Cleveland, OH
January 9, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Richard Thompson Herman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Amy L. Sheurman
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Lithuania, has filed a motion to reopen the proceedings and requesting that we reconsider our previous decision, dated August 23, 2012. In the decision, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from a September 9, 2011, Immigration Judge decision denying the respondent a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(i). The motion will be denied.
 
We review the findings of fact, including determinations of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether or not the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
In her motion, the respondent argues that her previous attorney was ineffective in failing to fully develop the record. She argues he failed to have her clarify problems in her testimony concerning a false claim on a student loan application and to have her husband clearly state that he would not travel to Lithuania, but only while his elderly mother was still alive and needed care in the United States.1 She also argues the attorney erred in failing to submit the student loan documentation. We first observe that the respondent has failed to fully comply with the requirements set out in our decision Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Sako v. Gonzales, 434 F.3d 857 (6th Cir. 2006); Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009). The respondent states that she did not file a complaint against her former attorney as he is a good person. However, this does not excuse compliance with the requirements. Whether or not the previous attorney meant well does not alter the purposes of this requirement, including the deterrence of meritless claims and highlighting the standards expected of attorneys in immigration proceedings. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639-40.
 
Nonetheless, even had the respondent complied with the filing requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, the motion would be denied as she has failed to demonstrate any prejudice from the actions of the previous attorney. See Sako v. Gonzales, supra. The Immigration Judge and this Board rested our denial of relief, in part, on the respondent’s false statement that her daughter was a lawful permanent resident on an application for college financial aid (BIA at 2; I.J. at 13). The respondent has now submitted a printout from the financial aid office indicating that the daughter’s status was recorded as “eligible - non-citizen” and a response from the office stating there was not enough information to confirm the daughter’s noncitizen status (Resp. Mot., Exh. B). The respondent also argues that, at the time of her testimony, she did not clearly recall what information she had provided on the form.
 
*2 However, the printout submitted fails to support the respondent’s argument, as it does not indicate what status she claimed for her daughter. It has never been argued that she indicated her daughter was a citizen. We continue to accept her testimony at the time of the hearing that she indicated that her daughter was a lawful permanent resident (Tr. at 66 (daughter), 96, 130-35). The failure of the previous attorney to properly submit this document is not material to the respondent’s claim, as it does not contradict the respondent’s testimony. Furthermore, while the respondent now wishes to clarify her and her husband’s testimony or supplement the testimony in various ways, this does not indicate that the previous attorney erred in presenting the case.
 
The respondent also argues that the Board failed to apply the proper legal standard and that we failed to consider all of the evidence of record, in particular the letter submitted by a doctor concerning the husband’s psychological health. We disagree. While the respondent may wish that we gave more weight to the letter and its conclusions, it was considered previously (BIA at 2). The husband will face emotional hardship from the separation and does suffer from depression related to the removal process. Although the letter states that separation would result in “exceptional and extremely unusual hardship” to the husband, we continue to find the hardship he will face does not rise to “extreme hardship” as provided for under immigration law. See Matter of Cervantes, 22 I&N Dec.560 (BIA 1999), aff’d 232 F.3d 684 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
The Board and the Immigration Judge also properly considered the husband’s testimony, at the time of the hearing that the husband would remain in the United States, due to the health of his mother (Tr. at 85-86). It follows that he may choose to travel to Lithuania once he no longer needs to care for her, but this is speculative. In any event, despite his depression and lack of language skills, his relocation to his wife’s country is not a hardship that is out of the ordinary for a couple where one member is facing removal. In closing, we also note the respondent’s arguments concerning family unity and the remand of her daughter’s record for possible relief. Nonetheless, this remand was part of our previous decision and was properly considered at that time. Based on the foregoing, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent has also submitted a letter from the former attorney to the Department of Homeland Security stating that he lacks experience in Immigration Court. However, this provides little support for the ineffective assistance of counsel claim and a review of the record reveals that the attorney provided proper representation.

2013 WL 416270 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALFREDO JUAREZ A.K.A. ALFREDO JUAREZ-MUNGUIA
File: AXXX XX5 349 - Aurora, CO
January 9, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Hans Meyer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Leila Cronfel
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated September 5, 2012, ordering his removal from the United States. We will dismiss the appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. Contrary to the respondent’s assertions on appeal, the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) presented evidence in these removal proceedings which established that the respondent was subject to removal from the United States as a result of a conviction for a drug-related crime. See section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i). In particular, the DHS filed a “Submission of Evidence in Removal Proceedings,” which the Immigration Judge admitted into the record of these removal proceedings as Exhibit 2.1
 
We acknowledge that not all of the documents contained in Exhibit 2 constitute portions of the respondent’s record of conviction. However, in reaching his decision, the Immigration Judge only relied upon two specific documents: the sentence order contained at page 4 and the plea agreement contained at page 10 (I.J. at 1-2). It was proper for the Immigration Judge to rely upon these two documents in reaching his conclusion that, as the respondent was convicted of possession of a controlled substance (pyrovalerone), he was subject to removal from the United States under the provisions of section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act (I.J. at 1-2). See Shepard v. United States, 544 U.S. 13 (2005); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1308.15(d)(1) (designating pyrovalerone as a controlled substance under Schedule V of the Controlled Substances Act). The respondent does not allege that, at the present time, he is eligible for any form of relief from removal.
 
“Good cause” did not exist for a further continuance of these removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29 1240.6. The respondent was personally served with the Notice to Appear (“NTA”) on June 28, 2012 (Exh. 1). At the time of the respondent’s ultimate removal hearing, on September 5, 2012, he was represented by counsel. The respondent does not allege his prior counsel provided him with ineffective assistance of counsel or that she was somehow unable to effectively represent him. Considering these circumstances, a continuance was not warranted to provide the respondent an opportunity to secure a new attorney. Cf. Matter of C-B-, 25 I&N Dec. 888 (BIA 2012) (holding that an alien did not have sufficient time to obtain counsel where the NTA was issued on January 17, 2012, and the Immigration Judge entered a removal order on January 25, 2012).
 
*2 The respondent’s desire to seek post-conviction relief also did not warrant a continuance. See Tejeda-Acosta v. Holder, No. 12-9503, 2012 WL 6691072 (10th Cir. Dec. 26, 2012); Jimenez-Guzman v. Holder, 642 F.3d 1294, 1297-98 (10th Cir. 2011); U.S. v. Adame-Orozco, 607 F.3d 647, 653 (10th Cir. 2010). The respondent has not presented any evidence that he has actually filed an appropriate motion for post-conviction relief with the appropriate state court. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980) (recognizing that the statements of counsel are not evidence). Considering the lack of evidence presented, the amount of time that the respondent was provided to pursue his post-conviction claims, and the overall speculative nature of post-conviction relief claims, we conclude that the Immigration Judge also properly denied this request for a continuance. See Matter of Sanchez-Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807, 815 (BIA 2012) (recognizing that a continuance should not be granted where it is being sought “as a dilatory tactic to forestall the conclusion of removal proceedings”).
 
As the respondent is subject to removal from the United States, has not applied for any form of relief from removal, and has not demonstrated that a continuance was warranted, the Immigration Judge properly entered an order of removal in this case. Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

It appears that, according to the respondent’s arguments on appeal, the DHS also filed a separate “Submission of Evidence in Bond Proceedings.” However, as bond proceedings are separate and apart from removal proceedings, the record of the respondent’s bond proceedings is not currently before this Board. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.19(d).

2012 WL 6968983 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NATASCHA THERESIA CHASE A.K.A. NATASCHA ANDREA THERESIA CHASE A.K.A. NATASCHA THERESIA HECHENBERGER A.K.A. NATASCHA ANDREA HECHENBERGER
File: AXXX XX5 940 - Arlington, VA
December 20, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Leslie D. Silverman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Pamela P. Ataii
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Canada, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 4, 2012. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and found her ineligible for relief from removal based on her criminal convictions. On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The Notice to Appear alleges that, on October 18, 2011, the respondent was convicted of Petit Larceny, in violation of Virginia law, and sentenced to 12 months imprisonment, with 10 months suspended. On March 8, 2011, the respondent was convicted of Possession of a Controlled Drug, to wit: Cocaine. A record of each of these convictions was entered into the record of proceedings. See section 240(c)(3)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(3)(B); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.41(a). Based on this evidence, the Immigration Judge found the conviction proved (I.J. at 1-2). We do not find this factual finding to be clearly erroneous.
 
The respondent was charged with being removable for having been convicted of an aggravated felony involving theft1 and a controlled substance violation2 (Exh. 1). The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged (I.J. at 1-2). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s convictions include an aggravated felony and that there is clear and convincing evidence of removability. See section 240(c)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(3); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(a); Woodby v. INS, 385 U.S. 276, 286 (1966). Cf. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.15, 1240.10.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent requests remand on the grounds that the convictions, which serve as the basis for removal, and which render her ineligible for relief from removal, are being challenged before the criminal court pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. _, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010).3 Moreover, the respondent alleges that the sentence of her Petit Larceny conviction has been reduced to less than 1 year. However, she has not provided evidence of that. She also concedes that the Possession of Controlled Drug conviction has not been vacated.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009).4 We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately considered the relevant factors to determine whether good cause for a continuance was shown. We conclude that good cause was not shown.
 
While the respondent is free to pursue the post-conviction motion, such a motion does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). The post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
Also on appeal, the respondent argues that a deferred sentence does not constitute a conviction. However, a conviction includes a deferred adjudication or deferred sentencing, where no imprisonment or fine is imposed, and where the charge is dismissed on completion of probation. See 8 U.S.C. 1101(a)(48)(A);5 Matter of Salazar-Regino, 23 I&N Dec. 223 (BIA 2002); Matter of Punu, 24 I&N Dec. 224 (BIA 1998).
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The term “aggravated felony” includes a theft offense (including receipt of stolen property) or burglary offense for which the term of imprisonment is at least 1 year. See section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(G). See also Gonzales v. Duenas-Alvarez, 549 U.S. 183 (2007).

2

Any alien who at any time after entry has been convicted of a violation of (or a conspiracy or attempt to violate) any law or regulation of a State, the United States, or a foreign country relating to a controlled substance (as defined in section 102 of the Controlled Substances Act, (21 U.S.C. 802)), other than a single offense involving possession for one’s own use of 30 grams or less of marijuana, is deportable. See section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i). Cocaine is a controlled substance under 21 U.S.C. § 812(c), sch. II (a)(4).

3

In Padilla v. Kentucky, the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel.

4

In determining whether good cause exists to continue removal proceedings, a variety of factors may be considered, including, but not limited to: the Department of Homeland Security’s response to the motion to continue and the reason for the continuance and any other relevant procedural factors. See Matter of Hashmi, supra.

5

Section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. 1101(a)(48)(A), provides:
The term “conviction” means, with respect to an alien, a formal judgment of guilt of the alien entered by a court or, if adjudication of guilt has been withheld, where--
(i) a judge or jury has found the alien guilty or the alien has entered a plea of guilty or nolo contendere or has admitted sufficient facts to warrant a finding of guilt, and
(ii) the judge has ordered some form of punishment, penalty, or restraint on the alien’s liberty to be imposed.

IN RE: JUAN JOSE CHAVEZ-GUZMAN

December 13, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lorena N. Gutierrez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on July 28, 2011, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. On July 30, 2012, more than 1 year after the Board’s order, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely, as it was not filed within 90 days of the Board’s final administrative decision. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). In the motion, the respondent argues that his proceedings should be reopened based on the ineffective assistance of counsel by attorney Keith Willeford, who represented him before the Immigration Judge and the Board (the “former counsel”). The respondent argues that the former counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel by failing to file before the Immigration Judge a proper application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1), in a timely manner, and by failing to request an extension. The respondent argues that he complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), by submitting an affidavit and notifying the former counsel of his ineffective assistance of counsel claims, as well as filing a complaint with the State Bar of Texas (Motion Exhs. B, C, D). The respondent also submitted an application for cancellation of removal and supporting documents, consisting of his birth certificate and identification card, his son’s birth certificate, his father’s certificate of naturalization, and the approval notice of an I-130 visa petition filed by his father (Motion Exhs. G, K).
 
However, the respondent’s motion is untimely as noted above, and the respondent has presented no arguments, and has submitted no evidence, showing how he acted with due diligence during the 1-year period since the Board dismissed his appeal. The United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit has not addressed in a published decision whether the motion time limitations may be equitably tolled in cases claiming ineffective assistance of counsel. See Ramos-Bonilla v. Mukasey, 543 F.3d 216, 219-220 (5th Cir. 2008) (holding that a request for equitable tolling of a time or number-barred motion to reopen on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel is “in essence an argument that the BIA should have sua sponte reopened the proceeding based upon the doctrine of equitable tolling”). An alien seeking equitable tolling of a motion to reopen period based on ineffective assistance of counsel must affirmatively demonstrate how he acted with due diligence during the entire period he seeks to toll, and the respondent has not done so. See, e.g., Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127 (2d Cir. 2008); Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167 (2d Cir. 2006).
 
*2 The respondent urges us to sua sponte reopen his proceedings under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Based on the circumstances presented in this case, we do not find that the respondent’s case presents “exceptional circumstances” that would warrant the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen sua sponte. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion will be denied.1
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

1

We also note that the respondent has submitted documents showing that his father and son are United States citizens and that he is the beneficiary of an I-130 visa petition filed by his father, but he has not submitted sufficient evidence showing that his qualifying relatives will suffer hardship rising to the level of “exceptional and extremely unusual hardship” upon his removal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (providing that a motion to reopen proceedings for the purpose of submitting an application for relief must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and “all supporting documentation”).

IN RE: SAMUEL ALAS CARTAGENA

December 13, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Aarti R. Sampat, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 On September 27, 2012, this Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The record showed that the respondent, through counsel, waived appeal. Our decision observed that the respondent did not, in filing his appeal, argue that the waiver was not a knowing and intelligent one. Thus, the Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of the right to appeal. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider, which will be denied.
 
In his motion, the respondent observes that the Immigration Judge’s order did not reflect that the appeal was waived in his case (Mot. at 1-2). The respondent also states that he did not recall waiving appeal in the immigration court and he asserts that the immigration court’s information system confirmed that the appeal in the case was due by July 11, 2012 (Mot. at 1-2). The respondent argues that appeal was not waived in his case (Mot. at 3-4). In the alternative, he argues that any waiver was not knowingly or intelligently made since his attorney did not explain his appeal rights to him (Mot. at 3-4).
 
The transcript in this matter establishes that the respondent was granted voluntary departure, the only form of relief for which he applied (Tr. at 45-49).1 The respondent does not currently dispute that the attorney who represented him at his hearing stated on the record that appeal would be waived. (Tr. at 49). Although the respondent now asserts that his attorney did not explain his appeal rights to him, the respondent has not filed a motion alleging ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Accordingly, he has not established a basis for reconsideration or reopening. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986) (providing that an alien is generally bound by the conduct of his attorney).
 
We separately note that even if the respondent had not waived his right to appeal, an appeal from a decision that granted the only relief that was requested is subject to a summary dismissal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(i)(C).
 
The respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

1

Voluntary departure was granted over the objection of the Department of Homeland Security (Tr. at 45-49; I.J. at 1-2).

IN RE: OSAMA MIKHO ORAHA

APPEAL

James B. Rudolph, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Brent Landis
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
*1 The respondent, a native of Iraq and citizen of Canada, appeals from the January 28, 2011, decision of an Immigration Judge, denying his motion to reopen in order to pursue adjustment of status. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings and the entry of a new decision.
 
The Board reviews findings of fact by an Immigration Judge only to determine whether they are clearly erroneous. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462, 464-65 (BIA 2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent entered the United States on October 3, 2004, as a non-immigrant “F-1” student. He remained in the United States beyond his period of authorization or after January 11, 2005. On May 18, 2005, he married Ms. Kimberly Walker, a United States citizen. During the marital interview in February of 2006, Ms. Walker admitted that she had married the respondent for the sole purpose of helping him become a lawful permanent resident. On October 4, 2006, a Notice to Appear was filed. On February 12, 2008, an Immigration Judge found that his application for adjustment of status had been abandoned but allowed him the privilege of voluntarily departing the United States. The respondent appealed. During the pendency of the appeal, the respondent departed the United States and the decision of the Immigration Judge became final, as though no appeal had been taken. On January 14, 2011, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The motion was denied by the Immigration Judge and is the subject of this appeal.
 
The record shall be remanded for further proceedings and the entry of a new decision by the Immigration Judge. In seeking reopening, the respondent conceded that the motion was untimely. However, he contended that the actions of his attorney at the time of his hearing, Mr. Chukwuma A. Obiesie, were ineffective and that the time limits for filing a motion to reopen, therefore, ought to be tolled based on this due process violation. He explains that he filed to reopen soon after it became apparent to him that attorney Obiesie had been ineffective and this was revealed on November 1, 2010, when he met with his current attorney to discuss his case.1 The motion filed before the Immigration Judge included, among other things, a statement from the respondent signed under penalty of perjury and a response from attorney Obiesie as to the allegation of ineffective assistance of counsel made against him.2 In spite of the respondent’s specific grounds seeking equitable tolling and his supporting evidence, the Immigration Judge’s decision consisted of two sentences, denying the motion and finding it to be untimely.
 
*2 In consideration of the respondent’s specific claim of ineffective assistance of counsel in the motion to reopen and the absence of any factual findings by the Immigration Judge as to it, we cannot conduct a proper appellate review. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv) (stating that the Board may not engage in fact finding in the course of deciding appeals except for taking administrative notice of commonly known facts). Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002) (in light of Board’s limited fact-finding ability on appeal, a remand is appropriate when the record is inadequate for review). Accordingly, a remand is warranted for further proceedings. Upon remand, the Immigration Judge shall entertain the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim and enter explicit findings of fact as to whether the respondent has proven equitable tolling to excuse his untimely motion to reopen and if so, whether reopening is warranted as a matter of law and discretion.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings and the entry of a new decision consistent with this decision.
 
Elise L. Manuel
FOR THE BOARD

The record reflects that on October 23, 2010, the respondent married Ms. Ignacia Vazquez.

2

The government also filed an 8-page brief which was responsive to the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.

IN RE: ELLIOT MUDIAGA ONOBRAKPEYA
File: AXXX XX3 882 - Atlanta, GA
December 10, 2012
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Richard C. Gordon, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 28, 1991, denied the respondent’s application for suspension of deportation and ordered him deported to Nigeria. The respondent, through counsel, filed a timely Notice of Appeal with the Board. Counsel subsequently failed to file a brief or otherwise present any specific factual or legal basis for the appeal, and the Board summarily dismissed the appeal in our decision entered on January 16, 1992. The respondent filed a motion to reopen these proceedings on August 23, 2012. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The motion, filed more than 20 years after the Board’s order in the matter, is barred by the applicable time limit. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (motion to reopen deportation proceedings must be filed no later than 90 days after the final deportation order). The motion does not cite any exception to the filing deadline, but claims that the respondent’s former counsel was ineffective in his handling of the appeal decided by the Board in 1992 (incorrectly asserting, however, that counsel did not file a Notice of Appeal). We note that the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit has held that the 90-day time limit for motions to reopen is “mandatory and jurisdictional” and not subject to equitable tolling, even where a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is raised. See Abdi v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 430 F.3d 1148, 1150 (11th Cir. 2005). We further note that the motion does not show that the respondent has complied with the requirements prescribed by the Board in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-40 (BIA 1988), nor does it show that the respondent has been diligent in pursuing his rights over the time period of more than 20 years since the Board’s decision in his appeal.
 
The motion is not accompanied by affidavits or evidence in any form. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (motion to reopen must be supported by affidavits or other material evidence stating the new facts that would be proven at a reopened hearing). Without an evidentiary submission, the motion does not demonstrate either that counsel’s representation was ineffective or that the respondent is eligible for any relief. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 561-62 (BIA 2003) (holding that alien asserting ineffective assistance of counsel must document that he is eligible for the relief sought). Further, while the respondent claims to be eligible for relief, the motion does not include an application for relief as required by 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1).
 
*2 For the above reasons, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen and motion for a stay are denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion for change of venue is denied as moot.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 6968968 (BIA)

2012 WL 6641809 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANTHONY AFUNWA
File: AXXX XX9 725 - Bloomington, MN
December 4, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Herbert A. Igbanugo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Thomas S. Madison
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 This case was last before us on July 16, 2012, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his petition to remove the conditional nature of his residence in the United States under section 216(c)(3)(D) of the Act. The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reopen proceedings on October 15, 2012. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
A motion to reopen shall not be granted unless it appears that the evidence sought to be offered “was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Further, this Board has held that a party who seeks to reopen proceedings to pursue a discretionary grant of relief from removal bears a “heavy burden” of demonstrating that if his motion to reopen were granted, the new evidence presented would likely change the result in the case. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992).
 
With his motion, the respondent has submitted over 30 affidavits from friends and acquaintances as support for his claim that he and his wife entered into a marriage in good faith. We find these statements and affidavits insufficient to bear the respondent’s heavy burden to show that such evidence would likely change the result in his case. Id. The various statements are from witnesses not subject to cross-examination; moreover, as the DHS argues in its opposition, such evidence was previously available during the respondent’s hearing. While the respondent states that his attorney should have told him to obtain such statements, he has not prepared an ineffective assistance of counsel claim in accordance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), nor has he otherwise demonstrated how his attorney’s advice, or lack thereof, excepts him from this regulatory requirement. See Fongwo v. Gonzales, 430 F.3d 944 (8th Cir. 2005)(attorney’s alleged advice to forego presentation of documents does not mean they were unavailable).1
 
Lastly, we do not find that the respondent’s evidence presents an exceptional circumstance which would warrant reopening in our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997)(stating that “[t]he power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”). As we noted in our previous decision, even without the respondent’s adverse credibility finding, the DHS proved by a preponderance of the evidence that the respondent did not enter the marriage in good faith (Bd. Dec. at 2; I.J. at 6). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s attempt to distinguish himself from Fongwo due to the fact that his affidavits were not yet prepared is an immaterial distinction.


2012 WL 6641809 (BIA)

2012 WL 6641745 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JAVIER DUARTE-JUAREZ
File: AXXX XX9 317 - Los Angeles, CA
November 27, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eudene Eunique Valle, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 The Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal on October 11, 2012, and the respondent filed a timely motion to reopen on October 22, 2012. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent, who is a native and citizen of Mexico, was found subject to removal as an aggravated felon, based on a 2007 California conviction for receiving stolen property, and sought no relief from removal.
 
The respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from his criminal defense attorney, as well as the attorney who earlier represented him before the Immigration Judge and Board. That is, according to the respondent, neither attorney has adequately assisted him in seeking to have his conviction vacated.
 
As we found in our October 11, 2012, decision, however, and despite the claims against counsel, the possibility that the respondent may obtain post-conviction relief does not change the finality of a criminal conviction for immigration purposes, unless and until it is overturned by a criminal court. See Matter of Ponce de Leon, 21 I&N Dec. 154, 156-67 (A.G. 1997; BIA 1997, 1996).
 
There is therefore no reason to reopen these proceedings. See Jimenez-Guzman v. Holder, 642 F.3d 1294, 1297 (10th Cir. 2011)(collateral attack on conviction does not disturb finality of conviction for immigration purposes, so continuance motion properly denied while respondent attempted to vacate criminal conviction); Cabral v. Holder, 632 F.3d 886, 890 (5th Cir. 2011) (collateral attack on conviction does not disturb finality of conviction for immigration purposes, so Board did not err in declining to hold appeal in abeyance while respondent attempted to vacate criminal conviction).
 
The motion to reopen is, therefore, denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 6641745 (BIA)
End of Document

2012 WL 6641752 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE ALEJANDRO BONILLA A.K.A. ALEJANDRO BONILLA A.K.A. ALEJANDRO J. BONILLA
File: AXXX XX3 886 - New York, NY
November 26, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
Appeal
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled (sustained)
 
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)] - Crime involving moral turpitude (sustained)
 
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of the El Salvador, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s July 31, 2012, decision finding him removable as charged and ineligible for voluntary departure. We also construe the respondent’s appellate filing as a motion to remand. The respondent’s motion to remand will be denied and the appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact made by the Immigration Judge, including the determination of credibility, for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof and issues of discretion, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent does not specifically allege error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that he is removable from the United States as charged by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) or that he is ineligible for voluntary departure, the only form of relief for which he applied before the Immigration Judge. Instead, the respondent alleges his attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel and that the proceedings before the Immigration Judge violated his right to due process of law. Additionally, the respondent requests remand to afford him the opportunity to apply for several grounds of relief from removal. Finally, the respondent indicates that he is seeking to vacate the conviction underpinning the charge of inadmissibility under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), which was sustained by the Immigration Judge.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent is removable from the United States under sections 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) and 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(6)(A)(i) (I.J. at 2). The record reflects the respondent, through counsel, admitted all of the factual allegations contained in the Notice to Appear and conceded removability as charged by the DHS (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 17). Absent egregious circumstances, an alien is bound by formal admissions made by his attorney acting in a professional capacity in the course of removal proceedings. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(c) (stating that the Immigration Judge may determine that removability as charged may be established by the admissions of the respondent). The respondent’s vague claim on appeal that his attorney did not adequately represent him does not establish egregious circumstances such that the respondent is not bound by those concessions. In particular, we note the respondent has presented no meaningful argument that he is not present in the United States without being admitted or paroled, or that his 2012 conviction for the offense of sexual abuse in the first degree, in violation of section 130.65(3) of the New York Penal Code, is not a crime involving moral turpitude. See Ashcroft v. Free Speech Coalition, 535 U.S. 234, 244 (2002); Matter of Olquin, 23 I&N Dec. 896 (BIA 2006). Thus, even assuming the respondent and his counsel may not have communicated optimally among themselves, the respondent has not demonstrated any prejudice in his attorney’s concession, because the respondent is, as a matter of both fact and law, removable from the United States as charged.
 
*2 We are also unpersuaded by the respondent’s vague claim that his constitutional rights were violated. As we indicated above, the Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent is removable as charged. On appeal, the respondent alleges he was deprived the opportunity to apply for relief from removal, violating this right to due process. Due process requires “the right to notice of the nature of the charges and a meaningful opportunity to be heard.” Brown v. Ashcroft, 360 F.3d 346, 350 (2d Cir. 2004) (quoting Choeum v. INS, 129 F.3d 29, 38 (1st Cir. 1997)). However, “[t]here is no requirement, either statutory or constitutional, that all possible defenses or collateral remedies be explained” to the alien against whom the government is proceeding. Brown v. Ashcroft, supra (quoting Aalund v. Marshall, 461 F.2d. 710, 712 (5th Cir. 1972). Our review of the record discloses that the respondent’s right to due process was not violated. Instead, the record reflects that the respondent was apprised of the nature of the charges against him and was provided with a meaningful opportunity to be heard, including the invitation to apply for relief from removal (Tr. at 18).1
 
Additionally, while the respondent claims generally to be eligible for several grounds of relief, including asylum under section 208 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1158, withholding of removal under section 241(b)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231, protection under the Convention Against Torture, 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.16-.18, and waivers of inadmissibility under sections 209(c) and 212(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1159(c), 1182(i), he has not established prima facie eligibility for any of the forms of relief he references in his brief. Thus, we conclude that the respondent has not established the proceedings before the Immigration Judge violated his right to due process or that these proceedings should be remanded to afford him the opportunity to explore eligibility for the forms of relief he references in his appellate filings. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992) (stating that a motion to remand is treated similarly to a motion to reopen, and therefore, requires both a showing of prima facie eligibility for relief and the proffer of material evidence that was “unavailable and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.”).
 
We also acknowledge the respondent’s claim that he has contacted a criminal defense attorney and hopes to have his conviction for sexual abuse in the first degree under section 130.65(3) of the New York Penal Code vacated (Respondent’s Br. at 5). However, the fact that the respondent may be pursuing post-conviction relief in the form of a collateral attack on his conviction in state criminal court does not affect the finality of such conviction for federal immigration purposes. Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992). As no evidence has been submitted to demonstrate that post-conviction relief has been granted, the respondent’s conviction is a final conviction for immigration purposes.
 
*3 Finally, while we acknowledge that the respondent and his family may suffer hardships in the event that he is removed from the United States, neither the Board nor the Immigration Judges have discretionary authority to grant relief from deportation solely on equitable or humanitarian grounds or as a matter of public policy. See Matter of Silva-Rodriguez, 20 I&N Dec. 448 (BIA 1992); Matter of Medina, 19 I&N Dec. 734, 742 (BIA 1988). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Our review of the record finds no support for the respondent’s claim that he was denied a fair administrative proceeding because he was not provided with a competency hearing (Respondent’s Br. at 3). The record does not disclose any basis for the respondent’s claim that his “mental condition through the course of this proceedings [sic] was below par” or that the Immigration Judge, “should have detected such incapacitation and lack of presence of mind” (Respondent’s Br. at 3). Rather, our review of the record discloses that the respondent answered the Immigration Judge’s queries responsively and appropriately, and that no other evidence in the record suggests the respondent may not be mentally competent. Thus, we are unpersuaded that there are any lingering ambiguities related to the respondent’s mental competency that require remand. See Matter of M-A-M-, 25 I&N Dec. 474, 477 (BIA 2011) (stating that “[a]bsent indicia of mental incompetency, an Immigration Judge is under no obligation to analyze an alien’s competency)
