** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MIGUEL M. GARCIA-MARTINEZ A.K.A. MIGUEL GARCIA-MARTINEZ A.K.A. MIGUEL MARTINEZ GARCIA
File: AXXX XX6 950 - Batavia, NY
November 21, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Anne E. Doebler, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 11, 2012. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and found him ineligible for relief from removal based on his criminal conviction.1 On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
On appeal, the respondent requests remand on the grounds that the conviction, which serves as the basis for removal, and which renders him ineligible for relief from removal, is being challenged before the criminal court pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. __, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010).2 The respondent alleges that he was not advised of the immigration consequences of his conviction, and he has filed a motion to vacate it. However, the conviction has not been vacated.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009).3 We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately considered the relevant factors to determine whether good cause for a continuance was shown. We conclude that good cause was not shown.
 
While the respondent is free to pursue the post-conviction motion, such a motion does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). The post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

1

Removability and relief from removal are not at issue on appeal.

2

In Padilla v. Kentucky, the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel.

3

In determining whether good cause exists to continue removal proceedings, a variety of factors may be considered, including, but not limited to: the Department of Homeland Security’s response to the motion to continue and the reason for the continuance and any other relevant procedural factors. See Matter of Hashmi, supra.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OSCAR ARNULFO ALAS-AGUILAR
File: AXXX XX0 139 - Los Angeles, California
November 15, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Reynold E. Finnegan, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a 26-year-old male native and citizen of El Salvador, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s July 30, 2012, written order which denied his motion to rescind his in absentia removal order. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including the credibility determination, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues involving questions of law, judgement and discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On October 5, 2005, the respondent was placed in removal proceedings with the issuance of a Notice to Appear, which he signed. He failed to appear at a hearing on November 7, 2007, and was ordered removed in absentia. The respondent filed a motion to rescind his in absentia order, claiming that he had not received notice of his hearing, and an Immigration Judge granted the motion on February 16, 2011.
 
On June 8, 2011, the respondent personally appeared, along with prior counsel, and was informed that his next hearing was on March 23, 2012. Following a hearing conducted in absentia on March 23, 2012, when he failed to appear, an Immigration Judge found the respondent subject to removal as charged, and ordered him removed from the United States. See generally section 240 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a.
 
An order of removal issued after in absentia proceedings may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in Federal or State custody. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995); Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or death of an immediate relative, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
On July 17, 2012, the respondent, through present counsel, filed a motion to rescind his March 23, 2012, in absentia order, claiming that he had not received notice of the March 23, 2012, hearing from his prior attorney because he was in a “disagreement” with her and she allegedly did not notify him about the March 23, 2012, hearing.1 He also requested reopening because his prior attorney allegedly committed ineffective assistance of counsel without elaboration. Finally, he claimed that he had not received the Notice to Appear.
 
*2 On July 30, 2012, an Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen because the respondent appeared in person at the June 8, 2011, hearing and received personal and written notification of the March 23, 2012, hearing, as well as the consequences for failure to appear.
 
We will affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent’s prior counsel was properly notified about the March 23, 2012, hearing, which constitutes notice to him. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (notice to alien’s counsel constitutes notice to alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). He also personally appeared at the hearing on June 8, 2011, and was notified about the hearing scheduled for March 23, 2012. Contrary to his contentions on appeal, the respondent received personal service of the Notice to Appear, and he was fully informed about the consequences for failing to appear because the Notice to Appear, which he signed on October 5, 2005, indicated that he received oral notice in Spanish about the consequences.
 
Insofar as the respondent alleged in his motion that his prior attorney had committed ineffective assistance of counsel, he failed to specify what conduct constituted the basis of his ineffective assistance claim, he did not comply with the standards to demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel because he had not filed a formal bar complaint against his prior counsel, and he has not specifically raised ineffective assistance as an issue on appeal.2 Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
Finally, the respondent alleged in a July 16, 2012, affidavit attached to his motion that he “was engaged in a criminal matter at the same time” without elaboration and that he “possibly” may have been in custody on March 23, 2012, but “I am unable to recall the dates of my incarceration.” In addition, the brief submitted on appeal claimed for the first time that the respondent was requesting reopening on the basis of unspecified “exceptional circumstances,” an apparent reference to his possible incarceration on March 23, 2012. However, it is inappropriate to raise exceptional circumstances as a basis for reopening for the first time on appeal because the Immigration Judge has not had an opportunity to address it, and we will not consider it now. Moreover, these allegations are insufficient without more to establish that the respondent was unable to appear because he was in custody on March 23, 2012, and he has not provided any documentation on appeal to establish whether he was incarcerated on March 23, 2012. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

On March 1, 2012, the respondent’s prior attorney filed a motion to withdraw, which was denied on March 6, 2012. She indicated that the respondent had failed to respond to both telephone calls and letters, and had failed to arrive for scheduled appointments.

2

His contentions for the first time on appeal about how his prior attorney allegedly did not notify him about the hearing because she mailed him letters in 2012 which he did not receive due to an incorrect zip code are irrelevant because he already had been personally notified on June 8, 2011 about his March 23, 2012, hearing, and he failed to maintain contact with his attorney.

Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAMIRO RICO-DE LEON
File: AXXX XX1 329 - Chicago, IL
November 14, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Anna I. Garcia, Esquire
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico. He filed a timely motion to reopen on October 9, 2012, after we missed his appeal on September 26, 2012. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has not responded to the motion. The motion will be granted, and the case will be remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.
 
The respondent was found to be subject to removal under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act, in that he was present in the United States without being admitted or paroled. The Immigration Judge, as affirmed by the Board, declined to grant a further continuance for the respondent to pursue post-conviction relief concerning a Kentucky marijuana trafficking conviction.
 
The respondent presents evidence that, on September 7, 2012, his narcotics conviction was vacated in state court. The motion to vacate the conviction in state court was based on ineffective assistance of counsel and cited Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. ___, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010).
 
Where, as here, a court with jurisdiction vacates a conviction based on a defect in the underlying criminal proceedings, a respondent no longer has a “conviction” within the meaning of section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Act. Matter of Adamiak, 23 I&N Dec. 878 (BIA 2006)(conviction vacated pursuant to Ohio law for failure of the trial court to advise the alien defendant of the possible immigration consequences of a guilty plea is no longer a valid conviction for immigration purposes).
 
The record will, therefore, be remanded to the Immigration Judge so that the respondent may seek any relief from removal to which he may be entitled.1
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
David B. Holmes

2012 WL 6641696 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALEMAYEHU MAMO
File: AXXX XX0 452 - Arlington, VA
November 9, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nash Fayad, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The final administrative order of removal in these proceedings was entered by the Board on November 13, 2008. On May 30, 2012, the respondent, a native and citizen of Ethiopia, filed the pending motion. The respondent does not dispute the fact that his motion is untimely. Rather, he contends that the applicable time limits should be equitably tolled because of the deficient performance of former counsel(s). The respondent asserts, in part, that former counsel(s) never informed him that his appeal had been dismissed. However, the respondent, who does not dispute that he was aware that an appeal was pending before the Board, has not described or identified what efforts he undertook during the period between the time his appeal was filed and his alleged 2009 discovery that it had been dismissed. Likewise, he has not described or identified what efforts he undertook between his alleged 2009 discovery and the filing of the instant motion 3 years later, other than to hire current counsel, for which he also provides no dates or details.
 
Nor has the respondent sufficiently complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, including the requirement that he show prejudice as a result of the alleged ineffective assistance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra. See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process).
 
Under the circumstances, we do not find equitable tolling of the 90-day-deadline warranted. See also Harris v. Hutchinson, 209 F.3d 325, 330 (4th Cir. 2000) (recognizing that equitable tolling may be appropriate where the litigant was either prevented from asserting his or her claim by some kind of wrongful conduct on the part of the defendant, or extraordinary circumstances beyond the litigant’s control made it impossible to comply with the statutory time limit).
 
On this record, the respondent has not provided an adequate basis to excuse the untimeliness of his motion. Nor has he demonstrated an exceptional situation that would warrant reopening in the exercise of discretion under the Board’s sua sponte authority. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
*2 FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 6641696 (BIA)

IN RE: MARCOS ANTONIO ACOSTA

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey B. Rubin, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 31, 2012. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and found him ineligible for relief from removal based on his criminal conviction.1 On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).

See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009).3 Because the prospect that the respondent will succeed in having his conviction vacated is only speculative, we agree with the Immigration Judge that good cause was not shown.

See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). The post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).

2012 WL 6641705 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA LORENZO-RAMIREZ
File: AXXX XX0 481 - Newark, NJ
November 6, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joseph Zurita, Esquire
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals the Immigration Judge’s August 6, 2012, decision denying her motion to reopen. We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment arising in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo, whereas we review findings of fact in such appeals under a “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent’s motion alleged that her prior attorney’s ineffective assistance led to the Immigration Judge’s September 16, 2011, decision ordering the respondent removed in absentia.
 
In Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), we stated that a motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement between the applicant and her legal representative and what actions were to be taken on her behalf, (2) that the counsel whose reputation is being impugned be informed of the allegations against him or her and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) an indication of whether a complaint had been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not.
 
Under our de novo standard of review, we conclude that the respondent’s motion to reopen did not comply with Matter of Lozada. In fact, the motion conceded that no compliance had yet been attempted. It instead asserted that evidence of compliance would be submitted later. The respondent submitted no further evidence during the 2 week period between the motion’s filing and the issuance of the Immigration Judge’s decision.
 
Nor has the respondent demonstrated compliance on appeal that would warrant a remand. The respondent has submitted a statement describing the events surrounding the issuance of her in absentia order of removal, see Respondent’s Brief at Tab A, but this statement is deficient, for it is signed but unsworn. Unsworn allegations do not constitute evidence. See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of S-M-, 22 I&N Dec. 49, 51 (BIA 1998). Even if this statement had been sworn, the respondent would not have demonstrated compliance with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, for she has not submitted evidence as to whether prior counsel has been informed of the allegations and given an opportunity to respond and evidence whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities.
 
Although the respondent’s motion did not meet the standards for reopening under Matter of Lozada, supra, a motion can nonetheless be a basis for reopening to apply for a form of relief, such as asylum, if the motion contains evidence of circumstances that have arisen subsequent to the final hearing. See 8 C.F.R § 1003.2(c)(1). The respondent’s motion, however, did not present evidence of changed circumstances or country conditions arising after the respondent’s final hearing.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 6641705 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SUMATIE GUYADEEN
File: AXXX XX3 358 - Miami, FL
November 6, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
*1 A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision or the mailing of a written decision unless the last day falls on a weekend or legal holiday, in which case the appeal must be received no later than the next working day. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38(b), (c). The Immigration Judge’s written decision was mailed to the respondent’s counsel in September 2007. The respondent did not file her Notice of Appeal with the Board until more than 5 years later on October 1, 2012. Thus, the appeal is untimely. The Immigration Judge’s August 29, 2007, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen accordingly remains final, and the record will be returned to the Immigration Court without further action.1 See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15.
 
Because we are dismissing the appeal as untimely, either party wishing to file a motion should follow the following guidelines: If you wish to file a motion to reconsider challenging the finding that the appeal was untimely, you must file your motion with the Board. However, if you are challenging any other finding or seek to reopen your case, you must file your motion with the Immigration Court.2 See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 16 (BIA 1998). You should also keep in mind that there are strict time and number limits on motions to reconsider and motions to reopen. See sections 240(c)(6)(A) and (B) and 240(c)(7)(A) and (C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(6)(A) and (B) and (c)(7)(A) and (C); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b)(1); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
We separately note that the respondent’s allegations that she was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel are not meaningfully raised, where she has not complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), nor shown prejudice.
 
*2 In light of the foregoing, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action.
 
David B. Holmes

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE MANUEL GOMEZ A.K.A. JOSEPH PFEIFER A.K.A. JOSEPH MANUEL PFEIFER-GOMEZ A.K.A. JOSEPH MANUEL PFEIFER
File: AXXX XX3 904 - Eloy, AZ
October 31, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marina Alexandrovich, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Alec J. Niziolek
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal on December 20, 2011. He submitted a motion to reopen September 14, 2012, also asking for a stay of removal. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the respondent’s motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely as it was filed more than 90 days after the Board’s decision. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). However, the respondent contends that former counsel provided him with ineffective assistance (Motion at 3). Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2000); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). He also asks that the Board sua sponte reopen proceedings (Motion at 9). 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
The respondent has submitted a statement averring that he filed a state bar complaint against former counsel, Mr. Bracamonte, and mailed a copy of the same to him. However, as he has not submitted a copy of the complaint, it is unclear what errors he alleged Mr. Bracamonte committed. He has not clarified this omission in his motion. In his statement he appears to contend that Mr. Bracamonte erred by admitting the factual allegations, and conceding removability. However, even assuming that was the basis for his bar complaint, he has not identified any error regarding the factual allegations, nor stated a meritorious ground for contesting the charges of removability (Exhs. 1, 1a). Further, as this Board previously noted, the record contains various exhibits supporting the Immigration Judge’s findings regarding both the factual allegations and the removal charges (Exhs. 2, 9, 10, 13). In this regard, we observe that in the instant motion the respondent concedes, rather than contests, his alienage (Declaration). In sum, given this record, Mr. Bracamonte could have reasonably decided to admit the allegations and concede removability. See Knowles v. Mirzayance, 129 S.Ct. 1411, 1422 (2009) ( “Counsel also is not required to have a tactical reason-above and beyond a reasonable appraisal of a claim’s dismal prospects for success-for recommending that a weak claim be dropped altogether.”); Magallanes-Damian v. INS, 783 F.2d 931, 934 (9th Cir. 1986) (attorney’s decision to forego contesting deportability was a tactical decision that did not rise to the level of ineffective assistance).
 
*2 In addition, the respondent has not shown that Mr. Bracamonte’s factual admissions and failure to challenge the grounds of removability “may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.” Iturribarria v. INS, supra at 899-900. Nor has he shown that there were “plausible grounds” for an alternative outcome. Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 826 (9th Cir. 2003). As such, he has not shown that he was prejudiced by Mr. Bracamonte’s actions. Furthermore, we do not find that this case presents exceptional circumstances warranting the exercise of our discretionary authority to sua sponte reopen proceedings. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Therefore, his motion is not exempt from the noted time-bar based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Given this record, neither reopening nor a stay of removal is warranted. Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FELIX ALEXANDER RIANO A.K.A. MICKEY ALBERT ROSELLO A.K.A. FELIY RIANO
File: AXXX XX4 619 - South Bay, Florida
October 31, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Anthony Alvarez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Christina Martyak
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)] - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Colombia and a lawful permanent resident of the United States since November 8, 1989, has filed a timely appeal of an Immigration Judge’s August 14, 2012, decision, denying the respondent’s request for a continuance; and finding the respondent removable, as charged, and statutorily ineligible for relief on account of the respondent’s 2003 Florida Aggravated Battery conviction, as well as his 2010 Florida Organized Fraud conviction, which the Immigration Judge also found to qualify as an aggravated felony “fraud offense” as defined under section 101(a)(43)(M) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(M). The appeal will be dismissed. The respondent’s request to proceed on appeal in forma pauperis is granted under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(a)(3). See Matter of Chicas, 19 I&N Dec. 114 (BIA 1984).
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in an appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent does not contest the Immigration Judge’s findings either as to his removability or as to his eligibility for relief from removal. The only issue raised on appeal concerns the Immigration Judge’s denial of his request for a continuance while awaiting a decision by the Florida Supreme Court on the question of nonretroactivity of the United States Supreme Court’s decision in the case of Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010).1 See Fundora v. State, 86 So.3d 544 (Fla.App. 2 Dist., 2012) (certifying that case to the Florida Supreme Court for a decision on the issue of nonretroactivity of Padilla v. Kentucky). However, in this regard, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not shown good cause for a continuance. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6 (stating that a party seeking a continuance has the burden of establishing good cause); Matter of Luviano, 21 I&N Dec. 235, 237 (BIA 1996) (stating that if good cause is shown, the decision to grant or deny a continuance is within the discretion of the Immigration Judge and that a denial will not be overturned unless it appears that the respondent was denied a full and fair hearing).
 
*2 The question as to when the Florida Supreme Court will reach the issue as to the nonretroactivity of Padilla v. Kentucky, and whether it will benefit the respondent, is completely speculative at this point, and does not amount to good cause for an indefinite continuance. The respondent’s conjecture that his convictions might be invalid, including in light of his arguments as to the retroactive applicability of Padilla v. Kentucky, does not change the finality for immigration purposes of such convictions, unless and until they are overturned by a criminal court. See Matter of Ponce de Leon, 21 I&N Dec. 154 (A.G. 1997; BIA 1997, 1996). The respondent has presented no evidence with this appeal that the Florida convictions underlying these removal proceedings have been vacated, and therefore the respondent has not established that the Immigration Judge’s denial of his continuance request has compromised the fairness of his removal proceedings.
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

While Padilla authorizes an ineffective assistance of counsel claim where a defendant is misadvised as to the deportation consequences in pleading to a crime, Florida courts have held that Padilla cannot be applied retroactively. See Smith v. State, 85 So.3d 551, 552 (Fla.App. 4 Dist., 2012) (citing Hernandez v. State, 61 So.3d 1144, 1151 (Fla. 3d DCA 2011)); see also McCalla v. State, 77 So.3d 845 (Fla. 4th DCA 2012); Davis v. State, 69 So.3d 315 (Fla. 4th DCA 2011). We note, moreover, the Federal Circuit courts are split as to whether Padilla applies retroactively. See, e.g., Chaidez v. United States, 655 F.3d 684, 686 (7th Cir. 2011) (Padilla does not apply retroactively to cases on collateral review); United States v. Orocio, 645 F.3d 630, 641 (3d Cir. 2011) (Padilla does apply retroactively). However, both the Eleventh Circuit, the jurisdiction wherein this case arises, and the United States Supreme Court have yet to rule on this issue. See U.S. v. Louis, 463 Fed.Appx. 819, 820 (11th Cir. 2012).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BERNARD AKWASI ACHEAMPONG A.K.A. BENARD ACHEAMPONG A.K.A. BERNARD O. ACHEAMPONG
File: AXXX XX1 747 - Baltimore, MD
October 31, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Polina Tomashevsky, Esquire
*1 This case was last before us on May 14, 2009, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s June 10, 2008, decision finding him removable as an aggravated felon. The respondent has now filed, on June 8, 2012, an untimely motion to reopen proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be granted pursuant to our sua sponte authority.
 
The procedural history of this case is as follows: The Immigration Judge initially granted the respondent’s motion to terminate proceedings on February 11, 2005, finding that the respondent was not removable under either section 237(a)(1)(C)(I) nor section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act. Subsequently, in a decision dated September 11, 2006, the Board reversed the Immigration Judge’s decision, and found that the respondent was removable pursuant to section 237(a)(1)(C)(i) of the Act for having violated his nonimmigrant status. We further found that the respondent’s conviction for resisting arrest constituted a crime of violence under section 101(a)(43)(F) of the Act, and therefore created his removability as an aggravated felon under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act. As such, we remanded the record for further proceedings regarding the respondent’s requests for relief from removal, or, in the alternative, for an entry of a final order of removal. The respondent appealed this decision to the United States Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit,1 but also appeared before the Immigration Court again, and stated on remand, through his attorney, that “we’re reserving all relief. We don’t have any relief at this moment (Tr. at 64). Accordingly, the Immigration Judge issued a summary order of removal on June 10, 2008, indicating that the respondent had not applied for relief. The respondent appealed this decision on September 26, 2008, arguing that the Immigration Judge had erred in finding the respondent removable as an aggravated felon. On May 14, 2009, we found that because the respondent had appealed to the Fourth Circuit rather than filing a timely motion to reconsider our June 10, 2008, decision, we would decline to revisit our previous decision, and we dismissed the respondent’s appeal (2009 Bd. Dec. at 1). The respondent has now filed a motion, on June 8, 2012, arguing that he received ineffective assistance of counsel during his prior proceedings because his prior counsel did not pursue an adjustment of status application in his behalf, even though he had originally requested such relief prior to the Board’s 2005 remand of his proceedings (Tr. at 2-3, 40).
 
*2 In support of these arguments, the respondent has filed evidence in substantial compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondent has also filed evidence in support of his claim that he has maintained an approved visa petition through his brother, with a current priority date, since 2003 (Tr. At 3-4), but that at the remanded hearing in 2007, his attorney stated that no relief was available to the respondent (Tr. at 51, 64). The respondent has also submitted an adjustment of status application, as well as various documents in support of his application for relief.
 
We find that a remand is warranted in our sua sponte authority. It does appear that due to the complicated procedural history of the respondent’s case, his opportunity to demonstrate eligibility for adjustment of status was overlooked at his various hearings. Inasmuch as the respondent does not appear prima facie ineligible for such relief, and given the particular circumstances in the respondent’s case, including the DHS’ lack of opposition, we will reopen the respondent’s proceedings under our sua sponte authority and will provide him a further opportunity to seek relief from removal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Upon remand, both parties may further address the respondent’s statutory and discretionary eligibility for adjustment and any commensurate waivers of inadmissibility. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is granted and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Fourth Circuit subsequently dismissed the petition based on lack of jurisdiction on motion by the government.
